## Herbert Amann

Vector-Valued Distributions

and Fourier Multipliers

This manuscript was originally planned to be Chapter VI, that is, the first chapter of Volume II of the treatise whose first volume is [1]. In the meantime I have changed the concept of that volume and do not plan to include this material. For this reason, and since some of the results presented here have already been cited by several authors being in possession of preliminary versions, I have decided to make it publicly available by putting it on my home page.

Zurich, July 2003

**Abstract**

We study the \(\beta\)-function of the \(\beta

---

## Preface

In the study of evolution equations by functional-analytical techniques one is led quite naturally to the study of pseudodifferential operators with operator-valued symbols. If one wants to avoid being restricted to a Hilbert space setting -- that would be much too narrow for nonlinear problems -- one has to be able to handle efficiently distributions with values in general Banach spaces. In particular, one has to be in possession of a good theory of convolutions for the case that both factors are Banach-space-valued distributions. For this reason, specializing the much more general results of L. Schwartz to a Banach space setting, we develop in the first section the theory of vector-valued distributions. Since there seems to be no other complete exposition of this theory easily available we try to be rather complete, thus going beyond the minimum we really need.

Vector-valued distributions are most useful in connection with techniques of harmonic analysis in a Banach space setting. In particular, we prove some Fourier multiplier theorems involving operator-valued symbols. These results, combined with dyadic decompositions in the Fourier image, are of fundamental importance. They are developed in Section 2. It is an important fact that, unlike the Banach space version of the Mikhlin multiplier theorem, our multiplier theorems do not require any restriction on the class of Banach spaces, like the UMD property, for example.

In the last section we first give a simple application of vector-valued distribution theory to linear evolution equations. Namely, we introduce the concept of distributional solutions and give sufficient conditions for their existence and uniqueness. The rest of that section is then essentially devoted to a detailed study of the Gauss-Weierstrass semigroup and related subjects. Its results are fundamental for an in-depth study of function spaces.

---

Vector-Valued Distributions

The theory of vector-valued distributions has been created and almost completely developed by L. Schwartz about forty years ago. His results are very general since he considered distributions with values in LCSs. For our purposes it suffices to consider distributions with values in Banach spaces only. This allows for some simplifications of the general theory, and we shall develop the relevant parts of that theory in this more restricted setting in the present section.

It will be crucial for our purposes to be in possession of a good theory of convolutions and 'point-wise multiplications' of vector-valued distributions. These are special cases of bilinear operations on vector-valued distributions. In order to handle such bilinear operations we prove in Subsection 1.5 a basic extension theorem that allows us to carry the desired operations from scalar distributions over to the vector-valued setting. To prove the extension theorem we need a considerable amount of preparation, namely rather deep results from the theory of LCSs as well as approximation and subtle continuity theorems for concrete spaces of distributions and related operations. These preparations occupy Subsections 1.1 to 1.4.

Having established the basic extension theorem in Subsection 1.5, it is not too difficult to define point-wise multiplications, tensor products, and convolutions for vector-valued distributions. This is done in Subsections 1.6-1.9. Finally, in the last subsection we introduce Laplace transforms for vector-valued distributions and prove the basic rules for dealing with them, as well as an important characterization theorem.

Although the proofs and techniques of this section are rather heavy, the final results are very simple to state and very satisfactory. Indeed, it can be said that, given the correct interpretation, all known rules for dealing with distributions carry over to the vector-valued setting.

In [1, Remark III.4.2.1] we have already commented on the possibility of defining a convolution for two vector-valued distributions and claimed that we will hardly have an occasion to use such a generality. In the meantime we became convinced, however, that the theory of distributions with values in Banach spaces is a very powerful tool with a high potential for nontrivial applications.

### Locally Convex Topologies

For the reader's convenience we collect in this subsection some more advanced topics from the theory of LCSs that we shall need below. Hereafter, we often use these facts without further mention.

---

#### The Uniform Boundedness Principle

An LCS is **barreled** if each absolutely convex, closed, and absorbing subset is a neighborhood of zero. Every Frechet space, hence every Banach space, is barreled ([16, III.6]). Every reflexive LCS is barreled. In fact, an LCS is reflexive iff it is barreled and bounded subsets are relatively weakly compact.

Let \(E\) and \(F\) be LCSs. A set \(\mathcal{A}\subset\mathcal{L}(E,F)\) is **equicontinuous** if for each neighborhood \(V\) of zero in \(F\) there exists a neighborhood \(U\) of zero in \(E\) such that \(A(U)\subset V\) for all \(A\in\mathcal{A}\). Equivalently, \(\mathcal{A}\) is equicontinuous iff to each continuous seminorm \(q\) on \(F\) there exists a continuous seminorm \(p\) on \(E\) such that

\[q(Ae)\leq p(e)\ ,\qquad e\in E\ ,\quad A\in\mathcal{A}\ .\]

The set \(\mathcal{A}\) is **uniformly bounded** if for each bounded subset \(B\) of \(E\) there exists a bounded set \(C\subset F\) such that \(A(B)\subset C\) for all \(A\in\mathcal{A}\). It is easily seen that each equicontinuous set is uniformly bounded. Finally, \(\mathcal{A}\) is **point-wise bounded** if \(\mathcal{A}(e):=\{\,Ae\ ;\ A\in\mathcal{A}\,\}\) is bounded in \(F\) for each \(e\in E\), that is, if \(\mathcal{A}\) is bounded in \(\mathcal{L}_{s}(E,F)\). Since \(\mathcal{L}(E,F)\hookrightarrow\mathcal{L}_{s}(E,F)\), every bounded subset of \(\mathcal{L}(E,F)\) is pointwise bounded. The uniform boundedness principle asserts that each pointwise bounded subset of \(\mathcal{L}(E,F)\) is equicontinuous, hence uniformly bounded, if \(E\) is barreled (e.g., [10, Proposition 11.1.1] or [15, Theorem III.4.2]). In particular, it follows that

\[\sup_{A\in\mathcal{A}}\|Ae\|<\infty\text{ for each }e\in E\quad\Longrightarrow \quad\sup_{A\in\mathcal{A}}\|A\|<\infty\ ,\] (1.1.1)

provided \(E\) and \(F\) are Banach spaces.

Suppose that \(E\) is barreled. Let \((A_{\alpha})\) be a net in \(\mathcal{L}(E,F)\) that is bounded in \(\mathcal{L}_{s}(E,F)\), and assume that \(Ae:=\lim A_{\alpha}e\) exists in \(F\) for each \(e\in E\). Then the Banach-Steinhaus theorem asserts that \(A\in\mathcal{L}(E,F)\) and that \(A_{\alpha}\to A\), uniformly on compact sets (e.g., [10, Theorem 11.1.3]). If \(E\) and \(F\) are Banach spaces and \((A_{j})\) is a sequence in \(\mathcal{L}(E,F)\) such that \((A_{j}e)\) converges in \(F\) for each \(e\in E\), it follows that

\[\|A\|\leq\liminf_{j\to\infty}\|A_{j}\|<\infty\] (1.1.2)

(e.g., [14, Corollary II.1.2]).

#### Hypocontinuity

Let \(G\) be a third LCS and let \(b:\,E\times F\to G\) be bilinear. Clearly, \(b\) is **separately continuous** if \(b(\cdot,f)\in\mathcal{L}(E,G)\) for each \(f\in F\) and \(b(e,\cdot)\in\mathcal{L}(F,G)\) for each \(e\in E\). Of course, every continuous bilinear map is separately continuous. A separately continuous bilinear map is continuous if \(E\) and \(F\) are Frechet spaces (e.g., [12, Theorem 2.1.7] or [16, Theorem 4.7.1]).

The map \(b\) is **hypocontinuous** if \(b(\cdot,f):\ E\to G\) is continuous, uniformly with respect to \(f\) in bounded subsets of \(F\), and \(b(e,\cdot):\ F\to G\) is continuous, uniformly

---

with respect to \(e\) in bounded subsets of \(E\). If \(b\) is hypocontinuous, it is separately continuous and \(b(A,B)\) is bounded in \(G\) if \(A\) is bounded in \(E\) and \(B\) is bounded in \(F\). Moreover, \(b\) is continuous on \(A\times F\) and on \(E\times B\), and uniformly continuous on \(A\times B\) (e.g., [10, Propositions 4.7.2 and 4.7.3] or [11, III.5.3]). Consequently, every hypocontinuous bilinear map is sequentially continuous.

Every separately continuous bilinear map is hypocontinuous if \(E\) and \(F\) are barreled (e.g., [10, Theorem 4.7.2] or [11, III.5.2]). Of course, a continuous bilinear map is hypocontinuous.

The following lemma will be useful for proving the hypocontinuity of certain bilinear maps.

**1.1.1 Lemma** _Suppose that \(F\) is barreled. Then the bilinear map_

\[{\cal L}(E,F)\times{\cal L}(F,G)\to{\cal L}(E,G)\ ,\quad(S,T)\mapsto TS\]

_is hypocontinuous._

**Proof** Let \(q\) be a continuous seminorm on \(G\) and let \(B\) be a bounded subset of \(E\). Let \({\cal T}\subset{\cal L}(F,G)\) be bounded. It follows from the uniform boundedness principle that there exists a continuous seminorm \(p\) on \(F\) such that

\[q(Tf)\leq p(f)\ ,\qquad f\in F\ ,\quad T\in{\cal T}\ .\]

Consequently,

\[\sup_{e\in B}q(TSe)\leq\sup_{e\in B}p(Se)\ ,\qquad S\in{\cal L}(E,F)\ ,\quad T \in{\cal T}\ ,\]

which shows that

\[(S\mapsto TS)\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal L}(E,F),{\cal L}(E,G)\bigr{)}\ ,\]

uniformly with respect to \(T\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal L}(F,G)\).

Let \({\cal S}\subset{\cal L}(E,F)\) be bounded. Then \({\cal S}\) is uniformly bounded. Hence there exists a bounded subset \(C\) of \(F\) such that \(S(B)\subset C\) for \(S\in{\cal S}\). Consequently,

\[\sup_{e\in B}q(TSe)\leq\sup_{f\in C}q(Tf)\ ,\qquad T\in{\cal L}(F,G)\ ,\quad S \in{\cal S}\ ,\]

which shows that

\[(T\mapsto TS)\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal L}(F,G),{\cal L}(E,G)\bigr{)}\ ,\]

uniformly with respect to \(S\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal L}(E,F)\).

**Montel Spaces** Recall that an LCS is a **Montel space** if it is barreled and bounded subsets are relatively compact. Since the unit-ball of a Banach space is relatively compact iff

---

it is finite-dimensional, there are no infinite-dimensional Banach-Montel spaces. Every Montel space is reflexive (e.g., [11, SS9]). The dual of a Montel space is a Montel space as well (e.g, [11, Proposition 9 in Section 3, SS9]).

#### Strict Inductive Limits

Let \(E\) be a vector space and let \(\{\,E_{\alpha}\,\,;\,\,\alpha\in\mathsf{A}\,\}\) be a family of subspaces such that:

1. Each \(E_{\alpha}\) is a Frechet space.
2. If \(E_{\alpha}\supset E_{\beta}\) then \(E_{\alpha}\) induces the original topology on \(E_{\beta}\).
3. There exists a cofinal increasing sequence \((E_{n})\) in \(\{\,E_{\alpha}\,\,;\,\alpha\in\mathsf{A}\,\}\), that is, \(E_{n}\subset E_{n+1}\) and to each \(E_{\alpha}\) there is an \(E_{n}\) with \(E_{n}\supset E_{\alpha}\).
4. \(\bigcup_{\alpha}E_{\alpha}=E\).

Then there exists a finest locally convex Hausdorff topology \(\tau\) on \(E\) such that \(E_{\alpha}\hookrightarrow E\) for each \(\alpha\in\mathsf{A}\), the (strict) **inductive limit topology** or **LF-topology** induced by \(\{\,E_{\alpha}\,\,;\,\,\alpha\in\mathsf{A}\,\}\). The LCS \((E,\tau)\) is denoted by

\[\lim_{\longrightarrow}E_{\alpha}=\lim_{\stackrel{{\longrightarrow }}{{\alpha}}}E_{\alpha}\]

and said to be an **LF-space**. Every LF-space is complete and barreled, and induces on each \(E_{\alpha}\) its original topology. A subset \(B\) of \(E\) is bounded iff \(B\subset E_{\alpha}\) for some \(\alpha\in\mathsf{A}\) and \(B\) is bounded in \(E_{\alpha}\). Let \(F\) be an LCS. Then a linear map \(T:\,E\to F\) is continuous iff \(T\,|\,E_{\alpha}\) is continuous from \(E_{\alpha}\) into \(F\) for each \(\alpha\in\mathsf{A}\). This is the case iff each \(T\,|\,E_{\alpha}\) is bounded. Thus an LF-space \(E\) is **bornological**, that is, every bounded linear map from \(E\) into an LCS is continuous. Clearly, every Frechet space, hence every Banach space, is an LF-space (e.g., [1, IV], [11, II.12], [12, II.6-II.8]).

It follows from (iii) that our inductive limits are countable. Thus, in principle, we could have restricted ourselves to the consideration of sequences \((E_{k})\) only, instead of admitting possibly uncountable families \(\{\,E_{\alpha}\,\,;\,\,\alpha\in\mathsf{A}\,\}\). However, the given formulation is well adapted to the concrete spaces we have in mind. In those cases, uncountable families of Frechet spaces occur naturally. Then we do not have to select a particular sequence and keep repeating that the topology is independent of that particular sequence.

#### Smooth Functions

Let \(X\) be a nonempty open subset of \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) and let \(E\) be a Banach space. Recall from [1, Subsection III.4.1] that

\[\mathcal{E}(X,E):=\Big{(}C^{\infty}(X,E),\,\,\{\,p_{m,K}\,\,;\,\,m\in\mathbb{N },\,\,K\subset\subset X\,\}\Big{)}\]

is a Frechet space, where the seminorms \(p_{m,K}\) are defined by

\[p_{m,K}(u):=\max_{|\alpha|\leq m}\|\partial^{\alpha}u\|_{\infty,K}\ .\] (1.1.3)

---

#### Test Functions

Given \(K\subset\subset X\), let

\[\mathcal{D}_{K}(X,E):=\big{\{}\,u\in\mathcal{E}(X,E)\ ;\ \operatorname{supp}(u) \subset\overline{K}\,\big{\}}\ .\] (1.1.4)

Then \(\mathcal{D}_{K}(X,E)\) is a closed linear subspace of \(\mathcal{E}(X,E)\), and

\[\bigcup_{K\subset\subset X}\mathcal{D}_{K}(X,E)=\big{\{}\,u\in\mathcal{E}(X,E) \ ;\ \operatorname{supp}(u)\subset\subset X\,\big{\}}\ .\]

Let

\[X_{k}:=\big{\{}\,x\in X\ ;\ \operatorname{dist}(x,X^{c})>1/k\,\big{\}}\cap k \mathbb{B}^{n}\ ,\qquad k\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\] (1.1.5)

where \(\operatorname{dist}(x,\emptyset):=\infty\). Then

\[X_{k}\subset\subset X_{k+1}\ ,\qquad\bigcup_{k}X_{k}=X\ .\] (1.1.6)

Hence \(\big{(}\mathcal{D}_{X_{k}}(X,E)\big{)}\) is a cofinal increasing sequence in \(\big{\{}\,\mathcal{D}_{K}(X,E)\ ;\ K\subset\subset X\,\big{\}}\). Thus

\[\mathcal{D}(X,E):=\lim_{K\subset\subset X}\,\mathcal{D}_{K}(X,E)\ ,\]

the **space of \(E\)-valued test functions**, is an LF-space (cf. Subsection III.1.1 of [1]).

#### Rapidly Decreasing Smooth Functions

Also recall from [1, Subsection III.4.1] that the Schwartz space of smooth rapidly decreasing \(E\)-valued functions on \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) is defined by

\[\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E):=\left(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E),\{\,q_{k,m} \ ;\ k,m\in\mathbb{N}\,\}\right)\ ,\]

where

\[q_{k,m}(u):=\sup_{x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\atop|\alpha|\leq m}(1+|x|^{2})^{k}\,| \partial^{\alpha}u(x)|\ .\] (1.1.7)

#### Slowly Increasing Smooth Functions

Finally, we recall that \(\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) is the space of slowly increasing smooth functions on \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\). This means that \(u\in\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) iff \(u\in\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and, given \(\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\), there exist \(m_{\alpha}\in\mathbb{N}\) and \(c_{\alpha}>0\) such that

\[|\partial^{\alpha}u(x)|\leq c_{\alpha}(1+|x|^{2})^{m_{\alpha}}\ ,\qquad x\in \mathbb{R}^{n}\ .\] (1.1.8)

Moreover, \(\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) is given the topology induced by the family of seminorms1

Footnote 1: For a proof of the fact that these seminorms are well-defined we refer to Proposition 1.6.1.

\[u\mapsto\|\varphi\partial^{\alpha}u\|_{\infty}\ ,\qquad\varphi\in\mathcal{S}( \mathbb{R}^{n})\ ,\quad\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\ ,\] (1.1.9)

so that it is an LCS.

---

#### Spaces of Vector-Valued Distributions

To simplify the writing we agree to put

\[\mathfrak{F}(X,E):=\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\qquad\text{if}\quad\mathfrak{F} \in\{\mathcal{S},\mathcal{O}_{M}\}\ .\] (1.1.10)

Then, as usual,

\[\mathfrak{F}(X):=\mathfrak{F}(X,\mathbb{K})\ ,\qquad\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{D} ,\mathcal{E},\mathcal{S},\mathcal{O}_{M}\}\ ,\]

if no confusion seems likely, and

\[\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X,E):=\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathfrak{F}(X),E\big{)}\] (1.1.11)

so that

\[\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)=\mathfrak{F}(X)^{\prime}\]

for \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{D},\mathcal{E},\mathcal{S},\mathcal{O}_{M}\}\). (Recall that \(\mathcal{L}\) is always given the bounded convergence topology.)

If \(u\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(X,E)\) then, as a rule, we write \(u(\varphi)\) for the value of \(u\) at \(\varphi\in\mathcal{D}(X)\). However, if \(u\) is a scalar distribution, that is, \(u\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(X)\), then we continue to denote \(u(\varphi)\) by \(\langle u,\varphi\rangle\).

**1.1.2 Theorem**: _Let \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{D},\mathcal{E},\mathcal{S},\mathcal{O}_{M}\}\). Then \(\mathfrak{F}(X)\) and \(\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\) are complete Montel spaces, hence reflexive._

Proof Every LF-space is complete and bornological. The dual of a bornological LCS is complete (e.g., [12, IV.6.1]). Hence \(\mathfrak{F}(X)\) and \(\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\) are complete for \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{D},\mathcal{E},\mathcal{S}\}\). The fact that \(\mathfrak{F}(X)\) is a Montel space for \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{D},\mathcal{E},\mathcal{S}\}\) is well-known (e.g., [13, Examples 3, 4, and 6 in Section 3, SS9]). Thus \(\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\) is a Montel space in these cases as well. The assertion for \(\mathfrak{F}=\mathcal{O}_{M}\) follows from [14, II.4.4].

### Convolutions

In [1, Subsection III.4.2] we have already stated the definition of the convolution of a vector-valued distribution and a scalar test function, as well as some of its basic properties. In this subsection we present proofs for some of the less obvious properties. In addition, we extend the definition to include convolutions of a vector-valued and a scalar distribution.

#### Convolutions of Distributions and Test Functions

Let \(E:=(E,|\cdot|)\) be a Banach space. Recall from [1, Subsection III.4.2] that, given

\[(u,\varphi)\in\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\times\mathfrak{F}( \mathbb{R}^{n})\ ,\qquad\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{D},\mathcal{E}\}\ ,\]

---

the convolution, \(u*\varphi\), of \(u\) and \(\varphi\), is defined by

\[u*\varphi(x):=u(\tau_{x}\check{\varphi})\ ,\qquad x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ .\] (1.2.1)

As already noted, the usual scalar proof (e.g., [13, Theorem 4.1.1]) carries over to the present situation to show that \(u*\varphi\in\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\) and

\[\partial^{\alpha}(u*\varphi)=\partial^{\alpha}u*\varphi=u*\partial^{\alpha} \varphi\ ,\qquad\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\ .\] (1.2.2)

Moreover,

\[\mathrm{supp}(u*\varphi)\subset\mathrm{supp}(u)+\mathrm{supp}(\varphi)\] (1.2.3)

so that

\[u*\varphi\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\qquad\mathrm{if}\quad(u,\varphi) \in\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\times\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\ .\] (1.2.4)

It is obvious that convolution is bilinear. For the reader's convenience we prove now that it is hypocontinuous.

**1.2.1 Proposition**: _Convolution is a bilinear and hypocontinuous mapping_:

* \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\times\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\to \,\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\);
* \(\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\times\,\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\to \,\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\);
* \(\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\times\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\to \mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\).

**Proof** To simplify the notation we put \(\mathcal{D}:=\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\) and \(\mathcal{E}:=\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\).

(i) Let \(K_{r}:=r\mathbb{B}^{n}\) for \(r>0\). Then \(u\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\) if and only if \(u\in\mathcal{L}(\mathcal{D}_{K_{r}}\,,E)\) for each \(r>0\).

Let \(A\) be a bounded subset of \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\). Given \(r>0\), the uniform boundedness principle implies the existence of \(k\in\mathbb{N}\) such that

\[|u(\varphi)|\leq cp_{k,K_{r}}(\varphi)\ ,\qquad\varphi\in\mathcal{D}_{K_{r}}\ , \quad u\in A\ .\]

Observe that

\[x\in K_{\rho}\ ,\ \ \varphi\in\mathcal{D}_{K_{r}}\quad\Longrightarrow\quad\tau _{x}\check{\varphi}\in\mathcal{D}_{K_{r+\rho}}\] (1.2.5)

and

\[p_{k,K_{r+\rho}}(\tau_{x}\check{\varphi})=p_{k,x-K_{r}}(\tau_{x}\check{\varphi })=p_{k,K_{r}}(\varphi)\] (1.2.6)

for \(\rho>0\). Consequently,

\[p_{K_{\rho}}(u*\varphi)=\sup_{x\in K_{\rho}}|u(\tau_{x}\check{\varphi})|\leq cp _{k,K_{r+\rho}}(\tau_{x}\check{\varphi})=cp_{k,K_{r}}(\varphi)\] (1.2.7)

for \(\varphi\in\mathcal{D}_{K_{r}}\) and \(u\in A\). Now we obtain from (1.2.2) that

\[p_{j,K_{\rho}}(u*\varphi)\leq cp_{k+j,K_{r}}(\varphi)\ ,\qquad\varphi\in \mathcal{D}_{K_{r}}\ ,\quad u\in A\ ,\quad j\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\quad\rho>0\ .\]

This shows that

\[(\varphi\mapsto u*\varphi)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{D}_{K_{r}}\,, \mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\big{)}\ ,\qquad u\in A\ ,\quad r>0\ .\] (1.2.8)

Hence \((\varphi\mapsto u*\varphi)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{D},\mathcal{E}( \mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\big{)}\), uniformly with respect to \(u\) in bounded subsets of \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\).

---

Now let \(B\) be a bounded subset of \(\mathcal{D}\). Then there exists \(r>0\) such that \(B\) is contained and bounded in \(\mathcal{D}_{K_{r}}\). It follows from (1.2.7) and (1.2.2) that

\[p_{j,K_{\rho}}(u*\varphi)=\max_{|\alpha|\leq j}\sup_{x\in K_{\rho}}|u*\partial^{ \alpha}\varphi(x)|\leq c\sup_{\psi\in C}|u(\psi)|\ ,\] (1.2.9)

where

\[C:=\big{\{}\,\tau_{x}(\partial^{\alpha}\varphi)^{\ \sim\ }\ ;\ x\in K_{\rho},\ | \alpha|\leq j,\ \varphi\in B\,\big{\}}\]

is a bounded subset of \(\mathcal{D}\), thanks to (1.2.5) and (1.2.6). Consequently,

\[(u\mapsto u*\varphi)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n}, E),\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\ ,\]

uniformly with respect to \(\varphi\) in bounded subsets of \(\mathcal{D}\). This proves the hypocontinuity of (i).

(ii) Let \(A\) be a bounded subset of \(\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)=\mathcal{L}(\mathcal{E},E)\). Then the uniform boundedness principle implies the existence of \(r>0\) and \(k\in\mathbb{N}\) such that

\[|u(\varphi)|\leq cp_{k,K_{r}}(\varphi)\ ,\qquad\varphi\in\mathcal{E}\ ,\quad u \in A\ .\] (1.2.10)

Thus, given \(\rho>0\) and \(x\in K_{\rho}\),

\[|u(\tau_{x}\varphi)|\leq cp_{k,K_{r}}(\tau_{x}\varphi)\leq cp_{k,K_{r}+\rho}( \varphi)\ ,\qquad\varphi\in\mathcal{E}\ ,\quad u\in A\ .\]

Hence, by (1.2.2), given \(\rho>0\),

\[p_{j,K_{\rho}}(u*\varphi)\leq p_{k+j,K_{r+\rho}}(\varphi)\ ,\qquad j\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\quad\varphi\in\mathcal{E}\ ,\quad u\in A\ ,\]

which shows that \((\varphi\mapsto u*\varphi)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{E},\mathcal{E}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\), uniformly with respect to \(u\) in bounded subsets of \(\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\).

If \(B\) is a bounded subset of \(\mathcal{E}\), estimate (1.2.9) is valid, where \(C\) is now a bounded subset of \(\mathcal{E}\). Hence \((u\mapsto u*\varphi)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n}, E),\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\), uniformly with respect to \(\varphi\) in bounded subsets of \(\mathcal{E}\). Thus (ii) is hypocontinuous as well.

(iii) Suppose that \(A\) is a bounded subset of \(\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\). Then we infer from (1.2.10) that \(u(\varphi)=0\) for \(\varphi\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\setminus K_{r})\) and \(u\in A\). Thus \(\operatorname{supp}(u)\subset K_{r}\) for \(u\in A\). Hence we deduce from (1.2.3) and (1.2.8) that

\[(\varphi\mapsto u*\varphi)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{D}_{K_{\rho}}, \mathcal{D}_{K_{r+\rho}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\ ,\qquad u\in A\ ,\quad\rho>0\ .\]

Since \(\mathcal{D}_{K_{r+\rho}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\hookrightarrow\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{ R}^{n},E)\), we see that

\[(\varphi\mapsto u*\varphi)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{D}_{K_{\rho}}, \mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\ ,\qquad\qquad u\in A\ ,\quad\rho>0\ .\]

Consequently, \((\varphi\mapsto u*\varphi)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{D},\mathcal{D}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\), uniformly for \(u\) in bounded subsets of \(\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\).

---

If \(B\) is a bounded subset of \({\cal D}\), then we deduce from (1.2.9) and (1.2.3) that

\[(u\mapsto u*\varphi)\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E),{ \cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\bigr{)}\ ,\]

uniformly with respect to \(\varphi\in B\). Hence (iii) is also hypocontinuous.

**Translation-Invariant Operators**

It is easy to verify that

\[u(\varphi)=u*\breve{\varphi}(0)=\breve{u}*\varphi(0)\] (1.2.11)

and

\[(u*\varphi)^{\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$ \,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\, \raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\, \raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\, \raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\, \,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\, \raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\, \raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\, \raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt \hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\,\,\,\,\,\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$

---

by Proposition 1.2.1. The same arguments show that

\[\bigl{(}\varphi\mapsto u*(v*\varphi)\bigr{)}\!\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal D}({\mathbb{ R}}^{n}\,),{\cal E}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\bigr{)}\ .\]

Thus (III.4.2.15) of [Ama95] and Theorem 1.2.2 guarantee the existence of a unique distribution \(u*v\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\), the **convolution** of \(u\) and \(v\), such that

\[(u*v)*\varphi=u*(v*\varphi)\ ,\qquad\varphi\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,)\ .\]

It is clear that convolution is bilinear. The next proposition shows that it is hypocontinuous as well.

**1.2.3 Proposition**: _Convolution is a bilinear and hypocontinuous mapping_:

(i) \({\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\times{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^ {n}\,)\to{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\);

(ii) \({\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\times\,{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}} ^{n}\,)\to{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\);

(iii) \({\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\times\,{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}} ^{n}\,)\to\,{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\).

**Proof** (i) Let \(A\) and \(B\) be bounded subsets of \({\cal D}^{\prime}:={\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,)\) and \({\cal D}:={\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,)\), respectively. Since reflection is obviously a toplinear automorphism of \({\cal D}\) and of \({\cal D}^{\prime}\), it follows from Proposition 1.2.1 and the boundedness properties of hypocontinuous maps that

\[C:=\bigl{\{}\,(u*\varphi)^{\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\mathchar 314$}}\ ;\ u\in A,\ \varphi\in B\,\bigr{\}}\]

is a bounded subset of \({\cal E}\). Hence, given \(v\in{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\),

\[\eqalign{\sup_{\varphi\in B}|v*u(\varphi)|=\sup_{\varphi\in B}|(v*u)*\varphi( 0)|&=\sup_{\varphi\in B}|v*(u*\varphi)(0)|\cr&=\sup_{\varphi\in B}\bigl{|}v \bigl{(}(u*\varphi)^{\raise 1.0pt\hbox{$\mathchar 314$}}\bigr{)} \bigr{|}=\sup_{\psi\in C}|v(\psi)|\ .\cr}\]

This shows that \((v\mapsto v*u)\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E),{ \cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\bigr{)}\), uniformly with respect to \(u\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal D}^{\prime}\).

Let \((u_{\alpha})\) be a net in \({\cal D}^{\prime}\) converging to zero. Then \(u_{\alpha}*\varphi\to 0\) in \({\cal E}\), uniformly with respect to \(\varphi\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal D}\), thanks to Proposition 1.2.1(i). Consequently, Proposition 1.2.1(ii) guarantees that \(v*(u_{\alpha}*\varphi)\to 0\) in \({\cal E}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\), uniformly with respect to \(\varphi\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal D}\) and to \(v\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\). Hence \((v*u_{\alpha})(\varphi)=(v*u_{\alpha})*\varphi(0)\to 0\) in \(E\), uniformly with respect to \(\varphi\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal D}\) and to \(v\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\). This shows that \((u\mapsto v*u)\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal D}^{\prime},{\cal D}^{\prime}({ \mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\bigr{)}\), uniformly with respect to \(v\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\). Hence (i) is hypocontinuous.

The hypocontinuity of (ii) and (iii) follows by modifying the above arguments in the obvious way.

**Elementary Properties of Convolutions**

**1.2.4 Remarks**: **(a) Let \(\delta\in{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,)\) be the Dirac distribution, that is,

\[\langle\delta,\varphi\rangle:=\varphi(0)\ ,\qquad\varphi\in{\cal E}({\mathbb{R}}^ {n}\,)\ .\]

---

Then \(u*\delta=u\) for \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\).

**Proof** From (1.2.1) it is obvious that \(\delta*\varphi=\varphi\) for \(\varphi\in{\cal E}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\). Hence

\[(u*\delta)*\varphi=u*(\delta*\varphi)=u*\varphi\ ,\qquad\varphi\in{\cal D}\ ,\]

by (1.2.13).

**(b)** Suppose that \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) and \(v\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) and that \(u\) or \(v\) has compact support. Then

\[\partial^{\alpha+\beta}(u*v)=\partial^{\alpha}u*\partial^{\beta}v\ ,\qquad \alpha,\beta\in{\mathbb{N}}^{n}\ .\]

**Proof** By applying repeatedly (1.2.2) we see that

\[\partial^{\alpha+\beta}(u*v)*\varphi =(u*v)*\partial^{\alpha+\beta}\varphi=u*(v*\partial^{\alpha+ \beta}\varphi)\] \[=u*\partial^{\alpha}(\partial^{\beta}v*\varphi)=\partial^{\alpha} u*(\partial^{\beta}v*\varphi)=(\partial^{\alpha}u*\partial^{\beta}v)*\varphi\]

for \(\varphi\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\).

**(c)** Let \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) and \(v\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) such that \(u\) or \(v\) has compact support. Then

\[\tau_{a}(u*v)=\tau_{a}u*v=u*\tau_{a}v\ ,\qquad a\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\ ,\]

and

\[(u*v)^{\,\cdot}=\check{u}*\check{v}\ .\]

**Proof** This follows easily from (1.2.13) and (1.2.12), or (III.4.2.15) of [1], respectively.

**(d)** If \(\{\,\varphi_{\varepsilon}\ ;\ \varepsilon>0\,\}\) is a mollifier then \(\varphi_{\varepsilon}\to\delta\) in \({\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) as \(\varepsilon\to 0\). Consequently, \(\varphi_{\varepsilon}*u\to u\) in \({\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) as \(\varepsilon\to 0\) for \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\).

**Proof** Given \(\psi\in{\cal E}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\),

\[\langle\varphi_{\varepsilon}-\delta,\psi\rangle=\int_{{\mathbb{R}}^{n}} \varphi_{\varepsilon}(x)\psi(x)\,dx-\psi(0)=\int_{{\mathbb{R}}^{n}}\varphi(y) \bigl{(}\psi(\varepsilon y)-\psi(0)\bigr{)}\,dy\ .\]

Hence, by the mean-value theorem,

\[|\langle\varphi_{\varepsilon}-\delta,\psi\rangle|\leq\sup_{|y|\leq 1}|\psi( \varepsilon y)-\psi(0)|\leq\varepsilon\sup_{|y|\leq 1}|\partial\psi(y)| \leq\varepsilon p_{1,{\mathbb{R}}^{n}}\,(\psi)\]

for \(0<\varepsilon\leq 1\). This shows that \(\langle\varphi_{\varepsilon}-\delta,\psi\rangle\to 0\) as \(\varepsilon\to 0\), uniformly with respect to \(\psi\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal E}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\), that is, \(\varphi_{\varepsilon}\to\delta\) in \({\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) as \(\varepsilon\to 0\). The second part of the assertion now follows from Proposition 1.2.3 and (a).

**Translation Groups on \({\cal S}\) and \({\cal S}^{\prime}\)**

Next we turn to the case of temperate distributions. For this we need some preparation.

---

**1.2.5 Lemma**: _The translation group acts strongly continuously on \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\)._

**Proof** Given \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(k,m\in\mathbb{N}\), we find

\[q_{k,m}(\tau_{x}\varphi)=\sup_{|x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\atop|x|\leq m}(1+|x+y|^{2})^ {k}|\partial^{\alpha}\varphi(y)|\leq 2^{k}(1+|x|^{2})^{k}q_{k,m}(\varphi)\] (1.2.14)

for \(x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\), thanks to the trivial inequality \(1+|x+y|^{2}\leq 2(1+|x|^{2})(1+|y|^{2})\). Hence \(\tau_{x}\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\). Thus, by the mean-value theorem,

\[q_{k,m}(\tau_{x}\varphi-\varphi)=q_{k,m}\Big{(}\int_{0}^{1}\sum_{j=1}^{n}x_{j} \tau_{tx}\partial_{j}\varphi\,dt\Big{)}\leq 2^{k}(1+|x|^{2})^{k}\sum_{j=1}^{n}|x_{j }|\,q_{k,m}(\partial_{j}\varphi)\ .\]

Consequently, \(\tau_{x}\varphi\to\varphi\) in \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) as \(x\to 0\). Since \(x\mapsto\tau_{x}\) is bounded on bounded sets, as follows from (1.2.14), we see that \((x\mapsto\tau_{x})\in C\big{(}\mathbb{R}^{n},\mathcal{L}_{s}(\mathcal{S}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E))\big{)}\).

We deduce from the Banach-Steinhaus theorem and the fact that \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) is a Montel space that

\[(x\mapsto\tau_{x})\in C\big{(}\mathbb{R}^{n},\mathcal{L}(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R }^{n}))\big{)}\ .\] (1.2.15)

Now \((\tau_{x}u)(\varphi)=u(\tau_{-x}\varphi)\) for \(u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\), \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), and \(x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\), and (1.2.15) imply that the translation group is also strongly continuous on \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\).

**Convolutions of Temperate Distributions**

It is clear that \((\varphi\mapsto\varphi)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E) \big{)}\). Thus, given \(u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), we can again define the **convolution**, \(u\ast\varphi\), by (1.2.1), since the right-hand side defines a continuous \(E\)-valued function of \(x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\) by Lemma 1.2.5.

In order to prove continuity properties of this convolution map we need the following technical result.

**1.2.6 Lemma**: \(\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) _is a complete LCS._

**Proof** Let \((a_{\beta})\) be a Cauchy net in \(\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\). From \(\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\hookrightarrow\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) it follows that \((a_{\beta})\) is a Cauchy net in \(\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\). Since the latter space is complete, there exists \(a\in\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) such that \(a_{\beta}\to a\) in \(\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\). Given \(\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\), \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), and \(\varepsilon>0\), there exists \(\beta_{0}\) such that

\[\|\varphi\partial^{\alpha}a_{\beta}-\varphi\partial^{\alpha}a_{\gamma}\|_{ \infty}<\varepsilon\ ,\qquad\beta,\gamma\geq\beta_{0}\ .\]

Thus, since \(a_{\gamma}\to a\) in \(\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\),

\[\|\varphi\partial^{\alpha}a_{\beta}-\varphi\partial^{\alpha}a\|_{\infty}\leq \varepsilon\ ,\qquad\ \ \beta\geq\beta_{0}\ .\]

Similarly,

\[\|\varphi\partial^{\alpha}a_{\gamma}\|_{\infty}\leq\|\varphi\partial^{\alpha}a _{\beta_{0}}\|_{\infty}+\varepsilon\ ,\qquad\gamma\geq\beta_{0}\ ,\]

implies \(\|\varphi\partial^{\alpha}a\|_{\infty}<\infty\). Hence \(a\in\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(a_{\beta}\to a\) in \(\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\).

---

**1.2.7 Proposition**: _Convolution is a bilinear and_

* _continuous map_:_ \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\times\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\to\mathcal{S}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E)\);
* _hypocontinuous map_:_ \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\times\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\to \mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\).

**Proof** (i) Given \(u\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\), it follows from \(\partial^{\alpha}u\in BUC(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) for \(\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\) and from [1, (III.4.2.10), (III.4.2.19)] that \(u\ast v\in\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) for \(v\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\subset L_{1}\). Note that

\[|x|^{2\ell}\leq(|x-y|+|y|)^{2\ell}=\sum_{j=0}^{2\ell}\tbinom{2\ell}{j}\,|x-y|^ {j}\,|y|^{2\ell-j}\]

implies

\[|x|^{2\ell}\,|u\ast v(x)| \leq\sum_{j=0}^{2\ell}\tbinom{2\ell}{j}\,\int|x-y|^{j}\,|u(x-y)| \,|y|^{2\ell-j}\,(1+|y|^{2})^{n}\,|v(y)|\,\frac{dy}{(1+|y|^{2})^{n}}\] \[\leq c_{\ell}q_{\ell,0}(u)q_{\ell+n,0}(v)\]

for \(u,v\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\). This proves (i).

(ii) If \(u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(\varphi\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) then \(u\ast\varphi\in\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) by Proposition 1.2.1. Let \(U\) be a bounded subset of \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\). Then there exist \(k,m\in\mathbb{N}\) with

\[|u(\varphi)|\leq cq_{k,m}(\varphi)\ ,\qquad u\in U\ ,\quad\varphi\in\mathcal{S} (\mathbb{R}^{n})\ .\]

Thus, for \(\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\), we deduce from (1.2.14) that

\[|\partial^{\alpha}(u\ast\varphi)(x)| =|(u\ast\partial^{\alpha}\varphi)(x)|=\big{|}u\big{(}\tau_{x}( \partial^{\alpha}\varphi)^{\,\cdot}\big{)}\,\big{|}\] \[\leq cq_{k,m}\big{(}\tau_{x}(\partial^{\alpha}\varphi)^{\,\cdot} \big{)}\leq c(1+|x|^{2})^{k}q_{k,m+|\alpha|}(\varphi)\]

for \(\varphi\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) and \(u\in U\). Consequently, given a bounded subset \(B\) of \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\),

\[\sup_{\varphi\in B}\|\psi\partial^{\alpha}(u\ast\varphi)\|_{\infty}\leq c_{k, m}\sup_{\varphi\in B}q_{k,0}(\psi)q_{k,m+|\alpha|}(\varphi)\]

for \(u\in U\) and \(\varphi\in B\cap\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\). This shows that, for \(u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\), the linear map

\[\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\to\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\quad \varphi\mapsto u\ast\varphi\ ,\]

is continuous with respect to the topology induced on \(\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) by \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\), uniformly with respect to \(u\) in bounded subsets of \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\). Since \(\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) is dense in \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) and \(\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) is complete, it follows that

\[(\varphi\mapsto u\ast\varphi)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n} ),\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\ ,\]

uniformly with respect to \(u\) in bounded subsets of \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\).

---

Now let \(B\) and \(C\) be bounded subsets of \({\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) and observe that, given \(\alpha\in{\mathbb{N}}^{n}\), the image of the map

\[{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times B\times C\to{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\ ,\quad(x,\psi, \varphi)\mapsto\psi(x)\tau_{x}(\partial^{\alpha}\varphi)\

---

**(d)**: The evaluation map \({\cal L}(E_{1},E_{0})\times E_{1}\to E_{0},\ (A,x)\mapsto Ax\).
**(e)**: Compositions \({\cal L}(E_{1},E_{2})\times{\cal L}(E_{0},E_{1})\to{\cal L}(E_{0},E_{2}),\ (S,T) \mapsto ST\).
**(f)**: Convolution in each one of the cases (III.4.2.18)-(III.4.2.22) of [1].
**(g)**: If \(b\in{\cal L}(E_{1},E_{2};E_{0})\) and \(b\neq 0\) then

\[E_{1}\times E_{2}\to E_{0}\ ,\quad(x_{1},x_{2})\mapsto\frac{1}{\|b\|}b(x_{1},x_{2})\]

is a multiplication. This shows that it is no restriction assuming that the norm of a multiplication is bounded by \(1\).

**Leibniz' Rule**

We also recall that, given any nonempty set \(S\), point-wise multiplication

\[E_{1}^{S}\times E_{2}^{S}\to E_{0}^{S}\ ,\quad(a_{1},a_{2})\mapsto a_{1}\bullet a _{2}\]

induced by (1.3.1) is defined by

\[a_{1}\bullet a_{2}(s):=a_{1}(s)\bullet a_{2}(s)\ ,\qquad s\in S\ .\]

**1.3.2 Lemma**: _Let \(p\in{\mathbb{K}}[X_{1},\ldots,X_{n}]\) be a polynomial of degree at most \(k\) in \(n\) indeterminates and let \(X\subset{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\) be a nonempty open subset of \(\ {\mathbb{R}}^{n}\). Then, putting_

\[p^{(\beta)}:=\partial^{\beta}p\ ,\qquad\beta\in{\mathbb{N}}^{n}\ ,\]

_the_ **generalized Leibniz rule**

\[p(\partial)(a_{1}\bullet a_{2})=\sum_{\beta}\frac{1}{\beta!}(\partial^{\beta} a_{1})\bullet p^{(\beta)}(\partial)a_{2}\ ,\]

_holds for \(a_{j}\in C^{k}(X,E_{j})\), \(j=1,2\)._

**Proof** From the obvious 'product rule'

\[\partial_{j}(a_{1}\bullet a_{2})=\partial_{j}a_{1}\bullet a_{2}+a_{1}\bullet \partial_{j}a_{2}\ ,\qquad 1\leq j\leq n\ ,\] (1.3.2)

we deduce by induction that

\[p(\partial)(a_{1}\bullet a_{2})=\sum_{\beta}\partial^{\beta}a_{1}\bullet q_{ \beta}(\partial)a_{2}\ ,\qquad a_{j}\in C^{k}(X,E_{j})\ ,\quad j=1,2\ ,\] (1.3.3)

where \(q_{\beta}\in{\mathbb{K}}[X_{1},\ldots,X_{n}]\) and \(q_{\beta}=0\) for \(|\beta|>k\). Given \(y_{j}\in E_{j}\) and \(\xi,\eta\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\), we put \(a_{1}:=e^{(\xi,\cdot)}y_{1}\) and \(a_{2}:=e^{(\eta,\cdot)}y_{2}\). Since

\[e^{-\langle\zeta,\cdot\rangle}q(\partial)e^{\langle\zeta,\cdot\rangle}=q(\zeta )\ ,\qquad\zeta\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\ ,\quad q\in{\mathbb{K}}[X_{1},\ldots,X_{n}]\ ,\] (1.3.4)

---

it follows from (1.3.3) that

\[p(\xi+\eta)y_{1}\bullet y_{2}=\sum_{\beta}\xi^{\beta}q_{\beta}(\eta)y_{1}\bullet y_ {2}\ .\]

Since this is true for every choice of \(y_{1}\) and \(y_{2}\),

\[p(\xi+\eta)=\sum_{\beta}\xi^{\beta}q_{\beta}(\eta)\ ,\qquad\xi,\eta\in{\mathbb{R}}^ {n}\ .\]

By differentiating this identity with respect to \(\xi\) and putting \(\xi=0\) it follows that \(\partial^{\beta}p(\eta)=\beta!\,q_{\beta}(\eta)\). This proves the assertion.

Letting \(p(\xi):=\xi^{\alpha}\) in Lemma 1.3.2, we obtain the standard **Leibniz rule**:

\[\partial^{\alpha}(a_{1}\bullet a_{2})=\sum_{\beta\leq\alpha}{{}^{\alpha}_{ \beta}\choose{\beta}}\partial^{\beta}a_{1}\bullet\partial^{\alpha-\beta}a_{2} \ ,\qquad a_{j}\in C^{|\alpha|}(X,E_{j})\ ,\quad j=1,2\ .\] (1.3.5)

**Approximation by Test Functions**

After these preparations we can prove a useful approximation theorem for vector-valued distributions. For this we recall convention (1.1.10).

**1.3.3 Proposition**: _Suppose that \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{{\cal D},{\cal E},{\cal S}\}\) and \(u\in\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X,E)\). Then there exists a sequence \((u_{j})\) in \({\cal D}(X,E)\) such that \(u_{j}\to u\) in \(\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X,E)\)._

**Proof** First suppose that \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{{\cal D},{\cal E}\}\). Denote by \((X_{k})\) a sequence of nonempty relatively compact open subsets of \({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\) such that \(\overline{X}_{k}\subset X_{k+1}\) and \(\bigcup X_{k}=X\). For each \(k\in{\mathbb{N}}\) fix \(\chi_{k}\in{\cal D}(X)\) with \(\chi_{k}\,|\,X_{k}=1\). Then \(\chi_{k}u\in{\cal E}^{\prime}(X,E)\subset{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\). Denote by \(\{\,\psi_{\varepsilon}\ ;\ \varepsilon>0\,\}\) a mollifier such that \(\psi_{1}\) is even and put

\[u_{k}:=(\chi_{k}u)*\psi_{1/k}\ ,\qquad k\in\hat{{\mathbb{N}}}\ .\]

Then \(u_{k}\in{\cal D}(X,E)\) by Proposition 1.2.1. Given \(\varphi\in{\cal D}(X)\),

\[u_{k}(\varphi) = \bigl{(}(\chi_{k}u)*\psi_{1/k}\bigr{)}*\check{\varphi}(0)=(\chi_{ k}u)*(\psi_{1/k}*\check{\varphi})(0)\] \[= \chi_{k}u*(\check{\psi}_{1/k}*\varphi){{}^{\cdot}}(0)=u\bigl{(} \chi_{k}(\psi_{1/k}*\varphi)\bigr{)}\ ,\]

where we used (1.2.11), (1.2.12), and the evenness of \(\psi_{1/k}\). Since \(\mathop{\rm supp}\nolimits(\varphi)\subset X_{j}\) for some \(j\in{\mathbb{N}}\), it follows from [Ama95, (III.4.2.10) and (III.4.2.25)] that \(\psi_{1/k}*\varphi\to\varphi\) in \({\cal D}(X)\). Also \(\chi_{k}\to{\bf 1}\) in \({\cal E}(X)\). Consequently,

\[\chi_{k}(\psi_{1/k}*\varphi)\to\varphi\quad\hbox{in }{\cal D}(X)\qquad\hbox{if }\varphi\in{\cal D}(X)\ ,\] (1.3.6)

so that \(u_{k}(\varphi)\to u(\varphi)\) in \(E\). This means that

\[u_{k}\to u\quad\hbox{in }{\cal L}_{s}\bigl{(}{\cal D}(X),E\bigr{)}\ .\] (1.3.7)

---

Next we choose \(\varphi\in{\cal E}(X)\). Then the estimate

\[\eqalign{|\partial^{\alpha}(\psi_{\varepsilon}*\varphi)(x)-\partial^{\alpha} \varphi(x)|&=|\psi_{\varepsilon}*\partial^{\alpha}\varphi(x)-\partial^{\alpha} \varphi(x)|\cr&=\Big{|}\int\psi_{\varepsilon}(y)\big{[}\partial^{\alpha}\varphi (x-y)-\partial^{\alpha}\varphi(x)\big{]}\;dy\Big{|}\cr&=\Big{|}\int\psi(y)\big{[} \partial^{\alpha}\varphi(x-\varepsilon y)-\partial^{\alpha}\varphi(x)\big{]} \;dy\Big{|}\cr&\leq\sup_{|y|<1}|\partial^{\alpha}\varphi(x-\varepsilon y)- \partial^{\alpha}\varphi(x)|\cr}\]

for \(x\in X\) and \(0<\varepsilon<{\rm dist}(x,\partial X)\) shows that \(\psi_{1/k}*\varphi\to\varphi\) in \({\cal E}(X)\). Thus

\[\chi_{k}(\psi_{1/k}*\varphi)\to\varphi\quad{\rm in}\ {\cal E}(X)\qquad{\rm if} \ \varphi\in{\cal E}(X)\ ,\]

so that \(u_{k}(\varphi)\to u(\varphi)\) in \(E\) for \(u\in{\cal E}^{\prime}(X,E)\). Consequently,

\[u_{k}\to u\quad{\rm in}\ {\cal L}_{s}\big{(}{\cal E}(X),E\big{)}\qquad{\rm if} \ u\in{\cal E}^{\prime}(X,E)\ .\]

Now suppose that \(\varphi\in{\cal S}({\tenmsb R}^{n})\). Choose any sequence \((\chi_{k})\) in \({\cal D}({\tenmsb R}^{n})\) satisfying \(\chi_{k}\,|\,(k{\tenmsb R}^{n})=1\) and \({\rm supp}\,\chi_{k}\subset(k+1){\tenmsb R}^{n}\) such that

\[\sup_{k}\|\partial^{\alpha}\chi_{k}\|_{\infty}<\infty\ ,\qquad\alpha\in{ \tenmsb N}^{n}\ .\]

For example, put

\[\chi_{k}:=\psi_{\varepsilon}*\chi_{(k+1/2){\tenmsb R}^{n}}\ ,\qquad k\in{ \tenmsb N}\ ,\]

for a fixed \(\varepsilon\in(0,1/4)\). Then, using Leibniz' rule and (1.3.8), it is easily seen that

\[\chi_{k}(\psi_{1/k}*\varphi)\to\varphi\quad{\rm in}\ {\cal S}({\tenmsb R}^{n})\ .\]

Consequently, \(u_{k}(\varphi)\to u(\varphi)\) in \(E\) if \(u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\tenmsb R}^{n},E)\), which means that

\[u_{k}\to u\quad{\rm in}\ {\cal L}_{s}\big{(}{\cal S}({\tenmsb R}^{n}),E\big{)} \qquad{\rm if}\ u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\tenmsb R}^{n},E)\ .\]

Altogether, (1.3.7), (1.3.10), and (1.3.13) show that

\[u_{k}\to u\quad{\rm in}\ {\cal L}_{s}\big{(}\hbox{\goth F}(X),E\big{)} \qquad{\rm if}\ u\in\hbox{\goth F}^{\prime}(X,E)\]

for \(\hbox{\goth F}\in\{{\cal D},{\cal E},{\cal S}\}\). Now, by the Banach-Steinhaus theorem, \(u_{k}(\varphi)\to u(\varphi)\) in \(E\), uniformly for \(\varphi\) in compact subsets of \(\hbox{\goth F}(X)\). Since \(\hbox{\goth F}(X)\) is a Montel space it follows that \(u_{k}\to u\) in \({\cal L}\big{(}\hbox{\goth F}(X),E\big{)}=\hbox{\goth F}^{\prime}(X,E)\). This proves the assertion.

**1.3.4 Remark** The arguments leading to (1.3.9) and (1.3.12) remain valid if \(\hbox{\goth F}(X)\) is replaced by \(\hbox{\goth F}(X,E)\). This proves that

\[{\cal D}(X,E)\buildrel d\over{\hookrightarrow}{\cal E}(X,E)\quad{\rm and} \quad{\cal D}({\tenmsb R}^{n},E)\buildrel d\over{\hookrightarrow}{\cal S}({ \tenmsb R}^{n},E)\ ,\]

with sequential density, since the continuity of these injections is clear. This has already been stated in [Ama95, (III.4.1.2) and (III.4.1.5)], respectively.

---

Our next proposition shows that \({\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) is dense in \({\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) as well.

**1.3.5 Proposition**: \({\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\hookrightarrow\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}{ \cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\)_._

**Proof** Suppose that \(K\subset\subset{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\). Then, given \(\varphi\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) and \(\alpha\in{\mathbb{N}}^{n}\),

\[\|\varphi\partial^{\alpha}u\|_{\infty}\leq\|\varphi\|_{\infty}\,\|\partial^{ \alpha}u\|_{\infty}\leq\|\varphi\|_{\infty}\,p_{|\alpha|,K}(u)\ ,\qquad u\in{ \cal D}_{K}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ .\]

This shows that \({\cal D}_{K}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\hookrightarrow{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\). Hence \({\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\hookrightarrow{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\).

Now fix \(u\in{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\), as well as \(\varphi\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) and \(\alpha\in{\mathbb{N}}^{n}\). There exist \(m\in{\mathbb{N}}\) and \(c_{\alpha}>0\) such that

\[|\partial^{\beta}u(x)|\leq c_{\alpha}(1+|x|^{2})^{m}\ ,\qquad x\in{\mathbb{R}}^ {n}\ ,\quad\beta\leq\alpha\ .\] (1.3.14)

Let \((\chi_{k})\) be a sequence as in (1.3.11). Then, by Leibniz' rule,

\[\partial^{\alpha}(\chi_{k}u)-\partial^{\alpha}u=\sum_{\beta<\alpha}{\alpha \choose\beta}\partial^{\alpha-\beta}\chi_{k}\partial^{\beta}u+(\chi_{k}-1) \partial^{\alpha}u\ .\]

Hence we infer from (1.3.11) and (1.3.14) that

\[\big{\|}\varphi\big{(}\partial^{\alpha}(\chi_{k}u)-\partial^{\alpha}u\big{)} \big{\|}_{\infty}\leq cq_{m+1,0}(\varphi)(1+k^{2})^{-1}\ ,\qquad k\in{ \mathbb{N}}\ .\]

Since \(\chi_{k}u\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\), it follows that \({\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) is dense in \({\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\).

The preceding approximation results can be used to prove a number of useful density results. For this we make use of the following elementary facts that will also often be used without further mention:

**Density by Iteration**

Let \(A\), \(B\), and \(C\) be topological spaces. It is obvious that

\[A\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}B\stackrel{{ d}}{{ \hookrightarrow}}C\quad\Longrightarrow\quad A\stackrel{{ d}}{{ \hookrightarrow}}C\] (1.3.15)

and

\[A\stackrel{{ d}}{{\subset}}C\ ,\quad A\subset B\subset C\quad \Longrightarrow\quad B\stackrel{{ d}}{{\subset}}C\ .\] (1.3.16)

Implication (1.3.16) is also true if \(d\) stands for 'sequentially dense'.

**Approximation by Tensor Products**

We recall from [1, Subsection V.2.4] that, given \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{{\cal D},{\cal E},{\cal S},{\cal O}_{M}\}\), the tensor product \(\mathfrak{F}(X)\otimes E\) is defined by putting first

\[\varphi\otimes e(x):=\varphi(x)e\ ,\qquad\varphi\in\mathfrak{F}(X)\ ,\quad e\in E \ ,\quad x\in X\ ,\] (1.3.17)

---

and then

\[\mathfrak{F}(X)\otimes E:=\operatorname{span}\bigl{\{}\,\varphi\otimes e\ ;\ \varphi\in\mathfrak{F}(X),\ e\in E\,\bigr{\}}\ ,\] (1.3.18)

where the linear hull is taken in \(E^{X}\). Analogously, we define the tensor product \(\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\otimes E\) by

\[(u\otimes e)(\varphi):=u(\varphi)e\ ,\qquad u\in\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\ ,\quad e\in E\ ,\quad\varphi\in\mathfrak{F}(X)\ ,\] (1.3.19)

and

\[\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\otimes E:=\operatorname{span}\bigl{\{}\,u\otimes e\ ;\ u\in\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X),\ e\in E\,\bigr{\}}\ ,\] (1.3.20)

now the span being taken in \(E^{\mathfrak{F}(X)}\). It is obvious that \(\mathfrak{F}(X)\otimes E\subset\mathfrak{F}(X,E)\) and \(\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\otimes E\subset\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X,E)\). In the following approximation theorem these tensor products are given the corresponding subspace topologies.

**1.3.6 Theorem**: \(\!\!

---

(ix) Given \(K=\overline{K}\subset\subset L=\overline{L}\subset\subset X\), choose \(\varphi\in{\cal D}(X)\) with \(\varphi\,|\,L=1\). Then \(\varphi u\in C^{k}(X,E)\) for \(u\in C^{k}(X,E)\), and \(\mathop{\rm supp}\nolimits(\varphi u)\subset\subset X\) with \(\varphi u\,|\,L=u\). By mollifying \(\varphi u\) we find \(v\in{\cal D}(X,E)\) satisfying \(v\,|\,K=u\). This gives \({\cal D}(X,E)\buildrel d\over{\hookrightarrow}C^{k}(X,E)\). Now the assertion is obvious, as is (x).

**Approximation by Polynomials**

Finally, we use this approximation theorem to prove the separability of the spaces under consideration, provided \(E\) is separable. For this we need a preparatory lemma that will be useful in Subsection 1.8 as well.

1.3.7 Lemma_Every element of \({\cal D}(X,E)\) is the limit in \({\cal E}(X,E)\) of a sequence of \((\)restrictions of\()\) polynomials \((\)in \(n\) indeterminates and with coefficients in \(E)\)._

**Proof** Define \(w\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) by

\[w(x):=(4\pi)^{-n/2}e^{-|x|^{2}/4}\ ,\qquad x\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\ .\]

Then \(w\) is analytic and everywhere positive. Since \(w(x)=\prod_{j=1}^{n}w(x_{j})\), it follows from Fubini's theorem that \(\|w\|_{1}=\|w\|_{L_{1}({\mathbb{R}})}^{n}\). But, again by Fubini's theorem,

\[\eqalign{\Bigl{(}\int_{{\mathbb{R}}}e^{-x^{2}/4}\,dx\Bigr{)}^{2}&=\int_{{ \mathbb{R}}^{2}}e^{-(x^{2}+y^{2})/4}\,d(x,y)\cr&=2\pi\int_{0}^{\infty}re^{-r^{ 2}/4}\,dr=-4\pi e^{-r^{2}/4}\bigl{|}_{0}^{\infty}=4\pi\ .\cr}\]

Hence \(\|w\|_{1}=1\), and, putting

\[w_{t}(x):=t^{-n/2}w\bigl{(}x/\sqrt{t}\bigr{)}=(4\pi t)^{-n/2}e^{-|x|^{2}/(4t) }\ ,\qquad x\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\ ,\quad t>0\ ,\]

it follows that \(\{\,w_{t}\ ;\ t>0\,\}\) is an approximate identity. Thus, given \(\varphi\in{\cal D}(X,E)\), we infer from [Ama95, (III.4.2.10) and (III.4.2.25)] that \(w_{1/k}*\varphi\to\varphi\) in \({\cal E}(X,E)\).

Let \(k\in\hbox{\raise 1.5pt\hbox{$\bullet$}\kern-12.0pt\raise 1.5pt\hbox{$ \bullet$}\kern-12.0pt\raise 1.5pt\hbox{$\bullet$}\kern-12.0pt\raise 1.5pt \hbox{$\bullet$}\kern-12.0pt\raise 1.5pt\hbox{$\bullet$}\kern-12.0pt \raise 1.5pt\hbox{$\bullet$}\kern-12.

---

**Proof** It is an obvious consequence of Theorem 1.3.6(i) that it suffices to prove that \({\cal D}(X)\) is separable. Thus let \({\cal P}\) be the set of all polynomials in \(n\) indeterminates with rational coefficients (in \({\mathbb{K}}\)). Define \((X_{k})\) by (1.1.5). If \(X_{k}\neq\emptyset\), fix \(\varphi_{k}\in{\cal D}(X_{k+1})\) with \(\varphi_{k}\,|\,X_{k}=1\), and put \(\varphi_{k}:=0\) if \(X_{k}=\emptyset\). Then \(S:=\{\,\varphi_{k}p\ ;\ p\in{\cal P},\ k\in{\mathbb{N}}\,\}\) is a countable subset of \({\cal D}(X)\). It is an easy consequence of Lemma 1.3.7 and the properties of the LF-topology of \({\cal D}(X)\) that \(S\) is dense in \({\cal D}(X)\).

**1.3.9 Corollary** _Suppose that_

\[\mathfrak{F}\in\{\,{\cal D}^{\prime},{\cal E},{\cal E}^{\prime},{\cal S},{ \cal S}^{\prime},{\cal O}_{M},C_{0},C^{k},W_{p}^{k}\ ;\ 1\leq p<\infty,\ k \in{\mathbb{N}}\,\}\ ,\]

_and that \(X={\mathbb{R}}^{n}\) if \(\mathfrak{F}=W_{p}^{k}\). Then \(\mathfrak{F}(X,E)\) is separable if \(E\) is separable._

**Proof** Theorem 1.3.6 implies \({\cal D}(X,E)\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}\mathfrak{F}(X,E)\). Hence the assertion follows from Proposition 1.3.8.

### Topological Tensor Products and the Kernel Theorem

In the first part of this subsection we collect some facts about tensor products of LCSs. This theory is easily accessible in standard books on linear functional analysis and topological vector spaces, in particular, in [30], [32], and [17]. Thus we are rather brief and do not give proofs. In the second part we prove a version of the kernel theorem. This general abstract theorem will be of fundamental importance for defining bilinear operations on vector-valued distributions.

#### Algebraic Tensor Products

Let \(V\) and \(W\) be vector spaces. A **tensor product** of \(V\) and \(W\) is a pair \((T,\beta)\) consisting of a vector space \(T\) and a bilinear map \(\beta:\,V\times W\to T\) such that

* \(T=\mbox{span}\bigl{(}\mbox{im}(\beta)\bigr{)}\),
* \(\beta({\cal V}\times{\cal W})\) is linearly independent in \(T\), if \({\cal V}\) and \({\cal W}\) are linearly independent in \(V\) and \(W\), respectively.

It can be shown that there exists a tensor product of \(V\) and \(W\) and that it is unique, except for linear isomorphisms. If \((T,\beta)\) is a tensor product of \(V\) and \(W\), we put

\[V\otimes W:=T\ ,\quad v\otimes w:=\beta(v,w)\ ,\qquad(v,w)\in V\times W\ ,\]

which is justified by 'uniqueness'.

**1.4.1 Remarks** **(a)** The tensor product has the following important **universality property**: if \(U\) is a vector space and \(b:\,V\times W\to U\) is bilinear, there exists a

---

unique linear map \(B:\ V\otimes W\to U\) such that the diagram

is commutative.

**Proof** Let \({\cal V}\) and \({\cal W}\) be bases of \(V\) and \(W\), respectively. Then \(\beta({\cal V},{\cal W})\) is a basis of \(V\otimes W\) by (i) and (ii) above. Define \(B\) on \(\beta({\cal V},{\cal W})\) by

\[B(v\otimes w):=b(v,w)\ ,\qquad(v,w)\in{\cal V}\times{\cal W}\ ,\]

and extend it linearly. Then \(B\) has the desired property.

**(b)** Let \(V_{j}\) and \(W_{j}\) be vector spaces and \(A_{j}\in{\rm Hom}(V_{j},W_{j})\) for \(j=1,2\). Then there exists a unique

\[A_{1}\otimes A_{2}\in{\rm Hom}(V_{1}\otimes V_{2},W_{1}\otimes W_{2})\ ,\]

the **tensor product** of \(A_{1}\) and \(A_{2}\), such that

\[(A_{1}\otimes A_{2})(v_{1}\otimes v_{2})=A_{1}v_{1}\otimes A_{2}v_{2}\ , \qquad(v_{1},v_{2})\in V_{1}\times V_{2}\ .\]

**Proof** Since \((v_{1},v_{2})\mapsto A_{1}v_{1}\otimes A_{2}v_{2}\) is a bilinear map from \(V_{1}\times V_{2}\) into \(W_{1}\otimes W_{2}\), the assertion follows from (a).

**(c)** Let \(V_{1}\), \(V_{2}\), and \(V_{3}\) be vector spaces. There exists a linear isomorphism

\[V_{1}\otimes(V_{2}\otimes V_{3})\to(V_{1}\otimes V_{2})\otimes V_{3}\] (1.4.1)

such that

\[v_{1}\otimes(v_{2}\otimes v_{3})\mapsto(v_{1}\otimes v_{2})\otimes v_{3}\ .\] (1.4.2)

This means that tensor products are (canonically) associative (so that parentheses can be omitted).

**Proof** Let \({\cal V}_{j}\) be a basis of \(V_{j}\). Define (1.4.1) by (1.4.2) on the basis \({\cal V}_{1}\otimes({\cal V}_{2}\otimes{\cal V}_{3})\) of \(V_{1}\otimes(V_{2}\otimes V_{3})\) and extend it linearly.

**Basic Examples**

**1.4.2 Examples**

Let \({\mathbb{K}}^{m\times n}\) be the vector space of all \((m\times n)\)-matrices with entries in \({\mathbb{K}}\) and let

\[{\mathbb{K}}^{m}\times{\mathbb{K}}^{n}\to{\mathbb{K}}^{m\times n}\ ,\qquad(x,y) \mapsto[x_{j}y_{k}]_{{1\leq j\leq m\atop 1\leq k\leq n}}\ .\]

---

Then \(\mathbb{K}^{m}\otimes\mathbb{K}^{n}=\mathbb{K}^{m\times n}\). Note that

\[x\otimes y=xy^{\top}\ ,\qquad x,y\in\mathbb{K}^{m}\times\mathbb{K}^{n}\ ,\]

if \(\mathbb{K}^{m}\) and \(\mathbb{K}^{n}\) are identified with \(\mathbb{K}^{m\times 1}\) and \(\mathbb{K}^{n\times 1}\), respectively, and \(a^{\top}\) denotes the **transposed** of \(a\in\mathbb{K}^{r\times s}\) for \(r,s\in\overset{\star}{\mathbb{N}}\). Furthermore,

\[x\otimes y=\langle y,\cdot\rangle x\ ,\qquad(x,y)\in\mathbb{K}^{m}\times \mathbb{K}^{n}\ ,\]

if \(\mathbb{K}^{m\times n}\) and \((\mathbb{K}^{n})^{\prime}\) are identified with \(\mathcal{L}(\mathbb{K}^{n},\mathbb{K}^{m})\) and \(\mathbb{K}^{n}\), respectively.

**(b)**: Let \(S\) be a nonempty set, \(V\) a vector space, and \(\Phi(S)\) a vector subspace of \(\mathbb{K}^{S}\). Define a bilinear map

\[\Phi(S)\times V\to V^{S}\ ,\qquad(\varphi,v)\mapsto\varphi\otimes v\]

by \(\varphi\otimes v(s):=\varphi(s)v\). Then

\[\Phi(S)\otimes V:=\operatorname{span}\bigl{\{}\,\varphi\otimes v\ ;\ \varphi \in\Phi(S),\ v\in V\,\bigr{\}}\]

is the tensor product of \(\Phi(S)\) and \(V\)_in_\(V^{S}\), that is, the span lies in \(V^{S}\) (cf. Subsections V.2.4 of [1] and 1.3).

**Proof** Let \(\varphi_{1},\ldots,\varphi_{n}\) be linearly independent in \(\Phi(S)\) and \(v_{1},\ldots,v_{n}\) linearly independent in \(V\). Suppose that there exist \(\xi_{jk}\in\mathbb{K}\) such that \(\sum_{jk}\xi_{jk}\varphi_{j}\otimes v_{k}=0\). Put \(\psi_{k}:=\sum_{j}\xi_{jk}\varphi_{j}\) so that \(\sum_{k}\psi_{k}\otimes v_{k}=0\). Then \(\sum\psi_{k}(s)v_{k}=0\) for each \(s\in S\). This implies \(\psi_{k}=0\) for \(k=1,\ldots,n\) by the linear independence of \(v_{1},\ldots,v_{n}\). Since the \(\varphi_{j}\) are linearly independent in \(\Phi(S)\), we see that each \(\xi_{jk}\) is zero.

**(c)**: Suppose that \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{D},\mathcal{E},\mathcal{S},\mathcal{O}_{M}\}\). Then \(\mathfrak{F}(X)\otimes E\), defined by (1.3.17) and (1.3.18), is _the_ tensor product of \(\mathfrak{F}(X)\) and \(E\) in \(\mathfrak{F}(X,E)\). Similarly, if \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{D},\mathcal{E},\mathcal{S}\}\) then \(\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\otimes E\), defined by (1.3.19) and (1.3.20), is the tensor product of \(\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\) and \(E\) in \(\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X,E)\).

**Proof** This follows from (b) by putting \(S:=X\) and \(S:=\mathfrak{F}(X)\), respectively.

**(d)**: Let \(E\) be a Banach space, \(S\) and \(T\) nonempty sets, and \(\Phi(S)\) and \(\Psi(T,E)\) vector subspaces of \(\mathbb{K}^{S}\) and \(E^{T}\), respectively. Define a bilinear map

\[\Phi(S)\times\Psi(T,E)\to E^{S\times T}\ ,\quad(\varphi,\psi)\mapsto\varphi \otimes\psi\]

by \(\varphi\otimes\psi(s,t):=\varphi(s)\psi(t)\). Then

\[\Phi(S)\otimes\Psi(T,E):=\operatorname{span}\bigl{\{}\,\varphi\otimes\psi\ ; \ \varphi\in\Phi(S),\ \psi\in\Psi(T,E)\,\bigr{\}}\]

is the tensor product of \(\Phi(S)\) and \(\Psi(T,E)\)_in_\(E^{S\times T}\).

**Proof** Identify \(E^{S\times T}\) and \((E^{T})^{S}\) by means of

\[\bigl{[}(s,t)\mapsto u(s,t)\bigr{]}\longleftrightarrow\bigl{[}s\mapsto u(s, \cdot)\bigr{]}\ .\]

Then the assertion follows from (b).

---

#### Projective Tensor Products

Let now \(F:=(F,\mathcal{P})\) and \(G:=(G,\mathcal{Q})\) be LCSs. For \((p,q)\in\mathcal{P}\times\mathcal{Q}\) we put

\[p\otimes_{\pi}q(z):=\inf\bigl{\{}\,\sum\!p(f_{j})q(g_{j})\ ;\ z=\sum\!f_{j} \otimes g_{j}\,\bigr{\}}\ ,\qquad z\in F\otimes G\ .\]

This defines a seminorm on \(F\otimes G\), the **tensor product** of the seminorms \(p\) and \(q\). In fact, it is the Minkowski functional of the absolutely convex hull of \(\mathbb{B}_{p}\otimes\mathbb{B}_{q}\) where \(\mathbb{B}_{p}:=[p<1]\). It has the property

\[p\otimes_{\pi}q(f\otimes g)=p(f)q(g)\ ,\qquad(f,g)\in F\times G\ .\] (1.4.3)

Thus the family of the tensor product seminorms is separating. Consequently, it defines a locally convex Hausdorff topology on \(F\otimes G\), the **projective topology**, and

\[F\otimes_{\pi}G:=\bigl{(}F\otimes G,\ \{\,p\otimes_{\pi}q\ ;\ p\in\mathcal{P}, \ q\in\mathcal{Q}\,\}\bigr{)}\]

is the **projective tensor product** of \(F\) and \(G\). We denote by \(F\stackrel{{\sim}}{{\otimes}}G\) the completion of the LCS \(F\otimes_{\pi}G\).

#### Nuclear Maps and Spaces

A linear map \(N\) from the LCS \(F\) into a Banach space \(E\) is **nuclear** if there exist an equicontinuous sequence \((f^{\prime}_{j})\) in \(F^{\prime}\), a bounded sequence \((b_{j})\) in \(E\), and a summable sequence \((\lambda_{j})\) in \(\mathbb{K}\) such that

\[Nf=\sum_{j}\lambda_{j}\langle f^{\prime}_{j},f\rangle b_{j}\ ,\qquad f\in F\ .\] (1.4.4)

The LCS \(F\) is **nuclear** if each continuous linear map from \(F\) into a Banach space is nuclear. It is **conuclear** if its dual is nuclear.

For the following theorem we recall convention (1.1.10).

**1.4.3 Theorem**: _Let \(X\) be a nonempty open subset of \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) and suppose that \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{D},\mathcal{E},\mathcal{S},\mathcal{O}_{M}\}\). Then \(\mathfrak{F}(X)\) is nuclear and conuclear._

**Proof** If \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{D},\mathcal{E},\mathcal{S}\}\), this can be found in [10] (also cf. [1] and [2]). As for \(\mathfrak{F}=\mathcal{O}_{M}\), we refer to [12, II.4.4].

#### Projective Tensor Products and Maps of Finite Rank

Lastly, we define an injective linear map by

\[\tau:\,F\otimes G\to\mathcal{L}(F^{\prime},G)\ ,\quad f\otimes g\mapsto \langle\cdot,f\rangle g\ ,\] (1.4.5)

the **canonical injection**. Then we prepare for the proof of the abstract kernel theorem by deriving a series of lemmas.

---

**1.4.4 Lemma**: _Let \(F\) be reflexive and nuclear. Then \(\tau\) is a toplinear isomorphism from \(F\otimes_{\pi}G\) onto the subspace of \({\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\) of maps of finite rank._

**Proof** Given a continuous seminorm \(q\) on \(G\) and a bounded subset \(B^{\prime}\) of \(F^{\prime}\),

\[\sup_{f^{\prime}\in B^{\prime}}q\bigl{(}\tau z(f^{\prime})\bigr{)}\leq\sum_{j} \sup_{f^{\prime}\in B^{\prime}}|\langle f^{\prime},f_{j}\rangle|\,q(g_{j})\ , \qquad z=\sum f_{j}\otimes g_{j}\in F\otimes G\ .\] (1.4.6)

Since

\[f\mapsto p(f):=\sup_{f^{\prime}\in B^{\prime}}|\langle f^{\prime},f\rangle|\]

defines a continuous seminorm on \(F^{\prime\prime}=F\), it follows from (1.4.6) that

\[\sup_{f^{\prime}\in B^{\prime}}q\bigl{(}\tau z(f^{\prime})\bigr{)}\leq p \otimes_{\pi}q(z)\ ,\qquad z\in F\otimes G\ .\]

Hence

\[\tau\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}F\otimes_{\pi}G,{\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\bigr{)}\ .\]

It is obvious that \(\tau z\) has finite rank for \(z\in F\otimes G\). Conversely, suppose that \(T\in{\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\) has finite rank, and let \(g_{1},\ldots,g_{m}\) be a basis of \({\rm im}(T)\). Then

\[Tf^{\prime}=\sum_{j=1}^{m}\xi_{j}(f^{\prime})g_{j}\ ,\qquad f^{\prime}\in F^{ \prime}\ ,\]

where

\[\xi_{j}(f^{\prime})=\langle g^{\prime}_{j},Tf^{\prime}\rangle_{G}=\langle T^{ \prime}g^{\prime}_{j},f^{\prime}\rangle_{F^{\prime}}\ ,\qquad f^{\prime}\in F^{ \prime}\ ,\]

the \(g^{\prime}_{j}\in G^{\prime}\) satisfying \(\langle g^{\prime}_{j},g_{k}\rangle=\delta_{jk}\). Since \(T^{\prime}\in{\cal L}(G^{\prime},F)\) by the reflexivity of \(F\), it follows that \(f_{j}:=T^{\prime}g^{\prime}_{j}\in F\) and \(T=\sum_{j=1}^{m}\langle\cdot,f_{j}\rangle_{F}g_{j}\). Hence

\[T=\tau z\qquad\hbox{with}\quad z:=\sum_{j=1}^{m}f_{j}\otimes g_{j}\in F \otimes G\ .\]

This shows that \(\tau\) is a bijection from \(F\otimes G\) onto the subspace of \({\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\) of finite rank operators.

Now let \(p\) and \(q\) be continuous seminorms on \(F\) and \(G\), respectively. Given \(T\in{\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\) of finite rank, let \(z:=\tau^{-1}T\), and let \(\sum f_{j}\otimes g_{j}\) be a representation of \(z\) in \(F\otimes G\). Denote by

\[{\mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{p}:=\bigl{\{}\,f^{\prime}\in F^{\prime}\ ;\ |\langle f^{ \prime},f\rangle|\leq 1\ \hbox{for}\ f\in{\mathbb{B}}_{p}\ \bigr{\}}\]

the polar of \({\mathbb{B}}_{p}\). Then

\[\eqalign{p\otimes_{\varepsilon}q(z)&:=\sup_{(f^{\prime},g^{\prime})\in{ \mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{p}\times{\mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{q}}\Bigl{|}\sum_{j}\langle f ^{\prime},f_{j}\rangle\langle g^{\prime},g_{j}\rangle\Bigr{|}\cr&=\sup_{f^{ \prime}\in{\mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{p}}\sup_{g^{\prime}\in{\mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{q }}\bigl{|}\bigl{\langle}g^{\prime},\tau z(f^{\prime})\bigr{\rangle}\bigr{|}= \sup_{f^{\prime}\in{\mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{p}}q\bigl{(}\tau z(f^{\prime})\bigr{)} \cr}\]

by the bipolar theorem. Since \(F\) is reflexive, \({\mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{p}\) is weakly compact by the Alaoglu-Bourbaki theorem. Hence it is weakly bounded and thus, thanks to Mackey's

---

theorem, bounded in \(F^{\prime}\) (cf. [Hor66], [Jar81], [Tre67], or [Sch71] for these standard theorems from the theory of LCSs).

Observe that \(p\otimes_{\varepsilon}q\), as defined in (1.4.7), is a seminorm on \(F\otimes G\). In the theory of LCSs it is shown that the family \(\{\,p\otimes_{\varepsilon}q\,\,;\,\,p\in{\cal P},\,\,q\in{\cal Q}\,\}\) is a separating family of seminorms on \(F\otimes G\) defining the 'injective tensor product topology' on \(F\otimes G\). Since \(F\) is nuclear, this injective tensor product topology coincides with the projective tensor product topology of \(F\otimes G\) (e.g., [Tre67, Theorem 50.1(f)]). Thus we infer from (1.4.7) that \(\tau^{-1}\in{\cal L}({\cal R},F\otimes_{\pi}G)\), where \({\cal R}\) is the linear subspace of \({\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\) of finite rank operators.

**Approximation by Maps of Finite Rank**

Let \(p\) be a continuous seminorm on \(F\). Then \(\ker(p)\) is a closed linear subspace of \(F\). Hence \(F_{p}:=F/\ker(p)\) is a normed vector space with respect to the quotient norm

\[\widehat{x}:=\widehat{x}_{p}:=x+\ker(p)\mapsto\widehat{p}(\widehat{x}):=\inf \bigl{\{}\,p(y)\,\,;\,\,y\in\widehat{x}\,\bigr{\}}\ .\] (1.4.8)

Observe that \(\widehat{p}(\widehat{x})=p(y)\) for \(y\in\widehat{x}\). Let \(q\) be a second continuous seminorm on \(F\) such that \(q\geq p\). Then \(\ker(q)\subset\ker(p)\) implies that \(\widehat{x}_{q}\mapsto\widehat{x}_{p}\) is a well-defined continuous linear map from \(F_{q}\) into \(F_{p}\) of norm at most one. Let \(\widetilde{F}_{p}\) be the completion of \(F_{p}\). Then we consider \(\widehat{x}_{q}\mapsto\widehat{x}_{p}\) to be a linear map from \(F_{q}\) into \(\widetilde{F}_{p}\), the **canonical map**\(F_{q}\to\widetilde{F}_{p}\).

**1.4.5 Lemma**: _Suppose that \(F\) is nuclear and \(p\) is a continuous seminorm on \(F\). Then there exists a continuous seminorm \(q\geq p\) on \(F\) such that the canonical map \(F_{q}\to\widetilde{F}_{p}\) is nuclear._

**Proof** Since \(F\) is nuclear, the quotient map \(\pi_{p}:\,F\to\widetilde{F}_{p}\) is nuclear. Hence \(\pi_{p}\) has a representation of the form (1.4.4) where \((b_{j})\) is a bounded sequence in \(\widetilde{F}_{p}\). Consequently,

\[p(f)=\widehat{p}(\pi_{p}f)\leq c\sup_{j}|\langle f^{\prime}_{j},f\rangle|=:q(f) \ ,\qquad f\in F\ ,\] (1.4.9)

with \(c:=\sum_{j}|\lambda_{j}|\sup_{j}\|b_{j}\|\). The equicontinuity of the sequence \((f^{\prime}_{j})\) implies that \(q\) is a continuous seminorm on \(F\). By (1.4.8) and (1.4.9) each \(f^{\prime}_{j}\) defines naturally a continuous linear form \(h^{\prime}_{j}\) on \(F_{q}\) of norm at most \(1/c\). Thus, since the canonical map \(F_{q}\to\widetilde{F}_{p}\) is given by

\[\widehat{f}_{q}\mapsto\sum_{j}\lambda_{j}\bigl{\langle}h^{\prime}_{j},\widehat {f}_{q}\bigr{\rangle}_{F_{q}}b_{j}\ ,\]

it is nuclear.

By means of the preceding lemma we can give sufficient conditions for linear operators to be approximable by operators of finite rank.

---

#### 1.4.6 Lemma

_If \(F\) is nuclear, the maps of finite rank are dense in \({\cal L}(F,G)\)._

ProofLet \(p\) be a continuous seminorm on \(F\). Lemma 1.4.5 guarantees the existence of a continuous seminorm \(q\geq p\) on \(F\) such that the canonical map \(S:F_{q}\to\widetilde{F}_{p}\) is nuclear. Hence there exist a summable sequence \((\lambda_{j})\) in \({\mathbb{K}}\) and bounded sequences \(\bigl{(}\widehat{f}^{\prime}_{j}\bigr{)}\) and \(\bigl{(}\widehat{f}_{j}\bigr{)}\) in \((F_{q})^{\prime}\) and \(\widetilde{F}_{p}\), respectively, such that

\[S\widehat{f}=\sum_{j}\lambda_{j}\bigl{\langle}\widehat{f}^{\prime}_{j},\widehat {f}\bigr{\rangle}\widehat{f}_{j}\ ,\qquad\widehat{f}\in F_{q}\ .\]

Thus, letting

\[S_{n}:=\sum_{j=0}^{n}\lambda_{j}\bigl{\langle}\widehat{f}^{\prime}_{j},\cdot \bigr{\rangle}\widehat{f}_{j}\ ,\qquad n\in{\mathbb{N}}\ ,\]

it is obvious that

\[S_{n}\to S\qquad\hbox{in }{\cal L}\bigl{(}F_{q},\widetilde{F}_{p}\bigr{)}\ .\]

Observe that the diagram

is commutative, where \(\pi_{q}\) and \(\pi_{p}\) are the quotient maps. Choose \(f_{j}\in\widehat{f}_{j}\) and put

\[T_{n}f:=\sum_{j=0}^{n}\lambda_{j}\bigl{\langle}\widehat{f}^{\prime}_{j},\pi_{q }f\bigr{\rangle}f_{j}\ ,\qquad f\in F\ .\]

Then, thanks to (1.4.10) and (1.4.11),

\[p(T_{n}f-f)\to 0\ ,\qquad n\to\infty\ ,\]

uniformly with respect to \(f\) in bounded subsets of \(F\).

Now let \(R\in{\cal L}(F,G)\) and a continuous seminorm \(r\) on \(G\) be given. Then \(p:=r\circ R\) is a continuous seminorm on \(F\). Put \(R_{n}:=R\circ T_{n}\). It follows from (1.4.12) that \(r\bigl{(}(R_{n}-R)f\bigr{)}\to 0\) as \(n\to\infty\), uniformly with respect to \(f\) in bounded subsets of \(F\). Since \(R_{n}\in{\cal L}(F,G)\) has finite rank, the assertion follows.

#### Completeness of Spaces of Linear Operators

In our last preparatory lemma we give conditions for \({\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\) to be complete.

#### 1.4.7 Lemma

_Let \(F\) and \(G\) be complete and let \(F\) be reflexive. Then \({\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\) is complete._

---

**Proof** Let \((T_{\alpha})\) be a Cauchy net in \({\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\). Since \(G\) is complete and \((T_{\alpha}f^{\prime})\) is a Cauchy net in \(G\), there exists \(T\in{\rm Hom}(F^{\prime},G)\) such that \(T_{\alpha}f^{\prime}\to Tf^{\prime}\) in \(G\) for each \(f^{\prime}\in F^{\prime}\). Given \(g^{\prime}\in G^{\prime}\),

\[\langle f^{\prime},T^{\prime}_{\alpha}g^{\prime}\rangle_{F}=\langle g^{\prime}, T_{\alpha}f^{\prime}\rangle_{G}\to\langle g^{\prime},Tf^{\prime}\rangle_{G}=g^{ \prime}\circ T(f^{\prime})\] (1.4.13)

for each \(f^{\prime}\in F^{\prime}\), thanks to \(F^{\prime\prime}=F\). Observe that \((T^{\prime}_{\alpha}g^{\prime})\) is a Cauchy net in \(F\). Hence we deduce from (1.4.13) and the completeness of \(F\) that \(g^{\prime}\circ T\in F\) for each \(g^{\prime}\in G^{\prime}\).

Now suppose that \(g^{\prime}_{\alpha}\stackrel{{ w^{*}}}{{\longrightarrow}}g^{\prime}\). Then

\[g^{\prime}_{\alpha}\circ T(f^{\prime})=\langle g^{\prime}_{\alpha},Tf^{\prime} \rangle_{G}\to\langle g^{\prime},Tf^{\prime}\rangle_{G}\ ,\qquad f^{\prime}\in F^{ \prime}\ .\]

This shows that \({}^{t}T\in{\cal L}(G^{\prime}_{w^{*}},F_{w})\), where \({}^{t}T\) is the algebraic dual of \(T\). Let \(q\) be a continuous seminorm on \(G\). Then \({\mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{q}\) is \(w^{*}\)-compact by the Alaoglu-Bourbaki theorem. Hence \(K:={}^{t}T({\mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{q})\) is weakly compact as well, thus weakly bounded. Consequently, \(K\) is bounded in \(F\). Hence \(V^{\prime}:=K^{\circ}\) is a neighborhood of zero in \(F^{\prime}\). Observe that \(f^{\prime}\in V^{\prime}\) iff \(|\langle f^{\prime},{}^{t}Tg^{\prime}\rangle_{F}|=|\langle g^{\prime},Tf^{ \prime}\rangle_{G}|\leq 1\) for \(g^{\prime}\in{\mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{q}\), that is, iff \(Tf^{\prime}\in{}^{\circ}({\mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{q})\), where, given \(C^{\prime}\subset G^{\prime}\),

\[{}^{\circ}C^{\prime}:=\big{\{}\,g\in G\ ;\ |\langle g^{\prime},g\rangle|\leq 1 \ {\rm for}\ g^{\prime}\in C^{\prime}\,\big{\}}\]

is the polar of \(C^{\prime}\) in \(G\). By the bipolar theorem \({}^{\circ}({\mathbb{B}}^{\circ}_{q})=\bar{{\mathbb{B}}}_{q}\). Hence

\[\sup\big{\{}\,q(Tf^{\prime})\ ;\ f^{\prime}\in V^{\prime}\,\big{\}}\leq 1\ .\] (1.4.14)

Since \(V^{\prime}\) is the polar of \(K\), it is absolutely convex and \(w^{*}\)-closed. Thus it is weakly closed by the reflexivity of \(F\). Hence it is closed. Denoting by \(p\) the Minkowski functional of \(V^{\prime}\), it follows that \(f^{\prime}\in V^{\prime}\) iff \(p(f^{\prime})\leq 1\). From this and (1.4.14) we infer that

\[q(Tf^{\prime})\leq p(f^{\prime})\ ,\qquad f^{\prime}\in F^{\prime}\ ,\]

which shows that \(T\in{\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\), thanks to the fact that \(p\) is a continuous seminorm on \(F^{\prime}\).

**The Abstract Kernel Theorem**

After these preparations we can prove the abstract kernel theorem in a form that is most suitable for our purposes.

**1.4.8 Theorem** _Let \(F\) and \(G\) be complete LCSs such that \(F\) is reflexive, nuclear, and conuclear. Then \(\tau\) is_ (_that is, extends to_) _a toplinear isomorphism_

\[\tau:\ F\stackrel{{\sim}}{{\otimes}}G\to{\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\ ,\] (1.4.15)

_the_ **canonical isomorphism**.

---

**Proof** It follows from Lemma 1.4.7 that \({\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\) is complete. Since \(F\) is conuclear, we infer from Lemma 1.4.6 that the linear subspace \({\cal R}\) of maps of finite rank is dense in \({\cal L}(F^{\prime},G)\). Since \(F\) is also nuclear, Lemma 1.4.4 guarantees that \(\tau\) is a toplinear isomorphism from \(F\otimes_{\pi}G\) onto \({\cal R}\). Now the assertion is an easy consequence of the density of \(F\otimes_{\pi}G\) in \(F\mathbin{\mathaccent 0{\sim}\mskip-4.0mu \otimes}G\) and a well-known result about continuous extensions of continuous linear maps (e.g., [30, Theorem 3.4.2]).

**Tensor Product Characterizations of Some Distribution Spaces**

As a first application of this general theorem we can prove the following characterizations for spaces of vector-valued distributions. They are the basis for defining bilinear maps of vector-valued distributions in the next subsection.

**1.4.9 Theorem** : _Let \(E\) be a Banach space. Then_

\[\mathfrak{F}(X)\mathbin{\mathaccent 0{\sim}\mskip-4.0mu \otimes}E=\mathfrak{F}(X ,E)\cong{\cal L}\bigl{(}\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X),E\bigr{)}\ ,\qquad \mathfrak{F}\in\{{\cal D},{\cal E},{\cal S},{\cal O}_{M}\}\ ,\]

_and_

\[\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\mathbin{\mathaccent 0{\sim}\mskip-4.0mu \otimes}E= \mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X,E)={\cal L}\bigl{(}\mathfrak{F}(X),E\bigr{)}\ , \qquad\mathfrak{F}\in\{{\cal D},{\cal E},{\cal S}\}\ ,\]

_where \(\cong\) denotes the canonical toplinear isomorphism._

**Proof** From Theorem 1.1.2 we know that \(\mathfrak{F}(X)\) and \(\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\) are complete and reflexive. Furthermore, \(\mathfrak{F}(X)\) and \(\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\) are both nuclear and conuclear, thanks to Theorem 1.4.3. Hence it follows from Theorem 1.4.8 that

\[\tau:\ \mathfrak{F}(X)\mathbin{\mathaccent 0{\sim}\mskip-4.0mu \otimes}E\to{ \cal L}\bigl{(}\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X),E\bigr{)}\]

for \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{{\cal D},{\cal E},{\cal S},{\cal O}_{M}\}\), and

\[\tau:\ \mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X)\mathbin{\mathaccent 0{\sim}\mskip-4.0mu \otimes}E \to{\cal L}\bigl{(}\mathfrak{F}(X),E\bigr{)}=\mathfrak{F}^{\prime}(X,E)\ .\]

for \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{{\cal D},{\cal E},{\cal S}\}\) are toplinear isomorphisms. This proves the second assertion and part of the first one. It remains to show that \(\mathfrak{F}(X)\mathbin{\mathaccent 0{\sim}\mskip-4.0mu \otimes}E= \mathfrak{F}(X,E)\).
