**1.8.11 Theorem**: \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\cong\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{D}(X_ {1}),\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\big{)}\) _by means of the canonical toplinear isomorphism._

ProofIt is easily verified that the bilinear map

\[\mathcal{D}(X_{1})\times\mathcal{D}(X_{2})\to\mathcal{D}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\ ,\quad(\varphi_{1},\varphi_{2})\mapsto\varphi_{1}\otimes\varphi_{2}\]

is continuous, hence hypocontinuous. Given \(u\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\) and \(\varphi_{1}\in\mathcal{D}(X_{1})\), put

\[\varphi_{1}\cdot u(\varphi_{2}):=u(\varphi_{1}\otimes\varphi_{2})\ ,\qquad \varphi_{2}\in\mathcal{D}(X_{2})\ ,\]

---

(using obvious identifications). It follows that \(\varphi_{1}\cdot u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\) and

\[T_{u}:=(\varphi_{1}\mapsto\varphi_{1}\cdot u)\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal D}(X_{1}),{ \cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\bigr{)}\ .\]

Let \((u_{\alpha})\) be a net in \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\) converging towards zero. Then \(\langle u_{\alpha},\varphi\rangle\to 0\), uniformly with respect to \(\varphi\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal D}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\). Consequently, \(T_{u_{\alpha}}\varphi_{1}=\varphi_{1}\cdot u_{\alpha}\to 0\) in \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\), uniformly with respect to \(\varphi_{1}\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal D}(X_{1})\). This shows that

\[T:=(u\mapsto T_{u})\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2}),{ \cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal D}(X_{1}),{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\bigr{)}\bigr{)}\ .\]

By Theorem 1.4.8 we know that

\[{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1})\mathrel{\mathop{\otimes}\limits^{\sim}}{\cal D}^{ \prime}(X_{2})\cong{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal D}(X_{1}),{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2}) \bigr{)}\ .\]

If \(u=u_{1}\otimes u_{2}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1})\otimes{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\), we see that \(T_{u}\varphi_{1}=\langle u_{1},\varphi_{1}\rangle u_{2}\) for \(\varphi_{1}\in{\cal D}(X_{1})\). This shows that \(T\,|\,{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1})\otimes{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\) equals the canonical injection (1.4.5) of the tensor product \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1})\otimes{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\) in \({\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal D}(X_{1}),{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\bigr{)}\). Thus we infer from (1.8.19) that \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\) induces on \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1})\otimes{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\) a topology that is stronger than the projective topology.

Conversely, let \((u_{\alpha})\) be a net in \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1})\otimes_{\pi}{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\) converging towards zero. Then \(\langle u_{\alpha},\varphi_{1}\otimes\varphi_{2}\rangle\to 0\), uniformly with respect to \(\varphi_{j}\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal D}(X_{j})\), \(j=1,2\), thanks to Lemma 1.8.10. Hence, given any \(\psi_{j}\in{\cal D}(X_{j}),\ j=1,2\), it follows that

\[\bigl{\langle}(\psi_{1}\otimes\psi_{2})u_{\alpha},\varphi_{1}\otimes\varphi_{2 }\bigr{\rangle}_{{\cal E}}=\langle u_{\alpha},\psi_{1}\varphi_{1}\otimes\psi_{ 2}\varphi_{2}\rangle_{{\cal D}}\to 0\ ,\]

uniformly with respect to \(\varphi_{j}\) in bounded subsets of \({\cal E}(X_{j}),\ j=1,2\). Now we infer from Lemma 1.8.10 that \((\psi_{1}\otimes\psi_{2})u_{\alpha}\to 0\) in \({\cal E}^{\prime}(X_{1})\otimes{\cal E}^{\prime}(X_{2})\). Theorem 1.8.9 implies \((\psi_{1}\otimes\psi_{2})u_{\alpha}\to 0\) in \({\cal E}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\) for each pair \((\psi_{1},\psi_{2})\in{\cal D}(X_{1})\times{\cal D}(X_{2})\). This entails \(u_{\alpha}\,|\,{\cal D}_{K}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\to 0\) for \(K\subset\subset X_{1}\times X_{2}\). Consequently, \(u_{\alpha}\to 0\) in \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\), which shows that the projective topology is stronger than the topology induced by \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\) on \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1})\otimes{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\).

We know from Corollary 1.8.2 that \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1})\otimes{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\) is dense in \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\). Hence it follows that

\[{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1})\mathrel{\mathop{\otimes}\limits^{\sim}}{\cal D}^{ \prime}(X_{2})={\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\ ,\]

which, together with (1.8.20), proves the assertion.

**1.8.12 Remarks** (a): It may be useful to reinterprete the Kernel Theorems 1.8.9 and 1.8.11. Suppose, for example, that \(k\in{\cal E}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2},E)\) and define \(K\) by the relation

\[(K\varphi_{1})(\varphi_{2})=k(\varphi_{1}\otimes\varphi_{2})\ ,\qquad\varphi_{j} \in{\cal E}(X_{j})\ ,\quad j=1,2\ .\]

Then Theorem 1.8.9 guarantees that

\[K\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal E}(X_{1}),{\cal E}^{\prime}(X_{2},E)\bigr{)}\ .\]

---

Conversely, for any \(K\) satisfying (1.8.23) there exists a unique \(k\in{\cal E}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2},E)\) such that (1.8.22) is true. Moreover, the map

\[{\cal E}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2},E)\to{\cal L}\big{(}{\cal E}(X_{1}),{\cal E} ^{\prime}(X_{2},E)\big{)}\ ,\quad k\mapsto K\] (1.8.24)

is a toplinear isomorphism. The distribution \(k\) is said to be the kernel of the map \(K\), and \(K\) is associated to the kernel \(k\). In _symbolic notation_ the isomorphism (1.8.24) is written as

\[K\varphi_{1}=\int_{X_{1}}k(x_{1},\cdot)\varphi_{1}(x_{1})\,dx_{1}\ ,\qquad \varphi_{1}\in{\cal E}(X_{1})\ .\]

In this case (1.8.22) takes the suggestive form

\[\int_{X_{2}}(K\varphi_{1})(x_{2})\varphi_{2}(x_{2})\,dx_{2}=\int_{X_{1}\times X _{2}}k(x_{1},x_{2})\varphi_{1}(x_{1})\varphi_{2}(x_{2})\,d(x_{1},x_{2})\ .\]

The kernel \(k\in{\cal E}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2},E)\) is said to be regularizing if its associated map \(K\) can be extended from \({\cal D}(X_{1})\) to a continuous linear map of \({\cal E}^{\prime}(X_{1})\) into \({\cal E}(X_{2},E)\). Thanks to Theorem 1.8.9 this is the case iff \(k\in{\cal E}(X_{1}\times X_{2},E)\). Similar definitions and conventions apply to tempered distributions and to the classical case of the Schwartz Kernel Theorem (1.8.11).
**(b)**: It is easily verified that the first part of the proof of Theorem 1.8.11 remains valid if we replace \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2})\) and \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2})\) by \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2},E)\) and \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2},E)\), respectively. From this we infer that

\[{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2},E)\hookrightarrow{\cal L}\big{(}{\cal D}(X _{1}),{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2},E)\big{)}\] (1.8.25)

by means of obvious identifications. This signifies that each kernel distribution \(k\in{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{1}\times X_{2},E)\) induces a unique continuous linear operator \(K\) from \({\cal D}(X_{1})\) to \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2},E)\), and that the map \(k\mapsto K\) is continuous and injective. But we do not know whether (1.8.25) is surjective. However,

\[{\cal L}\big{(}{\cal D}(X_{1}),{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2},E)\big{)}\cong{\cal D}^ {\prime}(X_{1})\stackrel{{\sim}}{{\otimes}}{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2 })\stackrel{{\sim}}{{\otimes}}E\ ,\]

where \(\cong\) denotes the canonical toplinear isomorphism.
**Proof** From Lemma 1.4.7 we infer that \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2},E)\) is complete. Hence Theorem 1.4.8 guarantees that

\[{\cal L}\big{(}{\cal D}(X_{1}),{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2},E)\big{)}\cong{\cal D}^ {\prime}(X_{1})\stackrel{{\sim}}{{\otimes}}{\cal D}^{\prime}(X_{2 },E)\ .\]

Now the assertion follows from Theorem 1.4.9 and the natural associativity of topological tensor products (cf. [10, SS I.1.4]).

---

### Convolutions of Vector-Valued Distributions

In Subsection 1.2 we have already defined convolutions of a vector-valued and a scalar distribution. In this subsection we extend these definitions to the case of two vector-valued distributions. In addition, we weaken the support restrictions.

#### The Basic Theorem

**1.9.1 Theorem**: _Suppose that either \(u_{1}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u_{2}\in{\cal E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\), or \(u_{1}\in{\cal S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u_{2}\in{\cal S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\). Then there exists a unique distribution in \({\cal D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\) or \({\cal O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\), respectively, the_ **convolution of \(u_{1}\) and \(u_{2}\) with respect to multiplication** (1.5.20), denoted by \(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\), such that_

\[(v_{1}\otimes e_{1})*_{\bullet}(v_{2}\otimes e_{2})=(v_{1}*v_{2})\otimes(e_{1} \bullet e_{2})\] (1.9.1)

_for \(v_{1},v_{2}\in{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), \(e_{j}\in E_{j}\), and \(j=1,2\), and such that the 'convolution maps' \((u_{1},u_{2})\mapsto u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\) are bilinear and hypocontinuous_:

\[{\cal D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times{\cal E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n}, E_{2})\to{\cal D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\] (1.9.2)

_and_

\[{\cal S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times{\cal S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\to{ \cal O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ ,\] (1.9.3)

_respectively. In addition, the convolution maps restrict to hypocontinuous bilinear maps_:

\[{\cal E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times{\cal E}^{\prime}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\to {\cal E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ ,\] (1.9.4) \[{\cal E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times{\cal E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^ {n},E_{2})\to {\cal E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ ,\] (1.9.5) \[{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times{\cal D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^ {n},E_{2})\to {\cal E}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ ,\] (1.9.6) \[{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times{\cal E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^ {n},E_{2})\to {\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ ,\] (1.9.7) \[{\cal S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times\ {\cal S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\to {\cal S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ .\] (1.9.8)

ProofLet \((\mathfrak{F}_{1},\mathfrak{F}_{2};\mathfrak{F}_{0})\) be either of the triplets \(({\cal D}^{\prime},{\cal E}^{\prime};{\cal D}^{\prime})\), \(({\cal S},{\cal S}^{\prime};{\cal O}_{M})\), \(({\cal E}^{\prime},{\cal E}^{\prime};{\cal E}^{\prime})\), \(({\cal E},{\cal E}^{\prime};{\cal E})\), \(({\cal D},{\cal D}^{\prime};{\cal E})\), \(({\cal D},{\cal E}^{\prime};{\cal D})\), or \(({\cal S},{\cal S};{\cal S})\), and put \(F_{j}:=\mathfrak{F}_{j}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), \(j=0,1,2\). Then the \(F_{j}\) are reflexive, complete, nuclear, and conuclear by Theorems 1.1.2 and 1.4.3. Moreover, \(F_{j}\stackrel{{\sim}}{{\otimes}}E_{j}=\mathfrak{F}_{j}(\mathbb{R} ^{n},E_{j})\) by Theorem 1.4.9. Define the maps (1.5.14) and (1.5.15) by \(f_{1}\odot f_{2}:=f_{1}*f_{2}\) and \(f_{1}\otimes v:=f_{1}*v\), respectively. From Propositions 1.2.1, 1.2.3, and 1.2.7 we know that they are well-defined and that (1.5.15) is hypocontinuous. Definition (1.2.1) of convolution immediately implies that condition (1.5.16) is satisfied. Hence Theorem 1.5.3 guarantees the existence of a unique hypocontinuous bilinear map

\[\mathfrak{F}_{1}(X,E_{1})\times\mathfrak{F}_{2}(X,E_{2})\to\mathfrak{F}_{0}(X, E_{0})\ ,\quad(u_{1},u_{2})\mapsto u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\] (1.9.9)

satisfying (1.9.1) for \(u_{j}\in\mathfrak{F}_{j}(X)\) and \(e_{j}\in E_{j}\), \(j=1,2\). By Theorem 1.3.6 the linear subspace \(\big{(}{\cal D}(X)\otimes E_{1}\big{)}\times\big{(}{\cal D}(X)\otimes E_{2}\big{)}\) is dense in \(\mathfrak{F}_{1}(X,E_{1})\times\mathfrak{F}_{2}(X,E_{2})\).

---

Hence the map (1.9.9) is determined by its restriction to this subspace. This proves the theorem.

**1.9.2 Remarks**:
**(a)**: It should be observed that Theorem 1.9.1 is 'symmetric in \(E_{1}\) and \(E_{2}\)', that is, the roles of \(E_{1}\) and \(E_{2}\) can be interchanged. This fact will be used throughout, usually without further mention.
**(b)**: Suppose that either \(u_{j}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{j})\), \(j=1,2\), and that \(u_{1}\) or \(u_{2}\) has compact support, or \(u_{1}\in{\cal S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u_{2}\in{\cal S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\). Then

\[u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}(\varphi)=u_{1}*_{\bullet}(u_{2}*\dot{\varphi})(0)=\big{<} u_{1}\bullet(\check{u}_{2}*\varphi)\big{>}_{\cal D}\] (1.9.10)

for \(\varphi\in{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\).

**Proof** We can assume that \(u_{2}\in{\cal E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\) in the first case. Then by Theorems 1.7.2 and 1.9.1 each one of the three expressions in (1.9.10) defines a separately continuous trilinear map

\[{\cal D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times{\cal E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n}, E_{2})\times{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\to E_{0}\]

in the first case, and

\[{\cal S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times{\cal S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2}) \times{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\to E_{0}\]

in the second one. Suppose that \(u_{j}=v_{j}\otimes e_{j}\in{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\times E_{j}\), \(j=1,2\). Then we deduce from (1.9.1), (1.2.11), and (1.2.13) that

\[u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}(\varphi) =(v_{1}\otimes e_{1})*_{\bullet}(v_{2}\otimes e_{2})(\varphi)=v_{ 1}*v_{2}(\varphi)(e_{1}\bullet e_{2})\] \[=v_{1}*(v_{2}*\dot{\varphi})(0)(e_{1}\bullet e_{2})=\big{[}v_{1}* (v_{2}*\dot{\varphi})\big{]}\otimes(e_{1}\bullet e_{2})(0)\] \[=u_{1}*_{\bullet}(u_{2}*\dot{\varphi})(0)\ .\]

Moreover, by these calculations, (1.2.11), Remark 1.2.4(c), and (1.7.4),

\[u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}(\varphi) =v_{1}*(v_{2}*\dot{\varphi})(0)(e_{1}\bullet e_{2})=\big{<}v_{1}, (v_{2}*\dot{\varphi})\char 1111\big{>}_{\cal D}(e_{1}\bullet e_{2})\] \[=\big{<}v_{1},\check{v}_{2}*\varphi)_{\cal D}(e_{1}\bullet e_{2}) =\big{<}(v_{1}\otimes e_{1})\bullet\big{[}(\check{v}_{2}*\varphi)\otimes e_{2 }\big{]}\big{>}_{\cal D}\] \[=\big{<}u_{1}\bullet(\check{u}_{2}*\varphi)\big{>}_{\cal D}\ .\]

Thus all three terms in (1.9.10) agree on the dense linear subspace

\[\big{(}{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\otimes E_{1}\big{)}\times\big{(}{\cal D}( \mathbb{R}^{n})\otimes E_{2}\big{)}\times{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\ ,\]

and so everywhere.

### \(L_{p}\)-Functions with Compact Supports

Given \(K\subset\subset X\), we put

\[L_{p,K}(X,E):=\big{\{}u\in L_{p}(X,E)\ ;\ \operatorname{supp}(u)\subset\overline{K} \,\big{\}}\ ,\qquad 1\leq p\leq\infty\ .\]

---

Then \(L_{p,K}(X,E)\) is a closed linear subspace of the Banach space \(L_{p}(X,E)\), hence a Banach space as well. Moreover,

\[\bigcup_{K\subset\subset X}L_{p,K}(X,E)=\big{\{}\,u\in L_{p}(X,E)\ ;\ \text{supp}(u)\subset\subset X\,\big{\}}\ .\]

Thus, recalling (1.1.5) and (1.1.6),

\[L_{p,c}(X,E):=\varinjlim_{K\subset\subset X}L_{p,K}(X,E)\ ,\qquad 1\leq p\leq\infty\ ,\] (1.9.11)

is a well-defined \(LF\)-space, the space of \(E\)-**valued \(L_{p}\)-functions with compact supports**. It is obvious that

\[L_{p,c}(X,E)\hookrightarrow L_{p}(X,E)\hookrightarrow L_{1,\text{loc}}(X,E) \hookrightarrow{\cal D}^{\prime}(X,E)\] (1.9.12)

for \(1\leq p\leq\infty\), and it is not difficult to see that

\[{\cal D}(X,E)\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}L_{p,c}(X,E)\ , \qquad 1\leq p<\infty\ ,\] (1.9.13)

by using Theorem 1.3.6(viii) with \(k=0\).

**Convolutions of Regular Distributions**

Our next proposition shows that the convolution product of regular distributions is again a regular distribution, if defined.

**1.9.3 Proposition** _Suppose that \(u_{1}\in L_{1,\text{loc}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u_{2}\in L_{1,c}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\). Then \(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\in L_{1,\text{loc}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\) and_

\[u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}(x)=\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}u_{1}(x-y)\bullet u_{2}(y)\,dy= \int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}u_{1}(y)\bullet u_{2}(x-y)\,dy\] (1.9.14)

_for a.a. \(x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\). Moreover, the convolution map \((u_{1},u_{2})\mapsto u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\) is bilinear and continuous_:

\[L_{1,\text{loc}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times L_{1,c}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2}) \to L_{1,\text{loc}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ .\] (1.9.15)

**Proof** We deduce from Theorem 1.9.1 and (1.9.12) that the convolution map is separately continuous from \(L_{1,\text{loc}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times L_{1,c}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\) into \({\cal D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\).

Suppose that \(K,K_{2}\subset\subset\mathbb{R}^{n}\). Then, letting \(K_{1}:=K-K_{2}\subset\subset\subset\mathbb{R}^{n}\), Tonelli's theorem implies

\[\int_{K}\Bigl{|}\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}u_{1}(x-y)\bullet u_{2}(y) \,dy\Bigr{|}\,dx \leq\int_{K}\int_{K_{2}}|u_{1}(x-y)|\,|u_{2}(y)|\,dy\,dx\] \[\leq\|u_{1}\|_{1,K_{1}}\,\|u_{2}\|_{1,K_{2}}\]

---

for \(u_{j}\in L_{1,\mathrm{loc}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{j})\) with \(\mathrm{supp}(u_{2})\subset K_{2}\). From this we deduce that

\[(u_{1},u_{2})\mapsto\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}u_{1}(\cdot-y)\bullet u_{2}(y)\,dy\]

is a bilinear and continuous map

\[L_{1,\mathrm{loc}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times L_{1,c}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E_{2}) \to L_{1,\mathrm{loc}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\hookrightarrow\mathcal{D}^{\prime }(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E_{0})\ .\]

By [1, (III.4.2.17)] and (1.9.1) we infer that both sides of (1.9.14) coincide on the linear subspace \(L_{1,\mathrm{loc}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times\bigl{(}\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{ n})\otimes E_{2}\bigr{)}\), which is dense by (1.9.13). Thus the assertion follows.

#### Tensor Products and Convolutions

Given the hypotheses of Proposition 1.9.3, we deduce from (1.9.14) by Fubini's theorem, a change of variables, and Remark 1.8.5(d) that

\[u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}(\varphi) =\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}\varphi(x)u_{1}(x-y) \bullet u_{2}(y)\,dy\,dx\] \[=\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}\times\mathbb{R}^{n}}\varphi(x+y)u_{1}(x) \bullet u_{2}(y)\,d(x,y)=u_{1}\otimes_{\bullet}u_{2}\bigl{(}\varphi(x_{1}+x_{2 })\bigr{)}\]

for \(\varphi\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), where in the last term we use our symbolic notation, meaning that \(u_{j}\) acts on the function following it as a variable of \(x_{j}\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\). The next theorem shows that this formula holds in general.

1.9.4 Theorem: _Suppose that one of the distributions \(u_{j}\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{j})\), \(j=1,2\), has compact support. Then_

\[u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}(\varphi)=u_{1}\otimes_{\bullet}u_{2}\bigl{(}\varphi(x _{1}+x_{2})\bigr{)}\ ,\qquad\varphi\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\ .\]

ProofBy Remark 1.9.2(b) and Fubini's rule for tensor products

\[u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}(\varphi) =\bigl{\langle}u_{1}\bullet(\tilde{u}_{2}\ast\varphi)\bigr{\rangle} _{\mathcal{D}}=\bigl{\langle}u_{1}\bullet\tilde{u}_{2}(\tau_{x_{1}}\varphi) \bigr{\rangle}_{\mathcal{D}}=\bigl{\langle}u_{1}\bullet u_{2}\bigl{(}\tau_{x_{ 1}}\varphi(-x_{2})\bigr{)}\bigr{\rangle}_{\mathcal{D}}\] \[=\bigl{\langle}u_{1}\bullet u_{2}\bigl{(}\varphi(x_{1}+x_{2}) \bigr{)}\bigr{\rangle}=u_{1}\otimes_{\bullet}u_{2}\bigl{(}\varphi(x_{1}+x_{2}) \bigr{)}\]

for \(\varphi\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\).

Theorem 1.9.4 enables us to define convolutions in certain cases where neither \(u_{1}\) nor \(u_{2}\) has compact support. For this we recall that a mapping between metric spaces is **proper**, if preimages of compact sets are compact. Given nonempty closed subsets \(A_{1},\ldots,A_{m}\) of \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\), we say \((A_{1},\ldots,A_{m})\) satisfies **condition** (\(\Sigma\)) if the map

\[A_{1}\times\cdots\times A_{m}\to\mathbb{R}^{n}\ ,\quad(x_{1},\ldots,x_{m}) \mapsto x_{1}+\cdots+x_{m}\]

is proper.

---

**1.9.5 Remarks** (a): A proper continuous mapping is **closed**, that is, it maps closed sets onto closed sets. Hence if \((A_{1},\ldots,A_{m})\) satisfies condition \((\Sigma)\) then the set \(A_{1}+\cdots+A_{m}\) is closed in \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\).
**(b)**: If \(A_{1},\ldots,A_{m}\) are closed in \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) and all but one are compact, then \((A_{1},\ldots,A_{m})\) satisfies condition \((\Sigma)\).
**(c)**: Let \(\Gamma\) be a closed convex proper cone in \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\). (A cone \(\Gamma\) is proper if it does not contain a full line, that is, if \(\Gamma\cap(-\Gamma)=\{0\}\).) Then the \(m\)-tuple \((\Gamma,\ldots,\Gamma)\) satisfies condition \((\Sigma)\). If \(\mathsf{H}:=\big{\{}\,y\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ ;\ \langle\eta,y\rangle\geq 0\, \big{\}}\) is a closed half-space with interior normal \(\eta\) lying in the interior of the dual cone \(\Gamma^{\prime}:=\big{\{}\,\xi\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ ;\ \langle\xi,x\rangle\geq 0 ,\ x\in\Gamma\,\big{\}}\), then the \(m\)-tuple \((\Gamma,\ldots,\Gamma,\mathsf{H})\) satisfies condition \((\Sigma)\). Note that \(\operatorname{int}(\Gamma^{\prime})\neq\emptyset\).

**Proof** First we assume that \(m=2\). Thus let \(\big{\{}(x_{j},y_{j})\big{\}}\) be a sequence in \(\Gamma\times\mathsf{B}\), where \(\mathsf{B}\in\{\Gamma,\mathsf{H}\}\), such that \(x_{j}+y_{j}\to z\) in \(\mathbb{R}^{m}\). Then, by the local compactness and the closedness of \(\Gamma\) and \(\mathsf{B}\), it suffices to prove that \((x_{j})\) and \((y_{j})\) are bounded sequences. If this is not the case, clearly both sequences have to be unbounded. Thus, by passing to a suitable subsequence we can assume that \(|x_{j}|\to\infty\) and \(x_{j}/|x_{j}|\to x\in\hat{\mathsf{I}}\). Hence \(y_{j}/|x_{j}|\to-x\in\hat{\mathsf{B}}\). If \(\mathsf{B}=\Gamma\), this is impossible since \(\Gamma\) is proper. If \(\mathsf{B}=\mathsf{H}\), it follows that \(0<\langle\eta,x\rangle\) since \(\eta\in\operatorname{int}(\Gamma^{\prime})\) and \(x\in\hat{\mathsf{I}}\), and \(\langle\eta,x\rangle\leq 0\) since \(-x\in\mathsf{H}\), which is again a contradiction. The general case is derived from an obvious induction argument.

In the following, the distributions \(u_{1},\ldots,u_{m}\) are said to have **convolutive supports** or to **satisfy condition**\((\Sigma)\) if their supports satisfy condition \((\Sigma)\).

Let \(u_{j}\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{j}),\ j=1,2\), satisfy condition \((\Sigma)\), and let \(X\) be a bounded open subset of \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\). Then there exists \(\rho:=\rho_{X}>0\) such that

\[\big{(}x_{j}\in\operatorname{supp}(u_{j})\ ,\ x_{1}+x_{2}\in X\big{)}\quad \Longrightarrow\quad\big{(}x_{j}\in\rho\mathbb{R}^{n}\ ,\ j=1,2\big{)}\ .\] (1.9.16)

Thus, given

\[\psi_{j}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\quad\text{with}\quad\psi_{j}\,|\, \rho\mathbb{B}^{n}\,=1\ ,\qquad j=1,2\ ,\] (1.9.17)

convolution \((\psi_{1}u_{1})\ast_{\bullet}(\psi_{2}u_{2})\) is well-defined, since \(\psi_{j}u_{j}\in\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{j})\). Moreover, for \(\chi_{j}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) with \(\chi_{j}\,|\rho\mathbb{B}^{n}=1\) it follows from Remarks 1.8.5(a) and (e), the fact that \(\psi_{j}-\chi_{j}\) vanish on \(\rho\mathbb{B}^{n}\), and from (1.9.16) that, given \(\varphi\in\mathcal{D}\),

\[\big{[}(\psi_{1}u_{1})\ast_{\bullet}(\psi_{2}u_{2})-(\chi_{1}u_{1 })\ast_{\bullet}(\chi_{2}u_{2})\big{]}(\varphi)\] \[\quad=\big{[}(\psi_{1}u_{1})\otimes_{\bullet}(\psi_{2}u_{2})-( \chi_{1}u_{1})\otimes_{\bullet}(\chi_{2}u_{2})\big{]}\big{(}\varphi(x_{1}+x_{2} )\big{)}\] \[\quad=\big{[}(\psi_{1}-\chi_{1})u_{1}\otimes_{\bullet}(\psi_{2}u_ {2})+(\chi_{1}u_{1})\otimes_{\bullet}(\psi_{2}-\chi_{2})u_{2}\big{]}\big{(} \varphi(x_{1}+x_{2})\big{)}\] \[\quad=\Big{\{}\big{[}(\psi_{1}-\chi_{1})\otimes\psi_{2}\big{]}(u_ {1}\otimes_{\bullet}u_{2})+\big{[}\chi_{1}\otimes(\psi_{2}-\chi_{2})\big{]}(u_ {1}\otimes_{\bullet}u_{2})\Big{\}}\big{(}\varphi(x_{1}+x_{2})\big{)}\] \[\quad=(u_{1}\otimes_{\bullet}u_{2})\Big{\{}\big{[}(\psi_{1}-\chi _{1})(x_{1})\psi_{2}(x_{2})+\chi_{1}(x_{1})(\psi_{2}-\chi_{2})(x_{2})\big{]} \varphi(x_{1}+x_{2})\Big{\}}=0\ .\]

---

This shows that \((\psi_{1}u_{1})*_{\bullet}(\psi_{2}u_{2})\) defines a distribution in \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X,E_{0})\), independently of the choice of the \(\psi_{j}\) satisfying (1.9.17). From this and the fact that a distribution is determined by its values on the open subsets of \({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\) (e.g., [Hor83, Theorem 2.2.4] and note that the proof carries over to the \(E\)-valued case) we see that

\[\left.\begin{array}{l}\mbox{given}\;\;u_{j}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^ {n},E_{j}),\;\;j=1,2,\;\mbox{having convolutive supports,}\\ \mbox{there exists a unique distribution $u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^ {n},E_{0})$, the $\mbox{\bf convolution of $u_{1}$ and $u_{2}$ with respect to multiplication (\ref{eq:1.5.20})}$,}\\ \mbox{satisfying}\\ \mbox{where $\psi_{j}$ satisfies (\ref{eq:1.9.17}) for $j=1,2$.}\end{array}\right\}\] (1.9.18)

#### Basic Properties

In the following remarks we collect some of the most important properties of convolutions.

**1.9.6 Remarks**: Unless explicit restrictions are given, we suppose that either \(u_{j}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{j})\) for \(j=1,2\), with convolutive supports, or that \(u_{1}\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u_{2}\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{2})\).
**(a)**: It is an obvious consequence of (1.9.18) and Remark 1.9.2(b) that

\[u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}(\varphi)=u_{1}*(u_{2}*\breve{\varphi})(0)=\big{\langle}u _{1}\bullet(\breve{u}_{2}*\varphi)\big{\rangle}_{\cal D}\]

for \(\varphi\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\).
**(b)**: If \(u_{j}\in L_{1,{\rm loc}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{j})\) satisfy condition \((\Sigma)\) then \(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\in L_{1,{\rm loc}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{0})\) and

\[u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}(x)=\int_{{\mathbb{R}}^{n}}\!\!u_{1}(x-y)\bullet u_{2}(y) \,dy=\int_{{\mathbb{R}}^{n}}\!\!u_{1}(y)\bullet u_{2}(x-y)\,dy\ ,\quad\mbox{ a.a. $x\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}$ }\ .\]

ProofThanks to (1.9.18) we can assume that \(u_{j}\) has compact support for \(j=1,2\). Then the assertion follows from Proposition 1.9.3.
**(c)**: _Associativity_ Let the associativity hypotheses of Remark 1.6.5(e) be satisfied and suppose that either \(u_{j}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{j})\), \(j=1,2,3\), have convolutive supports, or \(u_{1}\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u_{k}\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{k})\), \(k=2,3\). Then

\[u_{1}*_{\bullet}(u_{2}*_{\bullet}u_{3})=(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2})*_{\bullet}u_{3 }\ .\] (1.9.19)

ProofIf condition \((\Sigma)\) is satisfied,2 it suffices, by (1.9.18), to prove the assertion for \(u_{j}\in{\cal E}^{\prime}(X,E_{j})\), \(j=1,2,3\). Then Theorem 1.9.1 implies that both sides of

---

(1.9.19) define separately continuous trilinear maps

\[{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{1})\times{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}, E_{2})\times{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{3})\to{\cal E}^{\prime}({ \mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{0})\]

if condition \((\Sigma)\) is satisfied, and

\[{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{1})\times{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{2} )\times{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{3})\to{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{0})\]

otherwise. Thanks to (1.9.1) and (1.2.13) they coincide on the dense linear subspace \(\prod_{j=1}^{3}{\cal D}(X)\otimes E_{j}\). Thus they are equal.

**(d)**_Commutativity_ Suppose that \(E_{1}=E_{2}=:E\) and multiplication (1.5.20) is symmetric. Then \(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}=u_{2}*_{\bullet}u_{1}\).

**Proof** By (1.9.18) we can assume that \(u_{j}\in{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) if condition \((\Sigma)\) is satisfied. Thus, by Theorem 1.9.1 and the density of \({\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) in \({\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) and in \({\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) and \({\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\), respectively, we can assume that \(u_{j}\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\). Now the assertion follows from (b).

**(e)**_Distributivity_ Let the distributivity hypotheses of Remark 1.8.5(e) be satisfied and suppose that \(u_{j}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{j})\) as well as \(v_{j}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{j+2})\), \(j=1,2\), have convolutive supports. Then

\[(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2})\otimes_{\bullet}(v_{1}*_{\bullet}v_{2})=(u_{1}\otimes_ {\bullet}v_{1})*_{\bullet}(u_{2}\otimes_{\bullet}v_{2})\ .\] (1.9.20)

**Proof** First we show that \(u_{j}\otimes_{\bullet}v_{j}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{ \mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{j+5})\) satisfy condition \((\Sigma)\). By Remark 1.8.5(a)

\[{\rm supp}(u_{j}\otimes_{\bullet}v_{j})\subset{\rm supp}(u_{j})\times{\rm supp }(v_{j})\ ,\qquad j=1,2\ .\] (1.9.21)

Let \(X\subset{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\) be bounded and open. Then there exists \(\rho>0\) such that \(x_{j}\in{\rm supp}(u_{j})\) and \(y_{j}\in{\rm supp}(v_{j})\) with \(x_{1}+x_{2}\) and \(y_{1}+y_{2}\) belonging to \(X\) imply \(x_{j},y_{j}\in\rho{\mathbb{B}}^{n}\). Thus, if \((x_{1},y_{1})+(x_{2},y_{2})\in X\times X\), it follows from (1.9.21) that

\[(x_{j},y_{j})\in\rho{\mathbb{B}}^{n}\times\rho{\mathbb{B}}^{n}\subset\rho_{1}{ \mathbb{B}}^{2n}\ ,\qquad j=1,2\ ,\]

for some \(\rho_{1}>0\). Since every bounded open subset of \({\mathbb{R}}^{2n}\) is contained in a product set \(X\times X\), we see that \(u_{j}\otimes v_{j}\) satisfy condition \((\Sigma)\). Hence both sides of (1.9.20) are well-defined.

Given \(\psi_{j},\psi_{j+2}\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\), it follows from Remark 1.8.5(e) that

\[(\psi_{j}u_{j})\otimes_{\bullet}v_{j}(\psi_{j+2}v_{j})=(\psi_{j}\otimes\psi_{j +2})(u_{j}\otimes_{\bullet}v_{j})\ ,\qquad j=1,2\ .\]

Using this and (1.9.18) it is easily seen that it suffices to prove the assertion for \(u_{j}\in{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E_{j})\) and \(v_{j}\in{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E_{j+2})\), \(j=1,2\). Now Theorems 1.8.4 and 1.9.1 together with a density argument show that we can assume that \(u_{j}\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E_{j})\)

---

and \(v_{j}\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{j+2})\). Thus, by (b) and Remark 1.8.5(d), the left-hand side of (1.9.20) equals

\[\int_{{\mathbb{R}}^{n}}u_{1}(x_{1}-y_{1})\bullet u_{2}(y_{1})\,dy_{1}\bullet\int _{{\mathbb{R}}^{n}}v_{1}(x_{2}-y_{2})\bullet v_{2}(y_{2})\,dy_{2}\ ,\quad(x_{1},x_{2}) \in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\ .\]

By Fubini's theorem and (1.8.12) this product takes the form

\[\int_{{\mathbb{R}}^{2n}}\bigl{(}u_{1}(x_{1}-y_{1})\bullet v_{1}(x_{2}-y_{2}) \bigr{)}\bullet\bigl{(}u_{2}(y_{1})\bullet v_{2}(y_{2})\bigr{)}\,d(y_{1},y_{2 })\ ,\]

which is the right-hand side of (1.9.20).

**(f)**: _Support Theorem_ Suppose that \(u_{j}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{j}),\ \ j=1,2\), have convolutive supports. Then \(\mathop{\rm supp}\nolimits(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2})\subset\mathop{\rm supp} \nolimits(u_{1})+\mathop{\rm supp}\nolimits(u_{2})\).

**Proof** From Remark 1.9.5(a) we know that \(C:=\mathop{\rm supp}\nolimits(u_{1})+\mathop{\rm supp}\nolimits(u_{2})\) is closed in \({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\). Let \(\varphi\in{\cal D}(C^{c})\). Then the support of the function \((x_{1},x_{2})\mapsto\varphi(x_{1}+x_{2})\) does not meet \(\mathop{\rm supp}\nolimits(u_{1})\times\mathop{\rm supp}\nolimits(u_{2})\), hence not \(\mathop{\rm supp}\nolimits(u_{1}\otimes_{\bullet}u_{2})\) by Remark 1.8.5(a). Thus \((\psi_{1}u_{1})*_{\bullet}(\psi_{2}u_{2})(\varphi)=(\psi_{1}u_{1})\otimes_{ \bullet}(\psi_{2}u_{2})\bigl{(}\varphi(x_{1}+x_{2})\bigr{)}=0\) for all \(\psi_{j}\in{\cal D}(X)\), \(j=1,2\). Consequently, (1.9.18) implies \(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}(\varphi)=0\), that is, the assertion.

**(g)**: \(\partial^{\alpha+\beta}(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2})=\partial^{\alpha}u_{1}*_{ \bullet}\partial^{\beta}u_{2},\ \ \alpha,\beta\in{\mathbb{N}}^{n}\).

**Proof** First suppose that \(u_{1}\) and \(u_{2}\) satisfy condition (\(\Sigma\)). Since

\[\mathop{\rm supp}\nolimits(\partial^{\gamma}u)\subset\mathop{\rm supp} \nolimits(u)\ ,\qquad u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ ,\quad\gamma\in{ \mathbb{N}}^{n}\ ,\]

it follows that \(\partial^{\alpha}u_{1}\) and \(\partial^{\beta}u_{2}\) satisfy condition (\(\Sigma\)). Hence \(\partial^{\alpha}u_{1}*_{\bullet}\partial^{\beta}u_{2}\) is well-defined. Given \(\varphi\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\),

\[\partial^{\alpha+\beta}(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2})(\varphi)=(-1)^{|\alpha|+|\beta |}(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2})(\partial^{\alpha+\beta}\varphi)\ .\]

Let \(X\) be a bounded open subset of \({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\) containing \(\mathop{\rm supp}\nolimits(\varphi)\) and choose \(\psi_{j}\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) with \(\psi_{j}\,|\,\rho_{X}{\mathbb{R}}^{n}=1\). Then

\[(-1)^{|\alpha|+|\beta|}(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2})(\partial^{\alpha+ \beta}\varphi) =(-1)^{|\alpha|+|\beta|}(\psi_{1}u_{1})*_{\bullet}(\psi_{2}u_{2}) (\partial^{\alpha+\beta}\varphi)\] \[=(-1)^{|\alpha|+|\beta|}(\psi_{1}u_{1})\otimes_{\bullet}(\psi_{2} u_{2})\bigl{(}\partial^{\alpha+\beta}\varphi(x_{1}+x_{2})\bigr{)}\] \[=\partial^{\alpha}(\psi_{1}u_{1})\otimes_{\bullet}\partial^{\beta }(\psi_{2}u_{2})\bigl{(}\varphi(x_{1}+x_{2})\bigr{)}\] \[=(\psi_{1}\partial^{\alpha}u_{1})\otimes_{\bullet}(\psi_{2} \partial^{\beta}u_{2})\bigl{(}\varphi(x_{1}+x_{2})\bigr{)}\] \[=(\psi_{1}\partial^{\alpha}u_{1})*_{\bullet}(\psi_{2}\partial^{ \beta}u_{2})(\varphi)\] \[=\partial^{\alpha}u_{1}*_{\bullet}\partial^{\beta}u_{2}(\varphi)\ ,\]

thanks to Remark 1.8.5(b), Leibniz' rule, and the properties of \(\psi_{j}\).

If \(u_{1}\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u_{2}\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{2})\), we can restrict ourselves as usual to the case \(u_{j}\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\otimes E_{j}\). Then the assertion follows from Remark 1.2.4(b).

---

**(h)**: \(\tau_{a}(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2})=(\tau_{a}u_{1})*_{\bullet}u_{2}=u_{1}*_{\bullet}\tau_ {a}u_{2}\), \(a\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\), and

\[(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2})\,\tilde{\phantom{a}}=\tilde{u}_{1}*_{\bullet}\tilde{u}_ {2}\ .\]

ProofIt is easily verified that these convolutions are all well-defined. If \(u_{1}\) and \(u_{2}\) satisfy condition \((\Sigma)\), we deduce from (1.9.18) that we can assume that \(u_{1}\) and \(u_{2}\) have compact supports. Thus in either case it suffices by Theorem 1.9.1 to consider the case where \(u_{j}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\otimes E_{j}\). Now the assertion follows from Remark 1.2.4(c).
**(i)**: Suppose that \(a\in E_{1}\) and \(u\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\). Then

\[\partial^{\alpha}\big{[}(\delta\otimes a)*_{\bullet}u\big{]}=(\partial^{\alpha }\delta\otimes a)*_{\bullet}u=a\bullet\partial^{\alpha}u=\partial^{\alpha}(a \bullet u)\]

for \(\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\).

ProofSince \(\partial^{\beta}\delta\otimes a\in\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\), we see that the above convolutions are well-defined. Thanks to (g) and Remark 1.6.5(f) we can assume that \(\alpha=0\). By Theorems 1.6.4 and 1.9.1 it suffices to consider \(u\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\times E_{2}\). Then the assertion follows from (1.9.1) and Remark 1.2.4(a).

### Convolution Algebras

Given a nonempty subset \(K\) of \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\), we put

\[\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{K}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E):=\big{\{}\,u\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime} (\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ;\ \operatorname{supp}(u)\subset\overline{K}\,\big{\}}\] (1.9.22)

and, as usual, \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{K}(\mathbb{R}^{n}):=\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{K}(\mathbb{R}^ {n},\mathbb{K})\), if no confusion seems likely. We also set

\[\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E):=\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{\mathbb{R}^{+}}(\mathbb{R} ,E)\ .\] (1.9.23)

Note that \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{K}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) is a closed linear subspace of \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\).

In the following theorem we collect some of the properties of convolutions in a particularly important setting.

**1.9.7 Theorem**: _Let \(\Gamma\) be a proper closed convex cone in \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) and let_

\[\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{D},\mathcal{S},\mathcal{E}^{\prime},\mathcal{D}^{ \prime}_{\Gamma}\}\ .\]

_Then convolution is a well-defined hypocontinuous bilinear map_

\[\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2}) \rightarrow\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ ,\quad(u_{1},u_{2})\mapsto u_{1}*_{ \bullet}u_{2}\]

_which possesses the associativity and commutativity properties of Remarks 1.9.6(c) and (d), respectively. In particular, if \((E,\bullet)\) is a \([\)commutative\(]\) Banach algebra then \(\big{(}\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E),*_{\bullet}\big{)}\) is a \([\)commutative\(]\) algebra, a \(\mathbf{convolution}\) algebra. If \((E,\bullet)\) has a unit \(e_{0}\) and \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{E}^{\prime},\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{\Gamma}\}\), then \(\big{(}\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E),*_{\bullet}\big{)}\) has a unit as well, namely \(\delta\otimes e_{0}\)._

---

ProofEverything, except the hypocontinuity of the convolution map if \(\mathfrak{F}=\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{\Gamma}\), follows easily from Theorem 1.9.1, the injection \(\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\hookrightarrow\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R }^{n},E_{2})\), and Remarks 1.9.5(c), 1.9.6(f), and 1.2.4(a). Recall that \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{\Gamma}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) is a closed linear subspace of \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\). Thus, if \((u_{\alpha})\) is a net in \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{\Gamma}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\) converging to zero, it converges to zero in \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\). Let \(V\) be a bounded subset of \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{\Gamma}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\), hence of \(\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\). Then, given any bounded subset \(B\) of \(\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), there exists \(K:=K_{B}\subset\subset\mathbb{R}^{n}\) such that \(\mathrm{supp}(\varphi)\subset\overline{K}\) for \(\varphi\in B\). Hence condition \((\Sigma)\) guarantees the existence of \(\rho>0\) such that \(x,y\in\Gamma\) with \(x+y\subset\overline{K}\) implies \(x,y\in\rho\mathbb{B}^{n}\). Thus, letting \(\psi_{j}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) satisfy \(\psi\,|\,\rho\mathbb{B}^{n}=1\), it follows from (1.9.18) that

\[u_{\alpha}\ast_{\bullet}v(\varphi)=(\psi_{1}u_{\alpha})\ast_{\bullet}(\psi_{2} v)(\varphi)\ ,\qquad\varphi\in B\ ,\quad v\in V\ .\] (1.9.24)

Note that \(\psi_{2}v(\chi)=v(\psi_{2}\chi)\) and \(\psi_{2}\chi\in\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{supp}(\psi_{2})}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) for \(\chi\in\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) imply that \(\{\,\psi_{2}v\;;\;v\in V\;\}\) is bounded in \(\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\). Now the hypocontinuity of the map (1.9.2) and (1.9.24) entail \(u_{\alpha}\ast_{\bullet}v(\varphi)\to 0\) in \(E_{0}\), uniformly with respect to \(v\in V\) and \(\varphi\in B\). Consequently, \(u_{\alpha}\ast_{\bullet}v\to 0\) in \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\), hence in \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{\Gamma}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\), uniformly with respect to \(v\) in bounded subsets of \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{\Gamma}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\). This proves, by symmetry, the asserted hypocontinuity.

**1.9.8 Remarks**:
**(a)**: Let \(\Gamma\) be a proper, closed, and convex cone in \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\). Then \(\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), \(\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), and \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{\Gamma}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) are commutative convolution algebras. Moreover, \(\mathcal{E}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) and \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{\Gamma}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) possess the unit \(\delta\).
**(b)**: Since \(\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) are barreled, the convolution product is, in fact, continuous on these spaces.
**(c)**: Suppose that \(u_{j}\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E_{j})\) are regular distributions. Then \(u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E_{0})\) is a regular distribution as well, and

\[u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}(t)=\int_{0}^{t}u_{1}(t-s)\bullet u_{2}(s)\,ds\ , \qquad\text{a.a. }t\in\mathbb{R}^{+}\ .\]

ProofThis is an easy consequence of Remarks 1.9.6(b) and (f).

### Convolutions of Bounded and Integrable Functions

For the reader's convenience and for later use we now collect the properties of convolution on certain Banach spaces of vector-valued convolutions, thus extending, in particular, assertions [Ama95, (III.4.2.19)-(III.4.2.22)] (note that in that book (III.4.2.18) is a special case of (III.4.2.21)).

**1.9.9 Theorem**: _Suppose that \((\mathfrak{F}_{1},\mathfrak{F}_{2};\mathfrak{F}_{0})\) is any one of the triplets_

\[(BUC,L_{1};BUC),(C_{0},L_{1};C_{0}),(L_{p},L_{1};L_{p}),(L_{\infty},L_{1};BUC), (L_{q},L_{q^{\prime}};C_{0})\ ,\]

---

_where \(1\leq p<\infty\) and \(1<q<\infty\). Then convolution with respect to multiplication (1.5.20) extends from \(\mathfrak{F}_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\) to a multiplication_

\[\mathfrak{F}_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times\mathfrak{F}_{2}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{ 2})\to\mathfrak{F}_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ .\]

_It is given by_

\[u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}(x)=\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}\!\!u_{1}(x-y)\bullet u_{2}(y)\, dy=\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}\!\!u_{1}(y)\bullet u_{2}(x-y)\,dy\ ,\ \ \ \ \mbox{a .a. }x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ .\]

Proof.: Since \(\mathfrak{F}_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\hookrightarrow L_{1,\mathrm{loc}}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\cap\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\), we see from (1.9.3) that

\[u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\in\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ ,\qquad u_{1} \in\mathfrak{F}_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\ ,\quad u_{2}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n} ,E_{2})\ ,\]

and Proposition 1.9.3 guarantees that \(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\) is given by the above integral in this case. Since \(\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\) is dense in \(\mathfrak{F}_{2}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\), it suffices to show that \(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{1}\in\mathfrak{F}_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\) and that the estimate

\[\|u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\|_{\mathfrak{F}_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})}\leq\|u_{1} \|_{\mathfrak{F}_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})}\,\|u_{2}\|_{\mathfrak{F}_{2}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})}\] (1.9.25)

is valid for \(u_{1}\in\mathfrak{F}_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u_{2}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\). Then the assertion follows by continuous extension.

If \(u_{1}\in L_{r}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\), \(1\leq r\leq\infty\), and \(u_{2}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\), it follows from

\[|u_{1}*u_{2}(x)|\leq\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}|u_{1}(x-y)|\,|u_{2}(y)|\,dy\ ,\qquad x \in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ ,\]

and the classical scalar Young inequality that

\[\|u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\|_{L_{r}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})}\leq\|u_{1}\|_{L_{r}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})}\,\|u_{2}\|_{L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})}\] (1.9.26)

and

\[\|u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\|_{L_{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})}\leq\|u_{1}\|_{L_{ r}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})}\,\|u_{2}\|_{L_{r^{\prime}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})}\] (1.9.27)

(cf. [Ama95, (III.4.2.20)-(III.4.2.22)]). If \(u_{j}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{j})\) for \(j=1,2\), we know from (1.9.7) that \(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\). Since \(\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\) is dense in \(\mathfrak{F}_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\) if \(\mathfrak{F}_{1}\) belongs to \(\{C_{0},L_{p},L_{q}\}\) and since \(C_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\) is a closed linear subspace of \(L_{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\) containing \(\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\), we infer from (1.9.26) and (1.9.27) that the assertion is true for \(\mathfrak{F}_{1}\in\{C_{0},L_{p},L_{q}\}\).

If \(u_{1}\in BUC(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u_{2}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\) then, given \(\varepsilon>0\), there exists \(\delta>0\) such that \(|u_{1}(x)-u_{1}(y)|\leq\varepsilon\) for \(x,y\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\) with \(|x-y|\leq\delta\). Hence

\[|u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}(x)-u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}(y)|\leq\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}|u _{1}(x-z)-u_{1}(y-z)|\,|u_{2}(z)|\,dz\leq\varepsilon\,\|u_{2}\|_{1}\]

for \(x,y\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\) with \(|x-y|\leq\delta\). This shows that \(u_{1}*_{\bullet}u_{2}\in BUC(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\) in this case. Hence the assertion follows for \(\mathfrak{F}_{1}=BUC\) from (1.9.26) and the fact that \(BUC(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\) is a closed linear subspace of \(L_{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\).

---

Finally, suppose that \(u_{1}\in L_{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u_{2}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\). Then we infer from Remark 1.9.6(h) and (1.9.26) that

\[\|\tau_{a}(u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2})-u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}\|_{\infty}=\|u_{1 }\ast_{\bullet}(\tau_{a}u_{2}-u_{2})\|_{\infty}\leq\|u_{1}\|_{\infty}\,\|\tau_{a }u_{2}-u_{2}\|_{1}\]

for \(a\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\). Since the translation group is strongly continuous on \(L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\), it follows that \(u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}\in\mbox{\it BUC}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\) in this case. Thus the assertion holds for \(\mathfrak{F}_{1}=L_{\infty}\) as well.

Given the hypotheses of Theorem 1.9.9, inequality (1.9.25), that holds for all \(u_{j}\in\mathfrak{F}_{j}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{j})\), \(j=1,2\), is again called **Young's inequality**.

#### The Convolution Theorem

Lastly, we extend the convolution theorem to vector-valued distributions.

**1.9.10 Theorem**: _If \(u_{1}\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u_{2}\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\) then \((u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2})^{\widehat{\phantom{\bullet}}}=\widehat{u}_{1}\bullet \widehat{u}_{2}\)._

**Proof** Since \(\mathcal{F}\) is a toplinear automorphism of \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and of \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\), it follows from Theorems 1.6.4 and 1.9.1, together with

\[\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\hookrightarrow\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E) \hookrightarrow\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\]

that the bilinear maps \((u_{1},u_{2})\mapsto(u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2})^{\widehat{\phantom{\bullet}}}\) and \((u_{1},u_{2})\mapsto\widehat{u}_{1}\bullet\widehat{u}_{2}\) are well-defined and hypocontinuous from \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_ {2})\) into \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\). Thanks to

\[(u\otimes e)^{\widehat{\phantom{\bullet}}}=\widehat{u}\otimes e\ ,\qquad u \otimes e\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\otimes E\ ,\]

we see from (1.6.10), (1.9.1), and the scalar convolution theorem that they coincide on the dense linear subspace \(\big{(}\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\otimes E_{1}\big{)}\times\big{(} \mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\otimes E_{2}\big{)}\), hence everywhere.

**1.9.11 Remarks**: **(a)**: Let

\[\mathcal{O}^{\prime}_{C}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E):=\mathcal{F}^{-1}\mathcal{O}_{M}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E):=\big{\{}u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ;\ \mathcal{F}u\in\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\,\big{\}}\ ,\]

endowed with the unique locally convex topology such that

\[\mathcal{F}\in\mathcal{L}\mbox{is}\big{(}\mathcal{O}^{\prime}_{C}(\mathbb{R}^ {n},E),\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\ .\]

Then it follows from Theorem 1.9.10, the continuity properties of \(\mathcal{F}\), and Theorem 1.6.4 that we can define a hypocontinuous bilinear map

\[\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times\mathcal{O}^{\prime}_{C}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\to\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ ,\quad(u_{1},u_{2})\mapsto u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}\ ,\] (1.9.28)

the **convolution with respect to multiplication** (1.5.20), by putting

\[u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}:=\mathcal{F}^{-1}(\widehat{u}_{1}\bullet\widehat{u}_ {2})\ ,\qquad u_{1}\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\ ,\quad u_{2}\in\mathcal{O}^{\prime}_{C}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2})\ .\] (1.9.29)

We leave it to the interested reader to carry over the properties of Remarks 1.9.6 to this more general case.

---

**(b)**: Given \(a\in{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{1})\), we put

\[a(D)u:={\cal F}^{-1}a_{\bullet}{\cal F}u:={\cal F}^{-1}(a\bullet\widehat{u})\ , \qquad u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{2})\ .\]

Then it follows from the isomorphism properties of the Fourier transform and from Theorem 1.6.4 that

\[a(D)\in{\cal L}\big{(}{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{2}),{\cal S}^{ \prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{0})\big{)}\ .\]

Moreover,

\[a(D)u:={\cal F}^{-1}(a)*_{\bullet}u\ ,\qquad u\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{2} )\ ,\]

thanks to the Convolution Theorem 1.9.10. Motivated by these facts we put

\[a(D)u:={\cal F}^{-1}(a_{\bullet}{\cal F})u:={\cal F}^{-1}(a)*_{\bullet}u\] (1.9.30)

whenever \(a\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{1})\) and \(u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{2})\) are such that the convolution product on the right-hand side is well-defined. In these general situations \(a(D)\) is said to be a **translation-invariant** (**pseudodifferential**) **operator** with **symbol**\(a\) (related to multiplication (1.5.20)). Of course, this is motivated by Theorem 1.2.2. Note that

\[a(D)\in{\cal L}\big{(}{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{2}),{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{ n},E_{0})\big{)}\cap{\cal L}\big{(}{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{2}),{ \cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{0})\big{)}\] (1.9.31)

if \(a\in{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{1})\).

ConventionIn the remainder of this work the multiplications which occur belong most of the time to one of the classes given in Examples 1.3.1(a)-(e). If this is the case, we usually omit the symbol \(\bullet\) everywhere, e.g., we simply write \(*\) for \(*_{\bullet}\) , etc. Note, for instance, that, if \(\bullet\) is the multiplication of Example 1.3.1(b), then (1.9.31) reduces to

\[a(D)\in{\cal L}\big{(}{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\big{)}\cap{\cal L}\big{(}{ \cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\big{)}\ ,\]

an assertion that has already been given in Chapter III. Similarly, using this particular multiplication, many of the results of the present subsection reduce to assertions given, and having been used already, in [10]. Nevertheless, even in this simpler situation we shall refer to the theorems and results formulated and proven in the present subsection since this is the place where all essential properties have been collected.

### Laplace Transforms

In this subsection we restrict ourselves to the consideration of distributions in one variable, that is, on \({\mathbb{R}}\) only, although most of the results below have extensions to distributions on \({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\). However, the one-dimensional case is the most important one and the only one we shall make use of.

---

**Definition of Laplace Transforms**

For abbreviation we put

\[e_{z}:=(x\mapsto e^{-zx})\in{\cal E}({\mathbb{R}})\ ,\qquad z\in{\mathbb{C}}\ .\]

Then, given \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\), we set

\[I_{u}:=\big{\{}\,\xi\in{\mathbb{R}}\ ;\ e_{\xi}u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}} ,E)\,\big{\}}\ .\]

Of course, \(I_{u}\) may be empty, as is the case for \(u\in L_{1,{\rm loc}}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) with \(u(x)=e^{x^{2}}u_{0}\) for some nonzero \(u_{0}\in E\). Moreover, \(0\in I_{u}\) iff \(u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\). The following lemma shows that \(I_{u}\) is convex.

**1.10.1 Lemma**: \(I_{u}\) _is an interval._

**Proof** For \(\xi_{0},\xi_{1}\in I_{u}\) and \(t\in[0,1]\) put \(\xi:=(1-t)\xi_{0}+t\xi_{1}\). Since

\[e_{\xi}=(e_{\xi_{0}})^{1-t}(e_{\xi_{1}})^{t}\leq e_{\xi_{0}}+e_{\xi_{1}}\]

it is easily verified that \(\chi:=e_{\xi}\big{/}(e_{\xi_{0}}+e_{\xi_{1}})\in BC^{\infty}({\mathbb{R}}) \hookrightarrow{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}})\).Consequently, \(e_{\xi}u=\chi e_{\xi_{0}}u+\chi e_{\xi_{1}}u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}} ,E)\), which proves the assertion.

The next lemma gives more detailed information on \(I_{u}\) if the support of \(u\) is limited to the left.

**1.10.2 Lemma**: _Suppose that \(\inf\mbox{supp}(u)>-\infty\). Then \(\sup I_{u}=\infty\) if \(I_{u}\neq\emptyset\)._

**Proof** Let \(\mbox{supp}(u)\subset[\alpha,\infty)\) for some \(\alpha\in{\mathbb{R}}\). Fix \(\xi_{0}\in I_{u}\) and \(\psi\in{\cal E}({\mathbb{R}})\) with \(\psi\,|\,[\alpha-1,\infty)=1\) and \(\psi\,|\,(-\infty,\alpha-2]=0\). Then, given \(\xi>\xi_{0}\),

\[a(\xi):=e_{\xi-\xi_{0}}\psi\in BC^{\infty}({\mathbb{R}})\hookrightarrow{\cal O }_{M}({\mathbb{R}})\]

and, consequently, \(e_{\xi}u=a(\xi)e_{\xi_{0}}u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\). Hence \(\xi\in I_{u}\) for \(\xi>\xi_{0}\).

**1.10.3 Remarks** (a) If \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\) and \(\xi_{0}\in I_{u}\) then \([\xi_{0},\infty)\subset I_{u}\). In particular, \({\mathbb{R}}^{+}\subset I_{u}\) if \(u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\cap{\cal D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\).

**Proof** This is an obvious consequence of Lemma 1.10.2.

**(b) If \(u\in{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) then \(I_{u}={\mathbb{R}}\).

**Proof** Since \(e_{\xi}\in{\cal E}({\mathbb{R}})\) for \(\xi\in{\mathbb{R}}\) we know from Lemma 1.6.3 that \(e_{\xi}u\in{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) for \(u\in{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\). Hence the assertion follows from \({\cal E}^{\prime}\hookrightarrow{\cal S}^{\prime}\).

Next we prove the basic technical lemma that will allow us to define Laplace transformations for certain distributions.

---

**1.10.4 Lemma** _Suppose that \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\). Then \(\widehat{e_{\xi}u}\in{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) for \(\xi\in\dot{\hat{I}}_{u}\), and the map_

\[\dot{\hat{I}}_{u}\to{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}},E)\ ,\quad\xi\mapsto\widehat{e_{ \xi}u}\]

_is smooth._

**Proof** Fix \(\xi_{0},\xi_{1}\in\dot{\hat{I}}_{u}\) with \(\xi_{0}<\xi_{1}\), and \(\varphi_{0}\in{\cal E}({\mathbb{R}})\) with \(0\leq\varphi_{0}\leq 1\) such that \(\varphi_{0}\,|\,{\mathbb{R}}^{+}=1\) and \(\varphi_{0}(x)=0\) for \(x\leq-1\). Also put \(\varphi_{1}:=1-\varphi_{0}\). Then

\[e_{\xi}u=e_{\xi-\xi_{0}}\varphi_{0}e_{\xi_{0}}u+e_{\xi-\xi_{1}}\varphi_{1}e_{ \xi_{1}}u\ ,\qquad\xi\in{\mathbb{R}}\ .\]

It is easily verified that

\[a_{j}:=(\xi\mapsto e_{\xi-\xi_{j}}\varphi_{j})\in C^{\infty}\bigl{(}(\xi_{0}, \xi_{1}),{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}})\bigr{)}\ ,\qquad j=0,1\ .\]

Hence

\[\widehat{a}_{j}\in C^{\infty}\bigl{(}(\xi_{0},\xi_{1}),{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}) \bigr{)}\ ,\qquad j=0,1\ ,\]

since \({\cal F}\in{\cal L}{\rm aut}\bigl{(}{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}})\bigr{)}\). From (1.10.1) and the convolution theorem we infer that

\[\widehat{e_{\xi}u}=\widehat{a_{0}e_{\xi_{0}}u}+\widehat{a_{1}e_{\xi_{1}}u}=(2 \pi)^{n}\bigl{(}\widehat{a}_{0}*\widehat{e_{\xi_{0}}u}+\widehat{a}_{1}* \widehat{e_{\xi_{1}}u}\bigr{)}\]

for \(\xi\in(\xi_{0},\xi_{1})\), thanks to \(\widehat{u}=(2\pi)^{n}\tilde{u}\) and Remark 1.9.6(h). Since \({\cal F}\) is a continuous automorphism of \({\cal S}\) and of \({\cal S}^{\prime}\) we infer from Proposition 1.2.7 that

\[(\varphi\mapsto\widehat{\varphi}*\widehat{v})\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal S}({ \mathbb{R}}),{\cal O}_{M}\left({\mathbb{R}},E\right)\bigr{)}\ ,\qquad v\in{\cal S }^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\ .\]

Now the assertion is a consequence of (1.10.2) and (1.10.3).

Suppose that \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) and \(\dot{\hat{I}}_{u}\neq\emptyset\). Then \(u\) is said to be **Laplace transformable** and the \(E\)-valued function

\[\widetilde{u}:\ \dot{\hat{I}}_{u}+i\,{\mathbb{R}}\to E\ ,\quad\zeta:=\xi+i\, \eta\mapsto\widetilde{u}(\zeta):=\widehat{e_{\xi}u}(\eta)\]

is the **Laplace transform** (or **transformation**) of \(u\). Note that \(\widetilde{u}\) is well-defined by Lemma 1.10.4.

**Weakly Holomorphic Functions**

Now we prove some facts about \(E\)-valued holomorphic functions that we need to derive further properties of Laplace transforms.

Recall that a function \(f:\,X\to E\), where \(X\) is an open subset of \({\mathbb{C}}\) (and \(E\) is a complex Banach space) is holomorphic iff it is (complex) differentiable. It is **weakly holomorphic** if \(e^{\prime}\circ f:\,X\to{\mathbb{C}}\) is holomorphic for each \(e^{\prime}\in E^{\prime}\).

**1.10.5 Proposition** _Let \(X\) be open in \({\mathbb{C}}\). Then \(f\in E^{X}\) is holomorphic iff it is weakly holomorphic._

---

**Proof** Suppose that \(f\) is weakly holomorphic. Let \(z_{0}\in X\) be given and choose \(r>0\) such that \(\bar{\mathbb{R}}_{\mathbb{C}}(z_{0},2r)\subset X\). Given \(e^{\prime}\in E^{\prime}\), Cauchy's integral formula implies

\[(\alpha-\beta)^{-1}\Big{[}\frac{e^{\prime}\circ f(z_{0}+\alpha)-e^ {\prime}\circ f(z_{0})}{\alpha}-\frac{e^{\prime}\circ f(z_{0}+\beta)-e^{\prime }\circ f(z_{0})}{\beta}\Big{]}\] \[=\frac{1}{2\pi i}\int_{|z-z_{0}|=2r}\frac{e^{\prime}\circ f(z)\,dz} {(z-z_{0}-\alpha)(z-z_{0}-\beta)(z-z_{0})}\]

for \(\alpha,\beta\in r\dot{\mathbb{B}}_{\mathbb{C}}\) with \(\alpha\neq\beta\). Since the integral is uniformly bounded for these \(\alpha\) and \(\beta\), the uniform boundedness principle implies the existence of a constant \(c\) such that

\[\Big{|}\frac{f(z_{0}+\alpha)-f(z_{0})}{\alpha}-\frac{f(z_{0}+\beta)-f(z_{0})}{ \beta}\Big{|}\leq c\,|\alpha-\beta|\enspace,\qquad\alpha,\beta\in r\dot{ \mathbb{B}}_{\mathbb{C}}\enspace.\]

Now we deduce from the completeness of \(E\) that \(f\) is differentiable in \(z_{0}\). Hence \(f\) is holomorphic. The converse is obvious.

As usual, we identify \(\mathbb{C}\) and \(\mathbb{R}^{2}\) by means of the canonical identification

\[\mathbb{C}\ni z=x+i\,y\quad\longleftrightarrow\quad(x,y)\in\mathbb{R}^{2}\enspace.\]

Then \(f\in E^{X}\) can be interpreted as a function of the complex variable \(z\in X\subset\mathbb{C}\) or as a function of the real variables \((x,y)\in X\subset\mathbb{R}^{2}\). We write \(\partial\) for the complex derivative \(\partial_{z}\) and say that \(f\) is real differentiable if \(f\) is differentiable with respect to the real variables \((x,y)\in X\subset\mathbb{R}^{2}\).

**1.10.6 Corollary** _Let \(X\) be open in \(\mathbb{C}\). Then \(f\in E^{X}\) is holomorphic iff \(f\) is real differentiable and \(\overline{\partial}f=0\), where_

\[\overline{\partial}:=\partial_{\overline{z}}:=\frac{1}{2}(\partial_{x}+i\, \partial_{y})\ ,\qquad z=x+i\,y\ .\]

_If this is the case then \(\partial f=\partial_{z}f=\partial_{x}f\)._

**Proof** Given \(e^{\prime}\in E^{\prime}\), it follows that \(\overline{\partial}(e^{\prime}\circ f)=e^{\prime}\circ(\overline{\partial}f)\). Hence \(\overline{\partial}f=0\) iff \(e^{\prime}\circ f\) satisfies the Cauchy-Riemann differential equations for each \(e^{\prime}\in E^{\prime}\), that is, iff \(f\) is weakly holomorphic, hence holomorphic by Proposition 1.10.5. If this is true then

\[e^{\prime}\circ\partial f=\partial(e^{\prime}\circ f)=\partial_{x}\big{(} \mathrm{Re}(e^{\prime}\circ f)\big{)}+i\,\partial_{x}(\mathrm{Im}(e^{\prime} \circ f)\big{)}=\partial_{x}(e^{\prime}\circ f)=e^{\prime}\circ\partial_{x}f\]

for each \(e^{\prime}\in E^{\prime}\). Hence \(\partial f=\partial_{x}f\).

**Holomorphy of Laplace Transforms**

After these preparations we can show that the Laplace transform of a distribution is a holomorphic function. At the end of this subsection we shall derive more precise information about its behavior at infinity.

---

**1.10.7 Theorem**: _If \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) is Laplace transformable then \(\widetilde{u}\) is holomorphic._

**Proof** From Lemma 1.10.4 we know already that \(\widetilde{u}\in C^{\infty}(\dot{\tilde{I}}_{u}\times i\,{\mathbb{R}},E)\). Writing \(\zeta=\xi+i\eta\) it is easily seen that

\[\partial_{\xi}\widetilde{u}(\zeta)=-(Xe_{\xi}u)^{\frown}(\eta)\ ,\] (1.10.4)

where \(X\) denotes multiplication with the independent variable. On the other hand,

\[\eqalign{(\partial_{\eta}\widetilde{u})(\varphi)&=-\widetilde{u}(\partial \varphi)=-\widehat{e_{\xi}u}(\partial\varphi)=-u\bigl{(}e_{\xi}\widehat{ \partial\varphi}\bigr{)}\cr&=-i\,u\bigl{(}e_{\xi}X\widehat{\varphi}\bigr{)}=-i \,(Xe_{\xi}u)^{\frown}(\varphi)\cr}\]

for \(\varphi\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}})\). Hence \(\overline{\partial}\widetilde{u}=0\) and the assertion follows from Corollary 1.10.6.

**1.10.8 Corollary**: _If \(u\in{\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) then \(\widehat{u}\) extends to an \(E\)-valued entire analytic function on \({\mathbb{C}}\), the Laplace transform \(\widetilde{u}\) of \(u\)._

**Properties of Laplace Transforms**

Thanks to the close relationship between the Fourier and the Laplace transforms the latter enjoy many properties that are similar to some of those we know to hold for the former.

**1.10.9 Proposition**: _Suppose that \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) is Laplace transformable. Then_

* \(\partial^{k}\widetilde{u}=(-1)^{k}(X^{k}u)^{\frown}\)_,_ \(k\in{\mathbb{N}}\)_, where_ \(X^{k}\) _denotes multiplication with the function_ \(x\mapsto x^{k}\)_._
* \(I_{\partial^{k}u}\supset I_{u}\) _and_ \[(\partial^{k}u)^{\frown}(\zeta)=\zeta^{k}\widetilde{u}(\zeta)\ ,\qquad\zeta\in\dot{I}_{u}+i\,{\mathbb{R}}\ ,\] _for_ \(k\in{\mathbb{N}}\)_._
* \(\tau_{\alpha}u\) _is Laplace transformable for_ \(\alpha\in{\mathbb{R}}\) _and_ \[(\tau_{\alpha}u)^{\frown}(\zeta)=e^{-\alpha\zeta}\,\widetilde{u}(\zeta)\ , \qquad\zeta\in\dot{\tilde{I}}_{u}+i\,{\mathbb{R}}\ .\]

**Proof** (i) follows from (1.10.4), Theorem 1.10.7, and Corollary 1.10.6.

(ii) Note that \(e_{\xi}\partial u=\partial(e_{\xi}u)+\xi e_{\xi}u\) for \(\xi\in{\mathbb{R}}\). Thus \(e_{\xi}\partial u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) if \(\xi\in I_{u}\). Consequently,

\[(\partial u)^{\frown}(\zeta)=(e_{\xi}\partial u)^{\frown}(\eta)=\bigl{(} \partial(e_{\xi}u)\bigr{)}^{\frown}+\xi\widetilde{u}=i\,\eta\,\widehat{e_{\xi }u}+\xi\widetilde{u}=\zeta\,\widetilde{u}\]

for \(\zeta\in\dot{\tilde{I}}_{u}+i\,{\mathbb{R}}\). Now the assertion follows by induction.

---

(iii) Observe that \(e_{\xi}\tau_{\alpha}u=e^{-\alpha\xi}\tau_{\alpha}(e_{\xi}u)\) for \(\xi\in\mathbb{R}\). Consequently,

\[\eqalignno{(\tau_{\alpha}u)^{\sim}(\zeta)&=(e_{\xi}\tau_{\alpha}u)^{\sim}(\eta)=e ^{-\alpha\xi}\bigl{(}\tau_{\alpha}(e_{\xi}u)\bigr{)}^{\sim}(\eta)\cr&=e^{-( \alpha\xi+i\,\alpha\eta)}\widehat{e_{\xi}u}(\eta)=e^{-\alpha\zeta}\,\widetilde {u}(\zeta)\cr}\]

for \(\zeta\in\dot{\hat{I}}_{u}+i\,\mathbb{R}\), thanks to [Ama95, (III.4.2.6)].

**1.10.10 Example** \((\partial^{k}\delta_{\alpha})^{\sim}(\zeta)=\zeta^{k}e^{-\alpha\zeta}\), \(\zeta\in\mathbb{C}\), \(\alpha\in\mathbb{R}\).

Next we prove an important and useful result, namely the convolution theorem for Laplace transforms.

**1.10.11 Theorem** _Let \(E_{1}\times E_{2}\to E_{0}\), \((e_{1},e_{2})\mapsto e_{1}\bullet e_{2}\) be a multiplication and suppose that \(u_{j}\in\Cal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R},E_{j})\), \(j=1,2\), are Laplace transformable, have convolutive supports, and satisfy \(\dot{\hat{I}}_{u_{1}}\cap\dot{\hat{I}}_{u_{2}}\neq\emptyset\). Then \(u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}\in\Cal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R},E_{0}\,)\) is Laplace transformable as well, and_

\[(u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2})^{\sim}(\zeta)=\widetilde{u}_{1}(\zeta)\bullet \widetilde{u}_{2}(\zeta)\ ,\qquad\zeta\in\bigl{(}\dot{\hat{I}}_{u_{1}}\cap\dot{\hat{I}}_{u_{2}}\bigr{)}+i \,\mathbb{R}\ .\]

**Proof** From (1.9.18) we know that \(u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}\) is a well-defined element of \(\Cal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R},E_{0}\,)\). Given \(\xi\in\dot{\hat{I}}_{u_{1}}\cap\dot{\hat{I}}_{u_{2}}\), it follows from \(e_{\xi}u_{j}\in\Cal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R},E_{j})\), Remark 1.9.11(a), and Lemma 1.10.4 that

\[\bigl{(}(e_{\xi}u_{1})\ast_{\bullet}(e_{\xi}u_{2})\bigr{)}^{\sim}=\widehat{e_ {\xi}u_{1}}\bullet\widehat{e_{\xi}u_{2}}\ .\]

Let \(X\) be a bounded interval in \(\mathbb{R}\). Since \(u_{1}\) and \(u_{2}\) satisfy condition \((\Sigma)\) there exists \(\rho>0\) such that \(x_{j}\in\operatorname{supp}(u_{j})\) and \(x_{1}+x_{2}\in X\) imply \(|x_{j}|<\rho\) for \(j=1,2\). Let \(\psi_{j}\in\Cal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\) satisfy \(\psi_{j}\,|[-\rho,\rho]=1\). Then, thanks to the definition of \((e_{\xi}u_{1})\ast(e_{\xi}u_{2})\), given \(\varphi\in\Cal{D}(\mathbb{R})\) with \(\operatorname{supp}(\varphi)\subset X\), it follows that

\[\eqalignno{(e_{\xi}u_{1})\ast_{\bullet}(e_{\xi}u_{2})(\varphi)&=e_{\xi}\psi_{1 }u_{1}\otimes_{\bullet}e_{\xi}\psi_{2}u_{2}\bigl{(}\varphi(x_{1}+x_{2})\bigr{)} \cr&=\psi_{1}u_{1}\otimes_{\bullet}\psi_{2}u_{2}\bigl{(}(e_{\xi}\varphi)(x_{1}+ x_{2})\bigr{)}=u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2}(e_{\xi}\varphi)\cr&=e_{\xi}(u_{1}\ast_{ \bullet}u_{2})(\varphi)\ .\cr}\]

Thus we infer from (1.10.5) that

\[\bigl{(}e_{\xi}(u_{1}\ast_{\bullet}u_{2})\bigr{)}^{\sim}=\widehat{e_{\xi}u_{1 }}\bullet\widehat{e_{\xi}u_{2}}\ ,\qquad\xi\in\dot{\hat{I}}_{1}\cap\dot{\hat{I}}_{2}\ ,\]

which, thanks to the definition of the Laplace transform, proves the theorem.

**Laplace Transformable Distributions in \(\Cal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\)**

In the remainder of this subsection we restrict ourselves to the most important case, that is, to the case of Laplace transformable distributions whose supports

---

are bounded below. Thanks to Proposition 1.10.9(iii) and the fact that

\[\operatorname{supp}(u)\subset[\alpha,\infty)\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad \operatorname{supp}(\tau_{\alpha}u)\subset\mathbb{R}^{+}\] (1.10.6)

for \(\alpha\in\mathbb{R}\) and \(u\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R},E)\), it is no loss of generality to consider only Laplace transformable distributions belonging to \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\). Thus we put

\[\mathcal{L}\mathrm{ap}_{+}(E):=\{\,u\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\ ;\ I_{u} \neq\emptyset\,\}\ .\]

From Lemma 1.10.2 we know that \(\sup I_{u}=\infty\) for \(u\in\mathcal{L}\mathrm{ap}_{+}(E)\), and we introduce \(\omega_{0}(u)\), the **abscissa of holomorphy** of \(\widetilde{u}\), by

\[\omega_{0}(u):=\inf I_{u}\ .\]

Then \(\widetilde{u}\) is defined and holomorphic on \(\big{[}\mathrm{Re}\,z>\omega_{0}(u)\big{]}\) with values in \(E\).

Suppose that \(u\in\mathcal{L}\mathrm{ap}_{+}(E)\). Fix \(\psi_{j}\in\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R})\), satisfying \(\operatorname{supp}(\psi_{j})\subset[-2,\infty)\) and \(\psi_{j}|\,[-1,\infty)=1\) for \(j=0,1\). Also let \(\xi_{1}>\xi_{0}>\omega_{0}(u)\). Then \(\psi_{j}e_{\zeta-\xi_{j}}\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R})\) for \(\mathrm{Re}\,\zeta>\xi_{1}\) and \(e_{\xi_{j}}u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R},E)\) for \(j=0,1\). Hence \(e_{\xi_{j}}u(\psi_{j}e_{\zeta-\xi_{j}})\) is well-defined for \(j=0,1\) and \(\mathrm{Re}\,\zeta>\xi_{1}\). Note that

\[\begin{split} e_{\xi_{0}}u(\psi_{0}e_{\zeta-\xi_{0}})& =e_{\xi_{0}}u(\psi_{0}\psi_{1}e_{\xi_{1}-\xi_{0}}e_{\zeta-\xi_{1}}) =\psi_{0}e_{\xi_{1}-\xi_{0}}e_{\xi_{0}}u(\psi_{1}e_{\zeta-\xi_{1}})\\ &=e_{\xi_{1}}u(\psi_{1}e_{\zeta-\xi_{1}})\end{split}\] (1.10.7)

for \(\mathrm{Re}\,\zeta>\xi_{1}\), thanks to \(\psi_{0}e_{\xi_{1}-\xi_{0}}\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R})\) and \(\psi_{j}=1\) in a neighborhood of \(\operatorname{supp}(u)\). This shows that, given any \(u\in\mathcal{L}\mathrm{ap}_{+}(E)\) and \(\zeta\in\mathbb{C}\) with \(\mathrm{Re}\,\zeta>\omega_{0}(u)\),

\[u(e_{\zeta}):=e_{\xi_{0}}u(\psi_{0}e_{\zeta-\xi_{0}})\] (1.10.8)

is well-defined, independently of \(\xi_{0}\in\big{(}\omega_{0}(u),\mathrm{Re}\,\zeta\big{)}\) and \(\psi_{0}\in\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R})\), provided \(\psi_{0}=1\) in a neighborhood of \(\operatorname{supp}(u)\) and \(\psi_{0}\) vanishes near \(-\infty\).

**1.10.12 Theorem**: _If \(u\in\mathcal{L}\mathrm{ap}_{+}(E)\) then \(\widetilde{u}(\zeta)=u(e_{\zeta})\) for \(\mathrm{Re}\,\zeta>\omega_{0}(u)\)._

**Proof** Write \(\xi+i\,\eta=\zeta\) and fix \(\xi_{0}\in\big{(}\omega_{0}(u),\xi\big{)}\) and \(\psi_{0}\in\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R})\) vanishing near \(-\infty\) and being equal to \(1\) in \([-1,\infty)\). Then, given \(\varphi\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R})\), the arguments of (1.10.7) show that

\[\widetilde{u}(\xi+i\ \cdot)(\varphi)=\widehat{e_{\xi}u}(\varphi)=e_{\xi}u( \widehat{\varphi})=e_{\xi_{0}}u(e_{\xi-\xi_{0}}\psi_{0}\widehat{\varphi})\ .\]

Note that

\[(e_{\xi-\xi_{0}}\psi_{0}\widehat{\varphi})(\eta)=\int_{\mathbb{R}}e^{-(\xi-\xi _{0})\eta}\psi_{0}(\eta)e^{-i\,\eta x}\varphi(x)\,dx\]

and \(e_{\xi_{0}}u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\) imply

\[\begin{split} e_{\xi_{0}}u(e_{\xi-\xi_{0}}\psi_{0}\widehat{ \varphi})&=\int_{\mathbb{R}}e_{\xi_{0}}u(e_{\xi-\xi_{0}+i\,x} \psi_{0})\varphi(x)\,dx=\int_{\mathbb{R}}u(e_{\xi+i\,x})\varphi(x)\,dx\\ &=u(e_{\xi+i\,.})(\varphi)\ ,\end{split}\]

as is easily verified. This proves the assertion.

---

**1.10.13 Corollary**: _Suppose that \(u\in L_{1,{\rm loc}}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) has its support in \({\mathbb{R}}^{+}\) and satisfies_

\[\omega(u):=\inf\bigl{\{}\,\omega\in{\mathbb{R}}\ ;\ e_{\omega}u\in L_{1}({ \mathbb{R}},E)\,\bigr{\}}<\infty\ .\] (1.10.9)

_Then \(u\in{\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}(E)\) with \(\omega_{0}(u)\leq\omega(u)\), and_

\[\widetilde{u}(\zeta)=\int_{0}^{\infty}e^{-\zeta x}u(x)\,dx\ ,\qquad{\rm Re}\, \zeta>\omega(u)\ .\] (1.10.10)

ProofIt suffices to recall that \(L_{1}({\mathbb{R}},E)\hookrightarrow{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\).

It should be remarked that \(\omega(u)\) is called abscissa of absolute convergence of the Laplace integral of \(u\), which is the integral appearing in (1.10.10).

**1.10.14 Examples**: Suppose that \(A\in{\cal G}(E,M,\sigma)\) for some \(M\geq 1\) and \(\sigma\in{\mathbb{R}}\).

**(a)**: Put \(e_{A}(t):=e^{-tA}\) for \(t\geq 0\), and \(e_{A}(t):=0\) for \(t<0\). Then \(e_{A}\in{\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}\bigl{(}{\cal L}(E)\bigr{)}\) and \(\widetilde{e_{A}}(\zeta)=(\zeta+A)^{-1}\) for \({\rm Re}\,\zeta>\sigma\).

ProofIt is easily verified that \(\omega(e_{A})\leq\sigma\). From semigroup theory it is known that

\[(\zeta+A)^{-1}=\int_{0}^{\infty}e^{-\zeta t}e^{-tA}\,dt\ ,\qquad{\rm Re}\, \zeta>\sigma\ .\]

Hence Corollary 1.10.13 implies the assertion.

**(b)**: Given \(z\in{\mathbb{C}}\), put \(u_{z}(t):=t^{z-1}e^{-tA}\) for \(t>0\), and \(u_{z}(t):=0\) for \(t<0\). Then \(u_{z}\in{\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}\bigl{(}{\cal L}(E)\bigr{)}\) for \({\rm Re}\,z>0\), and \(\widetilde{u}_{z}(\zeta)=\Gamma(z)(\zeta+A)^{-z}\) for \({\rm Re}\,\zeta>\sigma\).

ProofAgain \(\sigma(u_{z})\leq\sigma\) for \({\rm Re}\,z>0\), and

\[\widetilde{u}(\zeta)=\int_{0}^{\infty}t^{z-1}e^{-(\zeta+A)t}\,dt\ ,\qquad{\rm Re }\,\zeta>\sigma\ ,\]

by Corollary 1.10.13. Now the assertion follows from [Ama95, Theorem III.4.6.6].

**An Inversion Formula**: Using the fact that \({\cal F}\) is an automorphism of \({\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}},E)\), it is possible to recover the distribution \(u\in{\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}(E)\) from its Laplace transform.

**1.10.15 Theorem**: _Suppose that \(u\in{\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}(E)\). Then_

\[u=e_{-\xi}{\cal F}^{-1}\widetilde{u}(\xi+i\ \cdot\ )\ ,\qquad\xi>\omega_{0}(u)\ .\] (1.10.11)

_If \(\widetilde{u}(\xi+i\ \cdot\ )\in L_{1}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) for some \(\xi>\omega_{0}(u)\) then \(e_{\xi}u\in C_{0}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) and_

\[u(x)=\frac{1}{2\pi i}\int_{\xi-i\,\infty}^{\xi+i\,\infty}e^{xz}\,\widetilde{u }(z)\,dz\ ,\qquad x\in{\mathbb{R}}\ .\] (1.10.12)

---

ProofThe first assertion follows directly from the definition of \(\widetilde{u}\). If \(\widetilde{u}(\xi+i\ \cdot\ )\) is integrable over \({\mathbb{R}}\) for some \(\xi>\omega_{0}(u)\) then the Riemann-Lebesgue theorem guarantees that \(e_{\xi}u={\cal F}^{-1}\widetilde{u}(\xi+i\ \cdot\ )\in C_{0}({\mathbb{R}},E)\). Moreover,

\[u(x)=\frac{e^{\xi x}}{2\pi}\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}e^{i\,x\eta}\,\widetilde{u}( \xi+i\eta)\,d\eta\ ,\qquad x\in{\mathbb{R}}\ ,\]

so that (1.10.12) is obvious.

**1.10.16 Remarks**: **(a)** From (1.10.11) we obtain immediately a _uniqueness theorem for Laplace transforms_: If \(u,v\in{\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}(E)\) and there is \(\xi>\max\bigl{(}\omega_{0}(u),\omega_{0}(v)\bigr{)}\) with \(\widetilde{u}\,|\,(\xi+i\,{\mathbb{R}})=\widetilde{v}\,|\,(\xi+i\,{\mathbb{R}})\), then \(u=v\).
**(b)** It should be remarked that \({\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}(E)\) is a vector subspace of \({\cal D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\) and that the Laplace transform satisfies

\[(u+\alpha v)^{\sim}(\zeta)=\widetilde{u}(\zeta)+\alpha\widetilde{v}(\zeta)\ , \qquad{\rm Re}\,\zeta>\omega_{0}(w)\vee\omega_{0}(v)\ ,\]

for \(u,v\in{\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}(E)\) and \(\alpha\in{\mathbb{R}}\).

**Characterization of Laplace Transforms**

Let \(X\) be open in \({\mathbb{K}}^{n}\). Then \(f\in E^{X}\) is said to be **polynomially bounded** in \(X\) if there exist \(c\geq 0\) and \(k\in{\mathbb{N}}\) such that

\[|f(x)|\leq c(1+|x|)^{k}\ ,\qquad x\in X\ .\]

Using this definition we now characterize those \(E\)-valued functions that are Laplace transforms of distributions in \({\cal D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\).

**1.10.17 Theorem**: _Suppose that \(u\in{\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}(E)\). Then \(\widetilde{u}\) is holomorphic in the half-plane \(\bigl{[}{\rm Re}\,z>\omega_{0}(u)\bigr{]}\) and polynomially bounded on \(\bigl{[}{\rm Re}\,z\geq\omega\bigr{]}\) for each \(\omega>\omega_{0}(u)\). Conversely, suppose that \(\omega\geq 0\) and \(v:\,\bigl{[}{\rm Re}\,z>\omega\bigr{]}\to E\) is holomorphic and polynomially bounded on \(\bigl{[}{\rm Re}\,z\geq\omega^{\prime}\bigr{]}\) for each \(\omega^{\prime}>\omega\). Then \(v\) is the Laplace transform of some \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\), and \(\omega_{0}(u)\leq\omega\)._

Proof(i) Suppose that \(u\in{\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}(E)\). Then \(\widetilde{u}\) is holomorphic in \(\bigl{[}{\rm Re}\,z>\omega_{0}(u)\bigr{]}\) by Lemma 1.10.2 and Theorem 1.10.7. Fix \(\omega>\omega_{0}(u)\) and put \(v:=e_{\omega}u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\). Then there exist \(k,m\in{\mathbb{N}}\) such that

\[|v(\varphi)|\leq cq_{k,m}(\varphi)\ ,\qquad\varphi\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}})\ .\] (1.10.13)

Choose \(\psi\in{\cal E}({\mathbb{R}})\) such that \({\rm supp}(\psi)\subset(-2,\infty)\) and \(\psi\,|\,(-1,\infty)=1\). Then, thanks to (1.10.8) and Proposition 1.10.12,

\[\widetilde{u}(\zeta)=v(\psi e_{\xi-\omega})\ ,\qquad{\rm Re}\,\zeta\geq\omega> \omega_{0}\ .\] (1.10.14)

From this and (1.10.13) we infer that \(\widetilde{u}\) is polynomially bounded on \(\bigl{[}{\rm Re}\,z\geq\omega\bigr{]}\) for each \(\omega>\omega_{0}(u)\).

---

(ii) First we consider the special case that \(f:\ [{\rm Re}\,z>\omega]\to E\) is holomorphic for some \(\omega\in{\mathbb{R}}\), and that

\[|f(\zeta)|\leq c(1+|\zeta|)^{-2}\ ,\qquad{\rm Re}\,\zeta>\omega\ .\]

Then \(f(\xi+i\ \cdot\ )\in L_{1}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) for \(\xi>\omega\). Motivated by Theorem 1.10.15 we put

\[u(x):={1\over 2\pi i}\int_{\xi-i\,\infty}^{\xi+i\,\infty}e^{x\xi}f(\zeta)\,d \zeta\ ,\qquad x\in{\mathbb{R}}\ .\]

Since (1.10.15) implies

\[|e^{x\zeta}f(\zeta)|\leq{ce^{x\xi}\over 1+\eta^{2}}\ ,\qquad\zeta=\xi+i\,\eta\ , \quad\xi>\omega\ ,\]

a standard application of Cauchy's theorem shows that the integral in (1.10.16) is independent of \(\xi>\omega\). Rewriting (1.10.16) in the form

\[(e_{\xi}u)(x)={1\over 2\pi}\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}e^{i\,x\eta}f(\xi+i\eta)\,d \eta={\cal F}^{-1}\bigl{(}f(\xi+i\,\cdot\,)\bigr{)}(x)\ ,\qquad x\in{ \mathbb{R}}\ ,\]

we deduce from the Riemann-Lebesgue lemma that \(e_{\xi}u\in C_{0}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) and that

\[f(\xi+i\eta)=\widehat{e_{\xi}u}(\eta)\ ,\qquad\xi>\omega\ ,\quad\eta\in{ \mathbb{R}}\ .\]

From (1.10.15) and (1.10.17) we infer that

\[|u(x)|\leq c{e^{\xi x}\over 2\pi}\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}{d\eta\over 1+\eta^{2}} \leq ce^{\xi x}\ ,\qquad\xi>\omega\ ,\quad x\in{\mathbb{R}}\ .\]

Since \(u\in C({\mathbb{R}},E)\) we see from this estimate that \(u(x)=0\) for \(x<0\). Hence \({\rm supp}(u)\) is contained in \({\mathbb{R}}^{+}\). This shows that \(u\in{\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}(E)\) and \(\widetilde{u}(\zeta)=f(\zeta)\) for \({\rm Re}\,\zeta>\omega\).

Now suppose that \(\omega\geq 0\) and \(g:\ [{\rm Re}\,z>\omega]\to E\) is holomorphic and, given \(\omega^{\prime}>\omega\), there exist \(c>0\) and \(m\in{\mathbb{N}}\) such that

\[|g(\zeta)|\leq c(1+|\zeta|)^{m}\ ,\qquad{\rm Re}\,\zeta>\omega^{\prime}\ .\]

Put \(f(\zeta):=\zeta^{-m-2}g(\zeta)\) for \({\rm Re}\,\zeta>\omega\). Then \(f\) is holomorphic in \([{\rm Re}\,z>\omega]\) and satisfies an estimate of the form (1.10.15) on \([{\rm Re}\,z\geq\omega^{\prime}]\). Hence, by what has already been shown and since (1.10.19) holds for each \(\omega^{\prime}>\omega\), there exists \(u\in{\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}(E)\) with \(\widetilde{u}(\zeta)=f(\zeta)\) for \({\rm Re}\,\zeta>\omega\). Then \(v:=\partial^{m+2}u\) belongs to \({\cal L}{\rm ap}_{+}(E)\) and \(\widetilde{v}(\zeta)\) equals \(g(\zeta)\) for \({\rm Re}\,\zeta>\omega\), thanks to Proposition 1.10.9(ii).

**1.10.18 Remarks** **(a)** The above proof shows that estimate (1.10.15) guarantees that the holomorphic function \(f\) is the Laplace transformation of a continuous function with support in \({\mathbb{R}}^{+}\). A characterization of Laplace transforms of functions in \(L_{\infty}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) with support in \({\mathbb{R}}^{+}\) has been given by Arendt in [Are87] (also

---

see [30, Theorem 0.2]). The standard reference for vector-valued Laplace transforms of functions and measures is [30, Chapter VI].

**(b)**: For simplicity we restricted ourselves to distributions on \(\mathbb{R}\). However, it is not difficult to verify that the proofs given in [19] or [31] for scalar Laplace transforms in \(n\) variables carry over to the case of vector-valued distributions. This is also true for vector-valued versions of the Paley-Wiener theorems characterizing Laplace transforms of test functions and distributions with compact supports.

The above proofs are adaptions of and straight-forward extensions from the scalar to the vector-valued case of the proofs given in [19], [19], and [31] (also see [15, Chapter XVI]). A convolution theorem more general than Theorem 1.10.11 is given, of course, in [19].

---

## 2 Dyadic Decompositions and Multiplier Theorems

This section is to a large extent of technical nature. The heart of the expounded material are the results on dyadic decompositions and Fourier multiplier theorems.

The technique of dyadic decompositions is well-known in harmonic analysis and belongs to the fundament on which one can build large parts of the theory of function spaces. Multiplier theorems are the principal tool for proving resolvent estimates for elliptic differential and pseudodifferential operators. As we know from [1], resolvent estimates belong to the cornerstones on which the theory of parabolic evolution equations rests.

### Lebesgue Spaces

In this subsection we collect some more or less well-known results on \(L_{p}\)-spaces of vector-valued functions. In particular, we consider sequence spaces where the elements of the sequences lie in Banach spaces.

#### Radon Measures

Let \(X\) be a \(\sigma\)**-compact** metrizable space, that is, \(X\) is a locally compact metrizable space that can be written as a countable union of compact sets. This is the case iff \(X\) is a locally compact separable metric space. By a positive **Radon measure**\(\mu\) on \(X\) we mean a complete \(\bar{\mathbb{R}}^{+}\)-valued measure on a \(\sigma\)-algebra \(\mathcal{A}_{\mu}\) containing the Borel \(\sigma\)-algebra of \(X\), which is finite on compact sets. Then \(\mu\) is **regular**, that is,

\[\mu(A)=\inf\bigl{\{}\,\mu(O)\ ;\ O\supset A,\ O\ \text{open}\,\bigr{\}}=\sup \bigl{\{}\,\mu(K)\ ;\ K\subset A,\ K\ \text{compact}\,\bigr{\}}\]

for \(A\in\mathcal{A}_{\mu}\) (e.g., [20, Theorem 2.18]). Clearly, if \(X\) is an open or a closed subset of \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) and \(\mu\) is the (restriction of the) \(n\)-dimensional Lebesgue measure on \(X\) then \(X\) is \(\sigma\)-compact and \(\mu\) is a positive Radon measure on \(X\).

Now let \(X\) be a \(\sigma\)-compact metrizable space, \(\mu\) a positive Radon measure on \(X\), and \(E:=(E,|\cdot|)\) a Banach space. In the following, we make free use (as we already did in [1]) of the Bochner integral and refer the reader to [10], [17], or [21] for details. In particular, we recall that Pettis' theorem guarantees that a function \(u\in E^{X}\) is \(\mu\)-measurable iff it is weakly \(\mu\)-measurable and \(\mu\)-almost separably valued. In particular, every \(u\in C(X,E)\) is \(\mu\)-measurable.

For \(u\in\mathcal{L}_{1}(X,\mu,E)\) and \(A\in\mathcal{A}_{\mu}\) with \(0<\mu(A)<\infty\) we define the **average of \(u\) over \(A\)** by

\[\mathchoice{{\vbox{\hbox{$-$}}\kern-13.499794pt}}{{\vbox{\hbox{$ -$}}\kern-12.149815pt}}{{\vbox{\hbox{$-$}} \kern-9.899849pt}}{{\vbox{\hbox{$-$}}\kern-8.999863pt}}\! \int_{A}u\,d\mu:=\frac{1}{\mu(A)}\int_{A}u\,d\mu\ .\]

#### 2.1.1 Proposition

_Let \(u\in\mathcal{L}_{1}(X,\mu,E)\) and let \(S\) be a closed subset of \(E\). If \(\mathchoice{{\vbox{\hbox{$-$}}\kern-13.499794pt}}{{\vbox{\hbox{$ -$}}\kern-12.149815pt}}{{\vbox{\hbox{$-$}} \kern-9.899849pt}}{{\vbox{\hbox{$-$}}\kern-8.999863pt}}\! \int_{A}u\,d\mu\in S\) for every \(A\in\mathcal{A}_{\mu}\) with \(0<\mu(A)<\infty\) then \(u(x)\in S\) for \(\mu\)-a.e. \(x\in X\)._

---

**Proof** We can assume that \(S\neq E\). By changing \(u\) on a \(\mu\)-null set we can assume that \(u(X)\) is separable, hence that \(E\) is separable. Let \(e\in S^{c}\) be given and let \(r>0\) be so small that \(\bar{\mathbb{B}}(e,r)\cap S=\emptyset\). Let \(K=\overline{K}\subset\subset X\) and suppose that \(A:=\bigl{[}u(x)\in\mathbb{B}(e,r)\bigr{]}\cap K\) has positive measure. Then

\[\Bigl{|}\,\fint_{A}u\,d\mu-e\Bigr{|}\leq\,\fint_{A}|u-e|\,d\mu\leq r\]

which contradicts \(\fint_{A}u\,d\mu\in S\). Hence we infer from the \(\sigma\)-compactness of \(X\) that \(\mu\bigl{(}\bigl{[}u(x)\in\mathbb{B}(e,r)\bigr{]}\bigr{)}=0\).
