Thanks to the separability of \(E\) there exists a countable dense subset \(M\) of \(S^{c}\). Consequently, \(S^{c}\) can be written as a countable union of open balls with centers in \(M\) and rational radii. Now the assertion is obvious.

**2.1.2 Corollary**: _If \(u\in\mathcal{L}_{1}(X,\mu,E)\) and \(\int_{A}u\,d\mu=0\) for each \(A\in\mathcal{A}_{\mu}\) with finite measure then \(u=0\)\(\mu\)-a.e._

**Dual Spaces**

For \(1\leq p\leq\infty\), \(u\in L_{p}(X,\mu,E)\), and \(v\in L_{p^{\prime}}(X,\mu,E^{\prime})\) with \(p^{\prime}:=p/(p-1)\), it follows from Holder's inequality that

\[\Bigl{|}\,\int_{X}\langle v,u\rangle_{E}\,d\mu\Bigr{|}\leq\int_{X}|\langle v,u \rangle_{E}|\,d\mu\leq\int_{X}|v|_{E^{\prime}}\,|u|_{E}\,d\mu\leq\|v\|_{p^{ \prime}}\,\|u\|_{p}\enspace.\] (2.1.1)

Thus, given \(v\in L_{p^{\prime}}(X,\mu,E^{\prime})\) and putting

\[T_{v}u:=\,\int_{X}\langle v,u\rangle_{E}\,d\mu\enspace,\qquad u\in L_{p}(X,\mu ,E)\enspace,\]

we see that

\[T_{v}\in L_{p}(X,\mu,E)^{\prime}\enspace,\quad\|T_{v}\|\leq\|v\|_{p^{\prime}}\enspace,\] (2.1.2)

thanks to (2.1.1). Consequently,

\[(v\mapsto T_{v})\in\mathcal{L}\bigl{(}L_{p^{\prime}}(X,\mu,E^{\prime}),L_{p}( X,\mu,E)^{\prime}\bigr{)}\enspace.\] (2.1.3)

In fact, more is true:

**2.1.3 Proposition**: _Given \(p\in[1,\infty]\), the map \(v\mapsto T_{v}\) is a linear isometry from \(L_{p^{\prime}}(X,\mu,E^{\prime})\) into \(L_{p}(X,\mu,E)^{\prime}\)._

**Proof** Let \(p>1\) and let \(v:=\sum e^{\prime}_{j}\chi_{A_{j}}\) be a \(\mu\)-simple \(E^{\prime}\)-valued function so that the \(A_{j}\) are pair-wise disjoint. Let \(\varepsilon>0\) be given. Then there exist \(e_{j}\in E\) such that \(|e_{j}|=1\) and \(\langle e^{\prime}_{j},e_{j}\rangle\geq(1-\varepsilon)\,\bigl{|}e^{\prime}_{j}\bigr{|}\), where \(\langle\cdot,\cdot\rangle:=\langle\cdot,\cdot\rangle_{E}\). Put

\[u:=\sum_{j}\bigl{|}e^{\prime}_{j}\bigr{|}^{p^{\prime}-1}\,e_{j}\chi_{A_{j}}\]

---

and observe that

\[\left\langle v,u\right\rangle=\sum\left|e_{j}^{\prime}\right|^{p^{\prime}-1}\left \langle e_{j}^{\prime},e_{j}\right\rangle\chi_{A_{j}}\geq(1-\varepsilon)\sum \left|e_{j}^{\prime}\right|^{p^{\prime}}\chi_{A_{j}}\ .\]

Hence

\[\int_{X}\left\langle v,u\right\rangle d\mu\geq(1-\varepsilon)\sum\left|e_{j}^{ \prime}\right|^{p^{\prime}}\mu(A_{j})=(1-\varepsilon)\left\|v\right\|_{p^{ \prime}}^{p^{\prime}}\]

and

\[\left\|u\right\|_{p}=\left(\sum\left|e_{j}^{\prime}\right|^{(p^{\prime}-1)p} \mu(A_{j})\right)^{1/p}=\left\|v\right\|_{p^{\prime}}^{p^{\prime}/p}=\left\|v \right\|_{p^{\prime}}^{p^{\prime}-1}\]

if \(p<\infty\). If \(p=\infty\) and \(v\neq 0\) then \(\left\|u\right\|_{\infty}=1\). Thus

\[T_{v}u=\int_{X}\left\langle v,u\right\rangle d\mu\geq(1-\varepsilon)\left\|v \right\|_{p^{\prime}}\left\|u\right\|_{p}\,.\]

Consequently, \(\left\|T_{v}\right\|\geq(1-\varepsilon)\left\|v\right\|_{p^{\prime}}\) and, letting \(\varepsilon\to 0\) and observing (2.1.2), we see that

\[\left\|T_{v}\right\|=\left\|v\right\|_{p^{\prime}}\] (2.1.4)

for each \(\mu\)-simple \(E^{\prime}\)-valued function \(v\) on \(X\). As the set of these functions is dense in \(L_{p^{\prime}}(X,\mu,E^{\prime})\), it follows from (2.1.3) that (2.1.4) is true for each \(v\in L_{p^{\prime}}(X,\mu,E^{\prime})\), provided \(1<p\leq\infty\).

Let \(p=1\) and \(v\in L_{\infty}(X,\mu,E^{\prime})\). Denote by \(\widetilde{v}\) an arbitrary representative of \(v\). By changing \(\widetilde{v}\) on a \(\mu\)-null set we may assume that \(\widetilde{v}(X)\) is separable. Hence \(F:=\overline{\operatorname{span}}\,\widetilde{v}(X)\) is a separable closed subspace of \(E^{\prime}\). Choose \(e^{\prime}\in F\) such that \(\left|e^{\prime}\right|>\left\|T_{v}\right\|\), let \(0<r<\left|e^{\prime}\right|-\left\|T_{v}\right\|\), and put \(B:=\left[\,\left|\widetilde{v}(x)-e^{\prime}\right|<r\right]\). Fix a positive \(\varepsilon\) strictly less than \((\left|e^{\prime}\right|-\left\|T_{v}\right\|-r)/\left|e^{\prime}\right|\) and choose \(e\in E\) with \(\left|e\right|=1\) and \(\left\langle e^{\prime},e\right\rangle>(1-\varepsilon)\left|e^{\prime}\right|\). Then

\[\left|\left\langle\widetilde{v}(x),e\right\rangle-\left|e^{\prime }\right|\right| \leq\left|\left\langle\widetilde{v}(x),e\right\rangle-\left\langle e ^{\prime},e\right\rangle\right|+\left|\left\langle e^{\prime},e\right\rangle- \left|e^{\prime}\right|\right|\leq\left|\widetilde{v}(x)-e^{\prime}\right|+ \varepsilon\left|e^{\prime}\right|\] (2.1.5) \[<\left|e^{\prime}\right|-\left\|T_{v}\right\|\]

for \(x\in B\). Observe that

\[\left|\int_{X}\left\langle\widetilde{v},e\chi_{A}\right\rangle d\mu\right|\leq \left\|T_{v}\right\|\mu(A)\ ,\qquad A\in\mathcal{A}_{\mu}\ ,\]

so that

\[\fint_{A}\left\langle\widetilde{v},e\right\rangle d\mu\in\bar{\mathbb{B}}(0, \left\|T_{v}\right\|)\]

for each \(A\in\mathcal{A}_{\mu}\) with \(0<\mu(A)<\infty\). Hence \(\left|\left\langle\widetilde{v}(x),e\right\rangle\right|\leq\left\|T_{v}\right\|\) for \(\mu\)-a.e. \(x\in X\), thanks to Proposition 2.1.1. Thus it follows from (2.1.5) that \(\mu(B)=0\). Now the separability of \(F\) implies -- similarly as in the proof of Proposition 2.1.1 -- that \(\left|\widetilde{v}(x)\right|\leq\left\|T_{v}\right\|\) for \(\mu\)-a.e. \(x\in X\). Consequently, \(\left\|v\right\|_{\infty}\leq\left\|T_{v}\right\|\) and (2.1.2) implies the assertion in this case as well.

---

**Definition 2.1.4**.: _Let \(\mu\) be a sequence of integers \(n\) and \(\mu\). Let \(\mu_{1},\ldots,\mu_{n}\) be distinct elements of \(\mu\). Then \(\mu_{1},\ldots,\mu_{n}\) are elements of \(\mu\)._

[MISSING_PAGE_POST]

**Definition 2.1.

---

We endow \(\mathbb{Z}\) with the discrete topology and denote by \(\mathcal{H}_{0}\) the counting measure on \(\mathbb{Z}\) (the zero-dimensional Hausdorff measure). Thus, if \(A\subset\mathbb{Z}\) is finite then \(\mathcal{H}_{0}(A):=\operatorname{card}(A)\), and \(\mathcal{H}_{0}(A):=\infty\) otherwise. Then \(\mathcal{H}_{0}\) is a positive Radon measure on the \(\sigma\)-compact metrizable space \(\mathbb{Z}\). Moreover, \(C(\mathbb{Z},E)=E^{\mathbb{Z}}\).

We put

\[\ell_{p}(E):=L_{p}(\mathbb{Z},\mathcal{H}_{0},E)\ ,\qquad 1\leq p\leq\infty\ ,\]

and

\[c_{0}(E):=C_{0}(\mathbb{Z},E)\ .\]

As usual,

\[\ell_{p}:=\ell_{p}(\mathbb{K})\ ,\quad c_{0}:=c_{0}(\mathbb{K})\ ,\]

if no confusion seems possible.

More generally, let \(\boldsymbol{E}:=(E_{j})\) be a doubly infinite sequence of \(\mathbb{K}\)-Banach spaces. Then we put

\[\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E}):=\Big{(}\big{\{}\,\boldsymbol{u}:=(u_{j}):\ \mathbb{Z}\to \bigcup_{j}E_{j}\ ;\ u_{j}\in E_{j},\ \|\boldsymbol{u}\|_{\ell_{p}}<\infty\, \big{\}},\ \|\boldsymbol{\cdot}\|_{\ell_{p}}\Big{)}\]

for \(1\leq p\leq\infty\), where

\[\|\boldsymbol{\cdot}\|_{\ell_{p}}:=\left\{\begin{array}{ll}\left(\sum_{j}\| u_{j}\|_{E_{j}}^{p}\right)^{1/p}\ ,&1\leq p<\infty\ ,\\ \sup_{j}\|u_{j}\|_{E_{j}}\ ,&p=\infty\ .\end{array}\right.\]

We also put

\[c_{0}(\boldsymbol{E}):=\big{\{}\,\boldsymbol{u}\in\ell_{\infty}(\boldsymbol{E} )\ ;\ \|u_{j}\|_{E_{j}}\to 0\ \text{as}\ j\to\pm\infty\,\big{\}}\ .\]

Clearly, if \(E_{j}=E\) for \(j\in\mathbb{Z}\), then

\[\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})=\ell_{p}(E)\ ,\quad 1\leq p\leq\infty\ ,\qquad c_{0}( \boldsymbol{E})=c_{0}(E)\ .\] (2.1.6)

It is an easy exercise to prove that \(\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})\) is a Banach space for \(1\leq p\leq\infty\), that \(c_{0}(\boldsymbol{E})\) is a closed linear subspace of \(\ell_{\infty}(\boldsymbol{E})\), and that

\[\|\boldsymbol{u}\|_{\ell_{p_{0}}}\leq\|\boldsymbol{u}\|_{\ell_{p_{1}}}\ ,\qquad 1 \leq p_{1}\leq p_{0}\leq\infty\ ,\] (2.1.7)

so that

\[\ell_{p_{1}}(\boldsymbol{E})\hookrightarrow\ell_{p_{0}}(\boldsymbol{E})\ , \qquad 1\leq p_{1}\leq p_{0}\leq\infty\ .\] (2.1.8)

Moreover,

\[\ell_{p_{1}}(\boldsymbol{E})\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}} \ell_{p_{0}}(\boldsymbol{E})\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}c_{0 }(\boldsymbol{E})\hookrightarrow\ell_{\infty}(\boldsymbol{E})\ ,\qquad 1\leq p_{1}\leq p_{0}< \infty\ .\] (2.1.9)

Let \(\boldsymbol{E}^{\prime}:=(E^{\prime}_{j})\). Then Holder's inequality implies that

\[\langle\boldsymbol{v},\boldsymbol{u}\rangle_{\ell_{p}}:=\sum_{j}\langle v_{j} ,u_{j}\rangle_{E_{j}}\ ,\qquad\boldsymbol{v}\in\ell_{p^{\prime}}(\boldsymbol{E}^{ \prime})\ ,\quad\boldsymbol{u}\in\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})\ ,\] (2.1.10)

---

is well-defined and satisfies

\[|\langle\boldsymbol{v},\boldsymbol{u}\rangle_{\ell_{p}}|\leq\left\|\boldsymbol{v} \right\|_{\ell_{p^{\prime}}(\boldsymbol{E}^{\prime})}\left\|\boldsymbol{u} \right\|_{\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})}\ .\] (2.1.11)

The following theorem shows that more is true:

**2.1.6 Theorem**: _With respect to the \(\ell_{p}\)-duality pairing (2.1.10)_

\[\left[\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})\right]^{\prime}=\ell_{p^{\prime}}(\boldsymbol{ E}^{\prime})\ ,\qquad 1\leq p<\infty\ ,\]

_and_

\[\left[c_{0}(\boldsymbol{E})\right]^{\prime}=\ell_{1}(\boldsymbol{E}^{\prime})\ .\]

Proof.: (i) Define \(T_{\boldsymbol{v}}\) for \(\boldsymbol{v}\in\ell_{p^{\prime}}(\boldsymbol{E}^{\prime})\) by

\[T_{\boldsymbol{v}}\boldsymbol{u}:=\langle\boldsymbol{v},\boldsymbol{u}\rangle_ {\ell_{p}}\ ,\qquad\boldsymbol{u}\in\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})\ .\]

Then an obvious modification of the first part of the proof of Proposition 2.1.3 shows that \(\boldsymbol{v}\mapsto T_{\boldsymbol{v}}\) is a linear isometry from \(\ell_{p^{\prime}}(\boldsymbol{E}^{\prime})\) into \(\left[\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})\right]^{\prime}\) if \(1<p\leq\infty\).

If \(p=1\) then

\[T_{\boldsymbol{v}}\in\left[\ell_{1}(\boldsymbol{E})\right]^{\prime}\quad\text {and}\quad\left\|T_{\boldsymbol{v}}\right\|\leq\left\|\boldsymbol{v}\right\|_ {\ell_{\infty}}\ ,\qquad\boldsymbol{v}\in\ell_{\infty}(\boldsymbol{E}^{\prime})\ .\]

Given \(\boldsymbol{v}\in\ell_{\infty}(\boldsymbol{E}^{\prime})\) and \(\varepsilon>0\), there exists \(k\in\mathbb{Z}\) with \(|v_{k}|_{E_{k}^{\prime}}\geq(1-\varepsilon)\left\|\boldsymbol{v}\right\|_{\ell _{\infty}}\). If \(E_{k}\neq\{0\}\) then we can find \(u_{k}\in E_{k}\) with \(|u_{k}|_{E_{k}}=1\) and

\[\langle v_{k},u_{k}\rangle_{E_{k}}\geq(1-\varepsilon)\left|v_{k}\right|_{E_{k} ^{\prime}}\geq(1-\varepsilon)^{2}\left\|\boldsymbol{v}\right\|_{\ell_{\infty} }\ .\]

Thus, letting \(\boldsymbol{u}:=(u_{k}\delta_{kj})_{j\in\mathbb{Z}}\), where \(\delta_{kj}\) is the Kronecker symbol, we see that \(\boldsymbol{u}\in\ell_{1}(\boldsymbol{E})\) with \(\left\|\boldsymbol{u}\right\|_{\ell_{1}}=1\) and

\[|T_{\boldsymbol{v}}\boldsymbol{u}|=\langle v_{k},u_{k}\rangle_{E_{k}}\geq(1- \varepsilon)^{2}\left\|\boldsymbol{v}\right\|_{\ell_{\infty}}\ .\]

This implies \(\left\|T_{\boldsymbol{v}}\right\|\geq\left\|\boldsymbol{v}\right\|_{\ell_{ \infty}}\), so that \(\boldsymbol{v}\mapsto T_{\boldsymbol{v}}\) is a linear isometry from \(\ell_{\infty}(\boldsymbol{E}^{\prime})\) into \(\left[\ell_{1}(\boldsymbol{E})\right]^{\prime}\) as well. Thus it remains to show that \(\boldsymbol{v}\mapsto T_{\boldsymbol{v}}\) is surjective.

Let \(f\in\left[\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})\right]^{\prime}\) be given. For each \(k\in\mathbb{Z}\) define \(e_{k}^{\prime}\in E_{k}^{\prime}\) by

\[\langle e_{k}^{\prime},e_{k}\rangle:=f\left((e_{k}\delta_{kj})_{j\in\mathbb{N }}\right)\ ,\qquad e_{k}\in E_{k}\ .\]

Let \(\boldsymbol{v}:=(e_{j}^{\prime})\) and put \(\boldsymbol{v}^{N}:=\boldsymbol{v}\chi_{\left\lfloor\,|j|\leq N\right\rfloor}\) for \(N\in\mathbb{N}\). Then \(\boldsymbol{v}^{N}\in\ell_{p^{\prime}}(\boldsymbol{E}^{\prime})\) and

\[\langle\boldsymbol{v}^{N},\boldsymbol{u}\rangle_{\ell_{p}}=\sum_{j=-N}^{N} \langle e_{j}^{\prime},u_{j}\rangle_{E_{j}}=f(\boldsymbol{u}^{N})\ ,\qquad \boldsymbol{u}\in\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})\ ,\] (2.1.12)

---

where \(\boldsymbol{u}^{N}:=\boldsymbol{u}\chi_{[\,|j|\leq N]}\). This means that

\[T_{\boldsymbol{v}^{N}}\boldsymbol{u}=f(\boldsymbol{u}^{N})\ ,\qquad\boldsymbol{u} \in\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})\ .\]

From this and the first part of the proof we deduce that

\[\|\boldsymbol{v}^{N}\|_{\ell_{p^{\prime}}}=\|T_{\boldsymbol{v}^{N}}\|_{[\ell_{ p}(\boldsymbol{E})]^{\prime}}\leq\|f\|_{[\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})]^{\prime}}\ ,\qquad N\in\mathbb{N}\ .\]

It is an easy consequence of this estimate that \(\boldsymbol{v}\in\ell_{p^{\prime}}(\boldsymbol{E})\). Since \(\boldsymbol{u}^{N}\to\boldsymbol{u}\) in \(\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})\) as \(N\to\infty\), thanks to \(1\leq p<\infty\), we now infer from (2.1.12) that \(\langle\boldsymbol{v},\boldsymbol{u}\rangle_{\ell_{p}}=f(\boldsymbol{u})\) for \(\boldsymbol{u}\in\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})\). This proves the surjectivity of the map \(\boldsymbol{v}\mapsto T_{\boldsymbol{v}}\).

(ii) The above proof applies to \(f\in[c_{0}(\boldsymbol{E})]^{\prime}\) as well.

The proof of Theorem 2.1.6 is a trivial extension of the corresponding result for the case where \(E_{j}=E\) for all \(j\in\mathbb{Z}\), which can be found in Lions and Peetre [26], although it was probably already known to Grothendieck much earlier. It should be noted that there are no restrictions on the Banach spaces \(E_{j}\).

Now suppose that

\[E_{j}:=\{0\}\ ,\qquad j\notin\{1,\ldots,n\}\ .\] (2.1.13)

Then

\[\|\boldsymbol{u}\|_{\infty}\leq\|\boldsymbol{u}\|_{\ell_{p}}\leq\sqrt[p]{n}\, \|u\|_{\infty}\ ,\qquad 1\leq p<\infty\ ,\] (2.1.14)

so that

\[\ell_{p}(\boldsymbol{E})\doteq c_{0}(\boldsymbol{E})\doteq\ell_{2}(\boldsymbol {E})=:E_{1}\times\cdots\times E_{n}\ ,\qquad 1\leq p\leq\infty\ .\] (2.1.15)

Note that the last definition means that we equip the product Banach space \(E_{1}\times\cdots\times E_{n}\) with the 'Euclidean norm', unless otherwise stated. If, in addition, \(E_{1}=\cdots=E_{n}=E\), then in abuse of notation

\[\ell_{p}^{(n)}(E):=(E^{n},|\cdot|_{\ell_{p}})\] (2.1.16)

and \(\ell_{p}^{(n)}:=\ell_{p}^{(n)}(\mathbb{K})\).

#### Weighted Sequence Spaces

In connection with dyadic resolutions of the identity we shall encounter the sequence spaces

\[\ell_{p}^{s}(E):=L_{p}(\mathbb{Z},\mu_{s};E)\ ,\qquad 1\leq p\leq\infty\ ,\quad s \in\mathbb{R}\ ,\] (2.1.17)

where \(\mu_{s}\) is the Radon measure on \(\mathbb{Z}\) that assigns the value \(2^{ks}\) to the singleton \(\{k\}\). Letting

\[E_{k}^{s}:=2^{ks}E:=(E,2^{ks}\,|\cdot|_{E})\doteq E\ ,\qquad k\in\mathbb{Z}\ , \quad s\in\mathbb{R}\ ,\] (2.1.18)

---

and \(\mbox{\boldmath$E$}^{s}:=(E^{s}_{k})\), it follows that

\[\ell^{s}_{p}(E)=\ell_{p}(\mbox{\boldmath$E$}^{s})\ ,\qquad 1\leq p\leq\infty\ ,\quad s \in\mathbb{R}\ .\] (2.1.19)

Observe that

\[\ell_{p}(E)=\ell^{0}_{p}(E)\ ,\qquad 1\leq p\leq\infty\ .\] (2.1.20)

We also put

\[c^{s}_{0}(E):=c_{0}(\mbox{\boldmath$E$}^{s})\ ,\]

so that \(c^{0}_{0}(E)=c_{0}(E)\).

The following theorem is an almost immediate consequence of the above duality theorem for \(\ell_{p}\) spaces.

**2.1.7 Theorem**: _With respect to the \(\ell_{p}\)-duality pairing_:

\[\left[\ell^{s}_{p}(E)\right]^{\prime}=\ell^{-s}_{p^{\prime}}(E^{\prime})\ , \qquad 1\leq p<\infty\ ,\]

_and_

\[\left[c^{s}_{0}(E)\right]^{\prime}=\ell^{-s}_{1}(E^{\prime})\]

_for \(s\in\mathbb{R}\)._

**Proof** Since \(\left(E^{s}_{k}\right)^{\prime}=\left(E^{\prime}\right)^{-s}_{k}\) with respect to the \(E^{\prime}\)-\(E\) duality pairing, Theorem 2.1.6 implies the assertion.

For later use we include a simple embedding theorem for \(\ell^{s}_{p}\)-spaces.

**2.1.8 Proposition**: _Suppose that \(s,t\in\mathbb{R}\) and \(p,q\in[1,\infty]\). Then_

\[\ell^{s}_{p}(E)\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}\ell^{s}_{q}(E) \stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}c^{s}_{0}(E)\hookrightarrow\ell ^{s}_{\infty}(E)\ ,\qquad p\leq q<\infty\ ,\] (2.1.21)

_and, if \(E_{k}=\{0\}\) for \(k<0\), then_

\[\ell^{s}_{p}(E)\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}\ell^{t}_{q}(E) \ ,\qquad s>t\ .\] (2.1.22)

_If \(E_{0}\) and \(E_{1}\) are Banach spaces with \(E_{1}\hookrightarrow E_{0}\) then_

\[\ell^{s}_{p}(E_{1})\hookrightarrow\ell^{s}_{p}(E_{0})\quad\mbox{and}\quad c^{ s}_{0}(E_{1})\hookrightarrow c^{s}_{0}(E_{0})\ .\] (2.1.23)

_If \(\left(E_{0},E_{1}\right)\) is a densely injected Banach couple then_

\[\ell^{s}_{p}(E_{1})\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}\ell^{s}_{p }(E_{0})\ \mbox{for }p<\infty\quad\mbox{and}\quad c^{s}_{0}(E_{1})\stackrel{{ d}}{{ \hookrightarrow}}c^{s}_{0}(E_{0})\ .\] (2.1.24)

**Proof** It follows from (2.1.8), (2.1.9), and (2.1.19) that the injections in (2.1.21) are well-defined and continuous. Put

\[C_{c}(\mathbb{Z},E):=\big{\{}\,u:\,\mathbb{Z}\to E\ ;\ \mbox{supp}(u)\ \mbox{is finite}\,\big{\}}\]

---

and note that

\[C_{c}(\mathbb{Z},E)\overset{d}{\subset}c_{0}^{s}(E)\ ,\qquad s\in\mathbb{R}\ .\] (2.1.25)

Since it is also easily verified that

\[C_{c}(\mathbb{Z},E)\overset{d}{\subset}\ell_{p}^{s}(E)\ ,\qquad s\in\mathbb{R}\ , \quad 1\leq p<\infty\ ,\] (2.1.26)

assertion (2.1.21) follows. If \(E_{k}=\{0\}\) for \(k<0\) then

\[\begin{split}\|\boldsymbol{u}\|_{\ell_{q}^{t}}=\big{\|}(2^{kt}u_{ k})_{k\in\mathbb{N}}\big{\|}_{\ell_{q}}&\leq\big{\|}(2^{k(t-s)})_{k \in\mathbb{N}}\big{\|}_{\ell_{q}}\big{\|}(2^{ks}u_{k})_{k\in\mathbb{N}}\big{\|} _{\ell_{\infty}}\\ &=c\,\|\boldsymbol{u}\|_{\ell_{\infty}^{s}}\leq c\,\|\boldsymbol{ u}\|_{\ell_{p}^{s}}\end{split}\] (2.1.27)

for \(\boldsymbol{u}:=(u_{k})\in\ell_{p}^{s}(E)\), where the last inequality follows from (2.1.21). Hence we see that \(\ell_{p}^{s}(E)\hookrightarrow\ell_{q}^{t}(E)\) for \(s>t\). The density assertion is now a consequence of (2.1.25) and (2.1.26).

Assertion (2.1.23) is obvious. If \(E_{1}\) is also dense in \(E_{0}\) then (2.1.24) is an easy consequence of (2.1.25) and (2.1.26),

Of course, if \(E_{k}=\{0\}\) for \(k<0\) then \(\ell_{p}(E)=L_{p}(\mathbb{N},\mathcal{H}_{0}\,,E),\ 1\leq p\leq\infty\), and \(c_{0}(E)=C_{0}(\mathbb{N},E)\), etc.

### Vector Measures and the Riesz Representation Theorem

In connection with duality theory and Besov spaces we need some explicit information on \(C_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)^{\prime}\). For this reason we present in this subsection some elements of the theory of vector measures and prove a generalization of the well-known Riesz representation theorem.

#### Measures of Bounded Variation

Throughout this subsection \(E:=(E,|\cdot|)\) is a Banach space, \(X\) is a \(\sigma\)-compact metrizable space, and \(\mathcal{B}_{X}\) denotes the Borel \(\sigma\)-algebra of \(X\). By an \(E\)-valued **vector measure**\(\mu\) on \(X\) we mean a \(\sigma\)-additive map \(\mu:\ \mathcal{B}_{X}\to E\) satisfying \(\mu(\emptyset)=0\). For such a vector measure \(\mu\) we define the **variation**\(|\mu|:\ \mathcal{B}_{X}\to\mathbb{R}^{+}\cup\{\infty\}\) by

\[|\mu|\,(B):=\sup_{\pi(B)}\sum_{A\in\pi(B)}|\mu(A)|\ ,\qquad B\in\mathcal{B}_{X}\ ,\]

where the supremum is taken over all partitions \(\pi(B)\) of \(B\) into a finite number of pair-wise disjoint Borel subsets. Then \(\mu\) is said to be of **bounded variation** if

\[\|\mu\|_{BV}:=|\mu|\,(X)<\infty\ .\]

---

The set of all \(E\)-valued vector measures on \(X\) of bounded variation is denoted by

\[{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E):=\left({\cal M}_{BV}(X,E),\;\|\!\cdot\!\|_{BV}\right)\ .\]

It is clear that \({\cal M}_{BV}(X,E)\) is a normed vector space with the obvious linear structure (as a vector subspace of \(E^{{\cal B}_{X}}\)).

Given \(\mu\in{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E)\), it follows from \(|\mu(B)|\leq|\mu|\,(B)\) for \(B\in{\cal B}_{X}\) that \(|\mu|\) is a positive Borel measure on \(X\). We denote its completion, defined on the \(\sigma\)-algebra \({\cal A}_{|\mu|}\), consisting of all \(A\subset X\) of the form \(A=B\cup N\) where \(B\in{\cal B}_{X}\) and \(N\) is a subset of a \(|\mu|\)-null set, again by \(|\mu|\). Then \(|\mu|\) is a positive finite Radon measure on \(X\).

**2.2.1 Remark** Denote by \(dx\) a positive Radon measure on \(X\). Then, given any function \(u\in L_{1}(X,dx,E)\),

\[(u\,dx)(B):=\int_{B}u\,dx\ ,\qquad B\in{\cal B}_{X}\ ,\]

defines an \(E\)-valued measure of bounded variation and \(\|u\,dx\|_{BV}=\|u\|_{1}\) (cf. [DU77, Theorem 4(iv) on p. 46] or [Lan69, Theorem XI.9]). Hence

\[(u\mapsto u\,dx):\;L_{1}(X,dx,E)\to{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E)\]

is a linear isometry. Occasionally, it will be convenient to identify \(u\) with \(u\,dx\), that is, to consider \(L_{1}(X,dx,E)\) as a closed linear subspace of \({\cal M}_{BV}(X,E)\).

**Integrals with Respect to Vector Measures**

Let \(E_{0}\), \(E_{1}\), and \(E_{2}\) be Banach spaces and suppose that

\[E_{1}\times E_{2}\to E_{0}\ ,\quad(e_{1},e_{2})\mapsto e_{1}\bullet e_{2}\]

is a multiplication. Let \({\cal B}(X,E_{1})\) be the closure in \(B(X,E_{1})\) of the linear subspace \(S(X,E_{1})\) of all simple functions

\[u=\sum_{B\in\pi}\chi_{B}\otimes e_{B}\ ,\qquad e_{B}\in E_{1}\ ,\quad\pi:=\pi(X)\ .\]

If \(u\in S(X,E_{1})\) is given by (2.2.2) and \(\mu\in{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E_{2})\) then we put

\[\int u\,d\mu:=\int_{X}u\,d\mu:=\sum_{B\in\pi}e_{B}\bullet\mu(B)\in E_{0}\ .\]

It follows that

\[S(X,E_{1})\times{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E_{2})\to E_{0}\ ,\quad(u,\mu)\mapsto\int u\,d\mu\]

---

is a well-defined bilinear map satisfying

\[\Big{|}\int u\,d\mu\Big{|}_{E_{0}}\leq\sum_{B\in\pi}|e_{B}|\,|\mu(B)|\leq\sum_{B\in \pi}|e_{B}|\,|\mu|\,(B)\leq\|u\|_{\infty}\,\|\mu\|_{BV}\ \ .\] (2.2.3)

Hence it possesses a unique continuous bilinear extension of norm at most one over \({\cal B}(X,E_{1})\times{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E_{2})\). Note that \(C_{0}(X,E_{1})\) is a closed linear subspace of \({\cal B}(X,E_{1})\). Thus, by restriction, we obtain a well-defined multiplication

\[C_{0}(X,E_{1})\times{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E_{2})\to E_{0}\ ,\quad(u,\mu)\mapsto\int u \,d\mu:=\int_{X}u\,d\mu\] (2.2.4)

and \(\int_{X}u\,d\mu\) is said to be the **integral of \(u\) over \(X\) with respect to \(\mu\)** (and the multiplication (2.2.1)). In fact, from (2.2.3) we deduce that

\[\Big{|}\int u\,d\mu\Big{|}_{E_{0}}\leq\int|u|\,d\,|\mu|\leq\|u\|_{\infty}\,\| \mu\|_{BV}\] (2.2.5)

for \(u\in C_{0}(X,E_{1})\) and \(\mu\in{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E_{2})\).

Now suppose that there are further Banach spaces \(E_{3}\), \(E_{4}\), and \(E_{5}\), and multiplications satisfying the associativity assumption of Remark 1.6.5(e). Then it follows from the definition of \(\int u\,d\mu\) that, for each \(u\) of the form (2.2.2),

\[\int u\,d\mu\bullet e=\sum_{B\in\pi}e_{B}\bullet\big{(}\mu(B)\bullet e\big{)} \in E_{5}\ ,\qquad e\in E_{3}\ .\] (2.2.6)

Note that, given \(e\in E_{3}\),

\[\mu\bullet e:=\mu(\cdot)\bullet e:\,{\cal B}_{X}\to E_{4}\ ,\quad B\mapsto\mu(B) \bullet e\]

is \(\sigma\)-additive. Moreover,

\[|\mu\bullet e(B)|\leq|\mu(B)|\,|e|\leq|\mu|\,(B)\,|e|\ ,\qquad B\in{\cal B}_{X}\ ,\]

plainly implies that \(\mu\bullet e\) is an \(E_{4}\)-valued vector measure on \(X\) of bounded variation, and that

\[{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E_{2})\times E_{3}\to{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E_{4})\ ,\quad(\mu,e) \mapsto\mu\bullet e\] (2.2.7)

is a multiplication. From this and (2.2.6) we infer that

\[\Big{(}\int u\,d\mu\Big{)}\bullet e=\int u\,d(\mu\bullet e)\] (2.2.8)

and that

\[C_{0}(X,E_{1})\times{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E_{2})\times E_{3}\to E_{5}\ ,\quad(u,\mu,e) \mapsto\int u\,d(\mu\bullet e)\] (2.2.9)

is a trilinear multiplication.

---

Similarly as above, we see that (2.2.1) induces a multiplication

\[E_{1}\times{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E_{2})\to{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E_{0})\ ,\quad(e,\mu)\mapsto e \bullet\mu\ .\] (2.2.10)

Thus, given

\[\varphi=\sum_{B\in\pi}\alpha_{B}\chi_{B}\in S(X,{\mathbb{K}})\ ,\]

it follows from

\[\varphi\otimes e=\sum_{B\in\pi}\alpha_{B}\chi_{B}\otimes e=\sum_{B\in\pi}\chi_{ B}\otimes(\alpha_{B}e)\in S(X,E_{1})\]

for \(e\in E_{1}\) that

\[\int\varphi\otimes e\,d\mu=\sum_{B\in\pi}\alpha_{B}e\bullet\mu(B)=\int\varphi\, d(e\bullet\mu)\ .\]

From this we deduce that

\[\int\varphi\otimes e\,d\mu=\int\varphi\,d(e\bullet\mu)\ ,\qquad\varphi\otimes e \in C_{0}(X)\otimes E_{1}\ ,\quad\mu\in{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E_{2})\ .\] (2.2.11)

In the following, we leave it to the reader to identify the choices for the spaces \(E_{0},\ldots,E_{5}\) and the multiplications that we are using in concrete situations.

#### Vector Measures as Distributions

Now suppose that \(X\) is open in \({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\) . Since \({\cal D}(X)\) is dense in \(C_{0}(X)\), it follows that the multiplication

\[C_{0}(X)\times{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E)\to E\ ,\quad(\varphi,\mu)\mapsto\int\varphi\,d\mu\]

is completely determined by its restriction to \({\cal D}(X)\times{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E)\). Put

\[T_{\mu}\varphi:=\int\varphi\,d\mu\ ,\qquad(\varphi,\mu)\in{\cal D}(X)\times{ \cal M}_{BV}(X,E)\ .\]

Then \({\cal D}(X)\hookrightarrow C_{0}(X)\) implies

\[(\mu\mapsto T_{\mu})\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E),{\cal D}^{\prime}(X,E )\bigr{)}\ .\] (2.2.12)

Suppose that \(T_{\mu}=0\) in \({\cal D}^{\prime}(X,E)\) for some \(\mu\in{\cal M}_{BV}(X,E)\). Then we infer from (2.2.8) that

\[\langle e^{\prime},T_{\mu}\varphi\rangle_{E}=\int\varphi\,d\langle e^{\prime}, \mu\rangle_{E}=0\ ,\qquad\varphi\in{\cal D}(X)\ ,\quad e^{\prime}\in E^{\prime}\ .\]

Since \(\langle e^{\prime},\mu\rangle_{E}\in{\cal M}_{BV}(X):={\cal M}_{BV}(X,{\mathbb{ K}})\) , this implies \(\langle e^{\prime},\mu\rangle_{E}=0\) for each \(e^{\prime}\in E^{\prime}\). Thus \(\bigl{\langle}e^{\prime},\mu(B)\bigr{\rangle}_{E}=0\) for \(e^{\prime}\in E^{\prime}\) and \(B\in{\cal B}_{X}\), which gives \(\mu=0\). This shows that (2.2.12) is an injection. Thus the following convention is justified.

---

Convention Let \(X\) be a nonempty open subset of \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\). Then we identify \(\mu\in\mathcal{M}_{BV}(X,E)\) with \(T_{\mu}\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(X,E)\) so that

\[\mathcal{M}_{BV}(X,E)\hookrightarrow\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(X,E)\] (2.2.13)

and

\[\mu(\varphi)=\int_{X}\varphi\,d\mu\ ,\qquad\mu\in\mathcal{M}_{BV}(X,E)\ ,\quad \varphi\in\mathcal{D}(X)\ .\] (2.2.14)

**Convolutions Involving Vector Measures**

Observe that \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}C_{0} (\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\) implies

\[\mathcal{M}_{BV}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\hookrightarrow\mathcal{S}^{\prime}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ .\] (2.2.15)

Hence we know from Proposition 1.2.7 that the convolution product \(\varphi*\mu\) is a well-defined element of \(\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) for \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\) and \(\mu\in\mathcal{M}_{BV}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\). Moreover,

\[(\varphi*\mu)(x)=\mu(\tau_{x}\dot{\varphi})=\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}\varphi(x-y) \,d\mu(y)\ ,\qquad x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ ,\] (2.2.16)

for \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\) and \(\mu\in\mathcal{M}_{BV}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\). Hence, by (2.2.5),

\[|\varphi*\mu(x)|_{E}\leq\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}|\varphi(x-y)|\,d\,|\mu|\,(y)=| \varphi|*|\mu|\,(x)\ ,\qquad x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ ,\]

so that, by Fubini's theorem,

\[\|\varphi*\mu\|_{1}\leq\|\varphi\|_{1}\,\|\mu\|_{BV}\ ,\qquad\varphi\in\mathcal{ S}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\ ,\quad\mu\in\mathcal{M}_{BV}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ .\] (2.2.17)

**2.2.2 Proposition** _Convolution is a well-defined multiplication_:

\[L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\times\mathcal{M}_{BV}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\to L_{1}( \mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\ .\]

**Proof** This follows from (2.2.17) by continuous extension.

If \(E=\mathbb{K}\) then, thanks to Remark 2.2.1, Proposition 2.2.2 reduces to the well-known fact that \(L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\) is an ideal in the convolution algebra of all \(\mathbb{K}\)-valued Borel measures (e.g., [Pet83, Theorem I.4.5]).

**2.2.3 Lemma** _Suppose \(\varphi\in L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\), \(u\in C_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\), and \(\mu\in\mathcal{M}_{BV}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E^{\prime})\). Then_

\[\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}\varphi*u\,d\mu=\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}u\,d(\dot{\varphi}* \mu)=\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}\langle\dot{\varphi}*\mu,u\rangle_{E}\,dx=\langle \dot{\varphi}*\mu,u\rangle\ .\] (2.2.18)

---

ProofFrom Theorem 1.9.9, Remark 2.2.1, Proposition 2.2.2, and (2.2.4) we infer that each one of the maps that send

\[(\varphi,u,\mu)\in L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\times C_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\times \mathcal{M}_{BV}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E^{\prime})\]

into one of the integrals in (2.2.18) is a trilinear \(\mathbb{K}\)-valued multiplication. Thus, since \(C_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\otimes E\) is dense in \(C_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) by Theorem 1.3.6(x), it suffices to consider the case where \(u=\psi\otimes e\in C_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\otimes E\). Since \(\varphi*(\psi\otimes e)=(\varphi*\psi)\otimes e\) we deduce from (2.2.11), (2.2.16), and Fubini's theorem that

\[\begin{split}\int\varphi*u\,d\mu&=\int\varphi*\psi \,d\langle\mu,e\rangle_{E}=\iint\varphi(x-y)\,d\langle\mu,e\rangle_{E}(x)\psi( y)\,dy\\ &=\int(\check{\varphi}*\langle\mu,e\rangle_{E})\psi\,dx\ .\end{split}\] (2.2.19)

Moreover, thanks to (2.2.8),

\[\begin{split}\check{\varphi}*\langle\mu,e\rangle_{E}(x)& =\int\check{\varphi}(x-y)\,d\langle\mu,e\rangle_{E}(y)=\Big{\langle}\int \check{\varphi}(x-y)\,d\mu(y),e\Big{\rangle}_{E}\\ &=\big{\langle}\check{\varphi}*\mu(x),e\big{\rangle}_{E}\ ,\end{split}\]

so that the last integral in (2.2.19) equals

\[\int\langle\check{\varphi}*\mu(x),\psi(x)\otimes e\big{\rangle}_{E}\,dx=\int \langle\check{\varphi}*\mu,u\rangle_{E}\,dx=\langle\check{\varphi}*\mu,u \rangle\ .\]

This proves the lemma.

#### The Riesz Representation Theorem

Finally, we prove a generalization of the classical (scalar) Riesz Representation Theorem.

**2.2.4 Theorem**: _Let \(X\) be a \(\sigma\)-compact metrizable space and let \(E\) be a Banach space. Then_

\[C_{0}(X,E)^{\prime}=\mathcal{M}_{BV}(X,E^{\prime})\]

_with respect to the duality pairing_

\[\langle\mu,u\rangle_{C_{0}}:=\int_{X}u\,d\mu\ ,\qquad\mu\in\mathcal{M}_{BV}(X,E ^{\prime})\ ,\quad u\in C_{0}(X,E)\ .\]

ProofSuppose that \(\mu\in\mathcal{M}_{BV}(X,E^{\prime})\). Then it follows from (2.2.4) that

\[\big{(}u\mapsto\int u\,d\mu\big{)}\in C_{0}(X,E)^{\prime}\]

---

and

\[\Big{|}\int u\,d\mu\Big{|}\leq\|\mu\|_{BV}\,\|u\|_{\infty}\ \ ,\qquad u\in C_{0}(X,E)\ .\] (2.2.20)

Conversely, let \(w\in C_{0}(X,E)^{\prime}\) be fixed. Then, given any \(e\in E\), the scalar Riesz representation theorem (e.g., [13, Theorems 2.14, 6.2, and 6.19]) implies the existence of a unique regular \(\mathbb{K}\)-valued Radon measure \(\mu_{e}\) on \(X\) satisfying

\[w(\varphi\otimes e)=\int\varphi\,d\mu_{e}\ ,\qquad\varphi\in C_{0}(X)\ ,\] (2.2.21)

and

\[\|\mu_{e}\|_{BV}=\sup_{\|\varphi\|_{\infty}\leq 1}\Big{|}\int\varphi\,d\mu_{e} \Big{|}=\sup_{\|\varphi\|_{\infty}\leq 1}|w(\varphi\otimes e)|\leq\|w\|_{C_{0}^{ \prime}}\,|e|_{E}\ \ .\]

Since, by uniqueness, the map \(E\to(\Cal{B}_{X}\to\mathbb{K})\), \(e\mapsto\mu_{e}\) is linear, it follows that

\[\big{\langle}\mu(B),e\big{\rangle}:=\mu_{e}(B)\ ,\qquad B\in\Cal{B}_{X}\ ,\] (2.2.22)

defines a map \(\mu:\,\Cal{B}_{X}\to E^{\prime}\) that is easily seen to be finitely additive.

In order to show that \(\mu\) has bounded variation we first consider a family \(\{\Cal{O}_{1},\ldots,\Cal{O}_{m}\}\) of pair-wise disjoint open subsets of \(X\). For each \(j\in\{1,\ldots,m\}\) we choose \(\varphi_{j}\in C_{0}(X)\) with \(\operatorname{supp}(\varphi_{j})\subset\Cal{O}_{j}\) and \(\|\varphi_{j}\|_{\infty}\leq 1\), as well as \(e_{j}\in\mathbb{B}_{E}\). Then, putting

\[\varepsilon_{j}:=\operatorname{sign}w(\varphi_{j}\otimes e_{j})\in\mathbb{K}\]

and using (2.2.21),

\[\eqalign{\sum_{j=1}^{m}\Bigl{|}\int_{\Cal{O}_{j}}\varphi_{j}\,d\mu_{e_{j}} \Bigr{|}&=\sum_{j=1}^{m}|w(\varphi_{j}\otimes e_{j})|=\sum_{j=1}^{m} \varepsilon_{j}w(\varphi_{j}\otimes e_{j})\cr&=w\Bigl{(}\sum_{j=1}^{m}\varphi_ {j}\otimes\varepsilon_{j}e_{j}\Bigr{)}\leq\|w\|_{C_{0}^{\prime}}\ .}\]

Consequently,

\[\sum_{j=1}^{m}\big{|}\mu_{e_{j}}\big{|}\,(\Cal{O}_{j})\leq\|w\|_{C_{0}^{\prime }}\ ,\qquad e_{j}\in\mathbb{B}_{E}\ ,\quad 1\leq j\leq m\ .\] (2.2.23)

Now let \(\pi:=\{B_{1},\ldots,B_{m}\}\) be an arbitrary partition of \(X\) into Borel sets. Then, given \(\varepsilon>0\), we find for each \(j\in\{1,\ldots,m\}\) an element \(e_{j}\in\mathbb{B}_{E}\) such that

\[|\mu(B_{j})|_{E^{\prime}}\leq\big{|}\big{\langle}\mu(B_{j}),e_{j}\big{\rangle} _{E}\big{|}+\varepsilon/m\ .\] (2.2.24)

The regularity of \(\mu_{e_{j}}\) implies the existence of a compact subset \(K_{j}\) of \(B_{j}\) with

\[\big{|}\mu_{e_{j}}\big{|}\,(B_{j})\leq\big{|}\mu_{e_{j}}\big{|}\,(K_{j})+ \varepsilon/m\ .\] (2.2.25)

---

Since \(K_{j}\cap K_{k}=\emptyset\) for \(j\neq k\) we find pair-wise disjoint open sets \({\cal O}_{1},\ldots,{\cal O}_{m}\) in \(X\) such that \(K_{j}\subset{\cal O}_{j}\) for \(1\leq j\leq m\). Now we infer from (2.2.23)-(2.2.25) that

\[\sum_{j=1}^{m}|\mu(B_{j})|_{E^{\prime}} \leq\sum_{j=1}^{m}\bigl{|}\bigl{\langle}\mu(B_{j}),e_{j}\bigr{\rangle} _{E}\bigr{|}+\varepsilon\leq\sum_{j=1}^{m}\bigl{|}\mu_{e_{j}}\bigr{|}\,(B_{j})+\varepsilon\] \[\leq\sum_{j=1}^{m}\bigl{|}\mu_{e_{j}}\bigr{|}\,(K_{j})+2\varepsilon \leq\sum_{j=1}^{m}\bigl{|}\mu_{e_{j}}\bigr{|}\,({\cal O}_{j})+2\varepsilon\leq \|w\|_{C^{\prime}_{0}}+2\varepsilon\ .\]

Hence \(\mu\) is of bounded variation.

Let now \((B_{j})\) be a sequence in \({\cal B}_{X}\) such that \(B_{j}\cap B_{k}=\emptyset\) for \(j\neq k\), and put \(B:=\bigcup B_{j}\). Then it is clear that

\[\sum_{j}|\mu(B_{j})|\leq\|\mu\|_{BV}<\infty\ .\]

Thus \(\bigl{\langle}\sum_{j}\mu(B_{j}),e\bigr{\rangle}_{E}\) is well-defined for \(e\in E\) and

\[\Bigl{\langle}\sum_{j}\mu(B_{j}),e\Bigr{\rangle}_{E}=\sum_{j}\bigl{\langle}\mu( B_{j}),e\bigr{\rangle}=\sum_{j}\mu_{e}(B_{j})=\mu_{e}(B)=\bigl{\langle}\mu(B),e \bigr{\rangle}\] (2.2.26)

by the \(\sigma\)-additivity of \(\mu_{e}\). Since (2.2.26) is true for every \(e\in E\), and \(E\) separates the points of \(E^{\prime}\), it follows that \(\mu(B)=\sum_{j}\mu(B_{j})\). Now (2.2.11), (2.2.21), (2.2.22), and the density of \(C_{0}(X)\otimes E\) in \(C_{0}(X,E)\) imply

\[\langle w,u\rangle_{C_{0}}=\int u\,d\mu\ ,\qquad u\in C_{0}(X,E)\ .\]

From this and (2.2.20) the assertion follows.

The above proof of the generalized Riesz representation theorem is due to W. Arendt (personal communication).

### Dyadic Decompositions

Dyadic decompositions are locally finite partitions of unity on \({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\) enjoying particularly 'nice' properties. They are used in the Fourier image of tempered distributions and give a convenient and easy way to control decay properties of Fourier transforms, hence regularity properties of the original distributions. These properties are the basis for introducing the important scales of Besov spaces and for their study.

---

**Elementary Properties of the Fourier Transform**

For the reader's convenience, and for later use, we begin by recalling and extending some of the basic properties of the Fourier transform.

Let \(E\) be a Banach space. Recall that

\[{\cal F}\in{\cal L}{\rm aut}\big{(}{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\big{)}\cap{\cal L }{\rm aut}\big{(}{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\big{)}\] (2.3.1)

and that

\[{\cal F}^{-1}u=(2\pi)^{-n}\,\tilde{\!\tilde{u}}=(2\pi)^{-n}\,\widehat{\!\widehat {u}}\ ,\qquad u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ .\] (2.3.2)

Moreover,

\[\widehat{D^{\alpha}u}=\xi^{\alpha}\widehat{u}\ ,\qquad\alpha\in{\mathbb{N}}^{n} \ ,\quad u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ .\] (2.3.3)

By replacing \(u\) by \({\cal F}^{-1}u\) in (2.3.3) we see that \(D^{\alpha}({\cal F}^{-1}u)={\cal F}^{-1}(\xi^{\alpha}u)\). Thus (2.3.2) implies \(D^{\alpha}\,\widehat{\!\widehat{u}}=\big{(}\widehat{\xi^{\alpha}u}\big{)}\,\tilde {\!\tilde{u}}\,\). Since reflection is its own inverse and

\[D^{\alpha}\widehat{u}=(-1)^{|\alpha|}(D^{\alpha}u)\,\tilde{\!\tilde{u}}\ , \qquad u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ ,\quad\alpha\in{\mathbb{N}}^{n}\ ,\] (2.3.4)

it follows that

\[\widehat{\xi^{\alpha}u}=(-1)^{|\alpha|}D^{\alpha}\widehat{u}\ ,\qquad\quad u\in{ \cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ ,\quad\alpha\in{\mathbb{N}}^{n}\ .\] (2.3.5)

We define the dilation \(\sigma_{t}u\) by \(t>0\) of \(u\in E^{({\mathbb{R}}^{n})}\) by3

Footnote 3: Now we prefer to use definitions (2.3.6) and (2.3.7) instead of [1, (III.4.2.7) and (III.4.2.8)], respectively. This is, of course, simply a matter of convenience.

\[\sigma_{t}u(x):=u(x/t)\ ,\qquad x\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\ ,\] (2.3.6)

and by

\[\sigma_{t}u(\varphi):=t^{n}u(\sigma_{t^{-1}}\varphi)\ ,\qquad u\in{\cal D}^{ \prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ ,\quad\varphi\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,)\ .\] (2.3.7)

Then \(\{\,\sigma_{t}\ ;\ t>0\,\}\), the group of dilations, is strongly continuous on \({\mathfrak{F}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\), where \({\mathfrak{F}}\in\{{\cal D},{\cal D}^{\prime},{\cal E},{\cal E}^{\prime},{\cal S },{\cal S}^{\prime}\}\), and

\[\sigma_{t}^{-1}=\sigma_{1/t}=\sigma_{t^{-1}}\ ,\qquad t>0\ .\] (2.3.8)

It is easily verified that \({\cal F}\sigma_{t}u=t^{n}\sigma_{t^{-1}}{\cal F}u\) for \(u\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) and, thanks to (2.3.7), that this is true for \(u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) as well, that is,

\[{\cal F}\sigma_{t}=t^{n}\sigma_{t^{-1}}{\cal F}\ ,\qquad\quad t>0\ .\] (2.3.9)

From this, (2.3.1), and (2.3.8) we infer that

\[{\cal F}^{-1}\sigma_{t}=t^{n}\sigma_{t^{-1}}{\cal F}^{-1}\ ,\qquad t>0\ .\] (2.3.10)

---

The chain rule implies \(\partial^{\alpha}(\sigma_{t}u)=t^{-|\,\alpha|}\sigma_{t}(\partial^{\alpha}u)\) for \(u\in{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\). Now we infer from (2.3.7) that this formula remains valid for \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\), that is,

\[\partial^{\alpha}\sigma_{t}=t^{-|\,\alpha|}\sigma_{t}\partial^{\alpha}\ ,\qquad t>0\ ,\quad\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\ .\] (2.3.11)

Lastly, it is easily verified that \(L_{p}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) is invariant under \(\{\,\sigma_{t}\ ;\ t>0\,\}\) for \(1\leq p\leq\infty\) and that

\[\left\|\sigma_{t}u\right\|_{p}=t^{n/p}\left\|u\right\|_{p}\ ,\qquad u\in L_{p}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\quad 1\leq p\leq\infty\ ,\quad t>0\ .\] (2.3.12)

#### Dyadic Resolutions of the Identity

Now we introduce the concept of dyadic decompositions. For this we fix a function satisfying the following conditions:

\[\psi\in{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\ ,\quad\psi|\,\mathbb{B}^{n}=1\ ,\quad\mbox{supp}(\psi)\subset 2\mathbb{B}^{n}=:\Omega_{0}\ .\] (2.3.13)

Then we put \(\widetilde{\psi}:=\psi-\sigma_{2^{-1}}\psi=\psi-\psi(2\cdot\,)\) and

\[\psi_{0}:=\psi\ ,\quad\psi_{k}:=\sigma_{2^{k}}\widetilde{\psi}=\psi(2^{-k} \cdot\,)-\psi(2^{-k+1}\cdot\,)\ ,\qquad k\in\lx@overaccentset{{ \bullet}}{\mathbb{N}}\ ,\] (2.3.14)

and

\[\eta_{j}:=\sigma_{2^{j}}\widetilde{\psi}=\psi(2^{-j}\cdot\,)-\psi(2^{-j+1} \cdot\,)\ ,\qquad j\in\mathbb{Z}\ .\] (2.3.15)

We also let

\[\Omega_{k}:=[2^{k-1}\leq|\xi|\leq 2^{k+1}]\ ,\qquad k\in\lx@overaccentset{{\bullet}}{\mathbb{N}}\ ,\] (2.3.16)

and

\[\Sigma_{j}:=[2^{j-1}\leq|\xi|\leq 2^{j+1}]\ ,\qquad j\in\mathbb{Z}\ .\] (2.3.17)

Then it follows that

\[\psi_{k},\eta_{j}\in{\cal D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\ ,\quad\mbox{supp}(\psi_{k})\subset\Omega_{k}\ ,\quad\mbox{supp}(\eta_{j})\subset\Sigma_{j}\] (2.3.18)

for \(k\in\mathbb{N}\) and \(j\in\mathbb{Z}\). Moreover,

\[\sum_{k=0}^{m}\psi_{k}(\xi)=\psi(2^{-m}\xi)\ ,\qquad\xi\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ ,\quad m\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\] (2.3.19)

and

\[\sum_{j=-m^{\prime}}^{m}\eta_{j}(\xi)=\psi(2^{-m}\xi)-\psi(2^{m^{\prime}+1}\xi )\ ,\qquad\xi\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ ,\quad m,m^{\prime}\in\mathbb{N}\ .\] (2.3.20)

Consequently,

\[\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}\psi_{k}(\xi)=1\ ,\qquad\xi\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ ,\] (2.3.21)

---

and

\[\sum_{j=-\infty}^{\infty}\eta_{j}(\xi)=1\ ,\qquad\xi\in(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{*}\ ,\] (2.3.22)

where for each \(\xi\in(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{*}\) at most two terms in the above series are different from zero. Thus \((\psi_{k}):=(\psi_{k})_{k\in\mathbb{N}}\) and \((\eta_{j}):=(\eta_{j})_{j\in\mathbb{Z}}\) are resolutions of the identity on \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) and on \((\mathbb{R}^{n})^{*}\), respectively, the **dyadic resolutions of the identity on \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) and on \((\mathbb{R}^{n})^{*}\)**, respectively, **induced by \(\psi\)**.

#### Basic Properties

Letting \(\psi_{-1}:=0\), put

\[\chi_{k}:=\psi_{k-1}+\psi_{k}+\psi_{k+1}\ ,\quad\lambda_{j}:=\eta_{j-1}+\eta_{ j}+\eta_{j+1}\ ,\qquad k\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\quad j\in\mathbb{Z}\ .\] (2.3.23)

Then it is an easy consequence of (2.3.11) and (2.3.13)-(2.3.18) that

\[\psi_{k}=\psi_{k}\chi_{k}\ ,\quad\eta_{j}=\eta_{j}\lambda_{j}\ ,\qquad k\in \mathbb{N}\ ,\quad j\in\mathbb{Z}\ ,\] (2.3.24)

and

\[2^{k|\alpha|}\,|\partial^{\alpha}\psi_{k}|\leq c(\alpha,\psi)\chi_{\Omega_{k}} \ ,\qquad k\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\quad\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\ ,\] (2.3.25)

as well as

\[2^{j|\alpha|}\,|\partial^{\alpha}\eta_{j}|\leq c(\alpha,\psi)\chi_{\Sigma_{j}} \ ,\qquad j\in\mathbb{Z}\ ,\quad\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\ .\] (2.3.26)

Since \(\psi_{k},\eta_{j}\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) it follows from the Convolution Theorem 1.9.10 and from (1.9.3) that

\[\psi_{k}(D),\eta_{j}(D)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n },E),\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\ ,\qquad k\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\quad j\in \mathbb{Z}\ .\] (2.3.27)

We also infer from (2.3.10) that

\[\mathcal{F}^{-1}\psi_{k}=2^{kn}\sigma_{2^{-k}}\mathcal{F}^{-1}\widetilde{\psi }\ ,\quad\mathcal{F}^{-1}\eta_{j}=2^{jn}\sigma_{2^{-j}}\mathcal{F}^{-1} \widetilde{\psi}\ ,\ \ \ \ k\in\lx@overaccentset{{\star}}{\mathbb{N}}\ ,\quad j\in \mathbb{Z}\ .\] (2.3.28)

Similarly, given \(u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(k\in\lx@overaccentset{{\star}}{\mathbb{N}}\),

\[\psi_{k}(D)u=\mathcal{F}^{-1}\psi_{k}\mathcal{F}u=\mathcal{F}^{-1}\sigma_{2^{ k}}(\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2^{-k}}\mathcal{F}u)=\sigma_{2^{-k}}\mathcal{F}^{-1} \widetilde{\psi}\mathcal{F}\sigma_{2^{k}}u\ .\]

Consequently,

\[\psi_{k}(D)=\sigma_{2^{-k}}\widetilde{\psi}(D)\sigma_{2^{k}}\ ,\quad\eta_{j}(D)= \sigma_{2^{-j}}\widetilde{\psi}(D)\sigma_{2^{j}}\ ,\qquad k\in\lx@overaccentset{{\star}}{\mathbb{N}}\ ,\quad j\in\mathbb{Z}\ .\] (2.3.29)

Since \(\int u\,dx=\widehat{u}(0)\) for \(u\in L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\), it follows that

\[\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}\mathcal{F}^{-1}\psi\,dx=1\ ,\quad\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}} \mathcal{F}^{-1}\psi_{k}\,dx=0\ ,\quad\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}\mathcal{F}^{-1} \eta_{j}\,dx=0\] (2.3.30)

for \(k\in\lx@overaccentset{{\star}}{\mathbb{N}}\) and \(j\in\mathbb{Z}\). Thus, letting \(\varphi_{1}:=\mathcal{F}^{-1}\psi\) and

\[\varphi_{\varepsilon}:=\mathcal{F}^{-1}\sigma_{\varepsilon^{-1}}\psi= \varepsilon^{-n}\sigma_{\varepsilon}\varphi_{1}=\varepsilon^{-n}\varphi_{1}( \varepsilon^{-1}\cdot\,)\qquad\varepsilon>0\ ,\] (2.3.31)

we see that \(\{\,\varphi_{\varepsilon}\ ;\ \varepsilon>0\,\}\) is an approximate identity.

---

Given any \(\varphi\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) and \(\alpha\in{\mathbb{N}}^{n}\),

\[\|\varphi\partial^{\alpha}(\sigma_{2^{m}}\psi-{\bf 1})\|_{\infty}\to 0\qquad{\rm as }\ m\to\infty\ .\] (2.3.32)

Consequently, \(\sigma_{2^{m}}\psi\to{\bf 1}\) in \({\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) as \(m\to\infty\), so that (2.3.19) implies

\[\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}\psi_{k}={\bf 1}\qquad{\rm in}\ {\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n })\ .\] (2.3.33)

Thus, given \(u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\), it follows from Theorem 1.6.4 that \(\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}\psi_{k}\widehat{u}=\widehat{u}\) in \({\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\). Hence we obtain from (2.3.1) that

\[\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}\psi_{k}(D)u=u\quad{\rm in}\ {\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^ {n},E)\ ,\qquad u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ ,\] (2.3.34)

that is,

\[\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}\psi_{k}(D)=1_{{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)}\qquad {\rm in}\ {\cal L}_{s}\big{(}{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\big{)}\ .\] (2.3.35)

This shows that the dyadic resolution of the identity on \({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\) induced by \(\psi\) gives a **dyadic decomposition**

\[u=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}u_{k}\quad{\rm in}\ {\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ , \qquad u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ ,\] (2.3.36)

into smooth functions

\[u_{k}:=\psi_{k}(D)u\in{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ ,\qquad k\in{\mathbb{N}}\ .\] (2.3.37)

Although (2.3.22) is true, it is not true that \(\sum_{j=-\infty}^{\infty}\eta_{j}={\bf 1}\) in \({\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\). This follows from the fact that \(\sigma_{2^{-m}}\psi\) does not converge to zero in \({\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) as \(m\to\infty\). Consequently, a relation analogous to (2.3.34), namely

\[\sum_{j=-\infty}^{\infty}\eta_{j}(D)u=u\qquad{\rm in}\ {\cal S}^{\prime}({ \mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ ,\] (2.3.38)

_does not hold for all \(u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\)._ On the other hand, for each \(\varphi\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) it is obvious that \(\langle\sigma_{2^{-m}}\psi,\varphi\rangle\to 0\) as \(m\to\infty\). Thus the Banach-Steinhaus theorem and the fact that \({\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) is a Montel space imply that \(\sigma_{2^{-m}}\psi\to 0\) in \({\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) as \(m\to\infty\). Together with \(\sigma_{2^{m}}\psi\to{\bf 1}\) in \({\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\hookrightarrow{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) as \(m\to\infty\), this entails, thanks to (2.3.20),

\[\sum_{j=-\infty}^{\infty}\eta_{j}={\bf 1}\qquad{\rm in}\ {\cal S}^{\prime}({ \mathbb{R}}^{n})\ .\] (2.3.39)

---

Now we deduce from Theorem 1.6.4 that

\[\sum_{j=-\infty}^{\infty}\eta_{j}u=u\quad\text{in }\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^ {n},E)\ ,\qquad u\in\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ .\] (2.3.40)

Since \(\mathcal{S}\hookrightarrow\mathcal{O}_{M}\), we infer from (2.3.40) that

\[\sum_{j=-\infty}^{\infty}\eta_{j}(D)u=u\quad\text{in }\mathcal{S}^{\prime}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\qquad u\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\] (2.3.41)

thanks to (2.3.1).

Dyadic decompositions are a standard tool in harmonic analysis and the theory of function spaces (see [10], [11], [12], [13], [14], for example, and the references in these books).

### Multiplier Spaces

In this subsection we prove some simple technical results that will be useful for verifying that certain maps define Fourier multipliers. Since positively homogeneous functions play a particularly important role in this connection, we also introduce spaces of homogeneous maps and collect some examples. For later purposes we allow suitable parameter dependencies.

#### Computing Derivatives

Let \(X\) be a nonempty open subset of \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\), let \(E_{0},E_{1},\ldots,E_{\ell}\) be Banach spaces, and let

\[E_{1}\times\cdots\times E_{\ell}\to E_{0}\ ,\quad(x_{1},\ldots,x_{\ell}) \mapsto x_{1}\bullet\cdots\bullet x_{\ell}\]

be a multiplication. Given \(a_{j}\in C^{k}(X,E_{j})\), it follows from the product rule (1.3.2) by induction that \(a_{1}\bullet\cdots\bullet a_{\ell}\in C^{k}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\) and

\[\partial^{\alpha}(a_{1}\bullet\cdots\bullet a_{\ell})=\sum_{\alpha_{1},\ldots, \alpha_{\ell}\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\atop\alpha_{1}+\cdots+\alpha_{\ell}=\alpha} \partial^{\alpha_{1}}a_{1}\bullet\cdots\bullet\partial^{\alpha_{\ell}}a_{\ell}\] (2.4.1)

for \(|\alpha|\leq k\).

Let \(X\) be a topological space, \(E\) a Banach space, and \(\mathfrak{F}(X,E)\) a LCS satisfying \(\mathfrak{F}(X,E)\hookrightarrow C(X,E)\). Then, given any nonempty subset \(Y\) of \(E\),

\[\mathfrak{F}(X,Y):=\big{\{}\,u\in\mathfrak{F}(X,E)\ ;\ u(X)\subset Y\,\big{\}}\ ,\]

endowed with the topology induced by \(\mathfrak{F}(X,E)\).

---

Let \(F\) be a second Banach space. Recall that \({\cal L}{\rm is}(E,F)\) is open in \({\cal L}(E,F)\) and that the inversion map

\[{\rm inv}:\ {\cal L}{\rm is}(E,F)\to{\cal L}{\rm is}(F,E)\ ,\quad A\mapsto A^{-1}\]

is smooth with

\[\bigl{[}\partial\,{\rm inv}(A)\bigr{]}B=-A^{-1}BA^{-1}\ ,\qquad A\in{\cal L}{\rm is }(E,F)\ ,\quad B\in{\cal L}(E,F),\]

(e.g., [Die69, Theorem (8.3.2) and Example (8.12.11)]).

**2.4.1 Lemma** _Let \(X\) be open in \({{R}}^{n}\) and suppose that \(a\in C^{k}\bigl{(}X,{\cal L}{\rm is}(E,F)\bigr{)}\) for some \(k\in{{N}}\). Then \(a^{-1}\in C^{k}\bigl{(}X,{\cal L}{\rm is}(F,E)\bigr{)}\) and_

\[\partial^{\alpha}a^{-1}=\varepsilon_{\alpha}\sum_{j=1}^{|\alpha|}\sum_{{a_{1}, \ldots,a_{j}\in({{N}}^{n})^{\bullet}\atop a_{1}+\cdots+a_{j}=\alpha}}a^{-1}( \partial^{\alpha_{1}}a)a^{-1}\cdots(\partial^{\alpha_{j}}a)a^{-1}\ ,\qquad| \alpha|\leq k\ ,\]

_where \(\varepsilon_{\alpha}\in\{-1,1\}\)._

**Proof** From (2.4.3) and the chain rule we infer

\[\partial_{j}a^{-1}=-a^{-1}(\partial_{j}a)a^{-1}\ ,\qquad 1\leq j\leq n\ .\]

Now (2.4.1) (with the composition of bounded linear operators as a multiplication) implies the assertion by induction.

**Multilinear Symmetric Maps**

An \(m\)-linear map \(T:\ E^{m}\to F\) is **symmetric** if

\[T(e_{1},\ldots,e_{m})=T(e_{\sigma(1)},\ldots,e_{\sigma(m)})\ ,\qquad(e_{1}, \ldots,e_{m})\in E^{m}\ ,\quad\sigma\in{\sf S}_{m}\ ,\]

where \({\sf S}_{m}\) is the group of all permutations of \(m\) elements. It is clear that

\[{\cal L}^{m}_{\rm sym}(E,F):=\bigl{\{}\,T\in{\cal L}^{m}(E,F)\ ;\ T\ {\rm is \ symmetric}\,\bigr{\}}\]

is a closed linear subspace of \({\cal L}^{m}(E,F)\), hence a Banach space. Recall that, given an open subset \(U\) of \(E\),

\[\partial^{m}f\in C\bigl{(}U,{\cal L}^{m}_{\rm sym}(E,F)\bigr{)}\ ,\qquad f \in C^{m}(U,F)\ .\]

**The Chain Rule**

Now suppose that \(X\) is open in \({{R}}^{n}\) and \(\varphi\in C^{m}(X,U)\). Then \(\varphi^{*}f:=f\circ\varphi\), the pull-back of \(f\) by \(\varphi\), belongs to \(C^{m}(X,F)\) and

\[\partial_{j}(\varphi^{*}f)=(\varphi^{*}\partial f)\partial_{j}\varphi\ ,\qquad 1 \leq j\leq n\ ,\]

by the chain rule. In the following lemma we describe the qualitative form of the higher derivatives of \(\varphi^{*}f\), where we write \([e]^{m}\) for the \(m\)-tuple \((e,e,\ldots,e)\).

---

**2.4.2 Lemma**: _Suppose that \(X\) is open in \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\), that \(U\) is open in \(E\), and that \(\varphi\in C^{m}(X,U)\) and \(f\in C^{m}(U,F)\). Then, given \(\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\) with \(|\alpha|=m>0\),_

\[\partial^{\alpha}(\varphi^{*}f)=\sum c_{\beta,\gamma}\varphi^{*}\partial^{|\beta |}f\big{(}[\partial^{\gamma_{1}}\varphi]^{\beta_{1}},\ldots,[\partial^{\gamma_ {m}}\varphi]^{\beta_{m}}\big{)}\ ,\]

_where the summation extends over all \((\beta,\gamma)\in\mathbb{N}^{m}\times(\mathbb{N}^{n})^{m}\) satisfying \(|\gamma_{i}|>0\) for \(i=1,\ldots,m\) and \(\sum_{i=1}^{m}\beta_{i}\,|\gamma_{i}|=m\). The constants \(c_{\beta,\gamma}\) depend on \(\beta\), \(\gamma\), \(n\), and \(m\) only._

ProofThis follows by induction from the chain rule.

For \(z\in\mathbb{C}\) we put

\[\Lambda^{z}(\xi,\rho):=\Lambda^{z}_{\rho}(\xi):=(|\xi|^{2}+\rho^{2})^{z/2}\ , \qquad(\xi,\rho)\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\times\mathbb{R}\ .\]

Thus \(\Lambda:=\Lambda^{1}\) is the Euclidean norm on \(\mathbb{R}^{n+1}\). (We do not indicate \(n\), the dimension of \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\), in this notation, since its value will always be clear from the context.)

#### Spaces of Multipliers

For \(m\in\mathbb{R}\) and \(\rho\in\mathbb{R}^{+}\) we denote by

\[S^{m}_{\rho}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E):=\big{(}S^{m}_{\rho}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E),\|\cdot\| _{S^{m}_{\rho}}\big{)}\]

the set of all \(a\in C^{n+1}\big{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star},E\big{)}\) such that

\[\|a\|_{S^{m}_{\rho}}:=\max_{|\alpha|\leq n+1}\|\Lambda^{|\alpha|-m}_{\rho} \partial^{\alpha}a\|_{\infty}<\infty\ .\]

It is easily verified that \(S^{m}_{\rho}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) is a Banach space such that

\[S^{m}_{\rho}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\hookrightarrow BC^{n+1}\big{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n}) ^{\star},E\big{)}\hookrightarrow L_{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\qquad m\leq 0\ , \quad\rho>0\ ,\]

and

\[S^{m}_{\rho}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\hookrightarrow W^{n+1}_{1,\mathrm{loc}}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\qquad m>0\ ,\quad\rho>0\ .\]

Each \(a\in S^{m}_{\rho}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) is a regular distribution on \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) whose distributional derivatives of order \(\leq n+1\) are represented on \((\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star}\) by continuous functions. Hence it is obvious that

\[S^{0}_{\rho}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\hookrightarrow S^{0}_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E) \hookrightarrow\mathcal{M}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\qquad\rho\geq 0\ ,\] (2.4.7)

where the norms of these injections are bounded by \(1\) and where \(\mathcal{M}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) is the (\(E\)-valued analogue) of the multiplier space introduced in [1, (III.4.4.6)]. As usual,

\[S^{m}_{\rho}(\mathbb{R}^{n}):=S^{m}_{\rho}(\mathbb{R}^{n},\mathbb{K})\quad \text{and}\quad S^{m}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E):=S^{m}_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ .\]

The reason for considering \(n+1\) derivatives in the definition of \(S^{m}_{\rho}\) will become apparent by looking at the multiplier theorems of Subsection 2.5.

---

In the following,

\[E_{1}\times E_{2}\to E_{0}\ ,\quad(e_{1},e_{2})\mapsto e_{1}\bullet e_{2}\] (2.4.8)

is a multiplication.

**2.4.3 Remarks**:
**(a)**: For \(m_{1},m_{2}\in\mathbb{R}\) point-wise multiplication induced by (2.4.8),

\[S_{\rho}^{m_{1}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\times S_{\rho}^{m_{2}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E _{2})\to S_{\rho}^{m_{1}+m_{2}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\ ,\quad(a_{1},a_{2})\mapsto a _{1}\bullet a_{2}\ ,\]

is well-defined, bilinear, and continuous.

**Proof** Leibniz' rule.
**(b)**: If \(\rho,\sigma>0\) then \(S_{\rho}^{m}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\doteq S_{\sigma}^{m}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and

\[\big{(}1\wedge(\sigma/\rho)^{n+1-m}\big{)}\,\|\!\cdot\!\|_{S_{\rho}^{m}}\leq\| \!\cdot\!\|_{S_{\sigma}^{m}}\leq\big{(}1\vee(\sigma/\rho)^{n+1-m}\big{)}\,\| \!\cdot\!\|_{S_{\rho}^{m}}\ .\]

**Proof** This is implied by the estimate

\[1\wedge(\sigma/\rho)\leq\Lambda_{\sigma}\Lambda_{\rho}^{-1}\leq 1\vee(\sigma/ \rho)\ ,\qquad\rho,\sigma>0\ ,\] (2.4.9)

which is obvious.
**(c)**: Suppose that \(a\in S_{\rho}^{0}\big{(}\mathbb{R}^{n},{\cal L}{\rm is}(E,F)\big{)}\) satisfies \(a^{-1}\in B\big{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star},{\cal L}(F,E)\big{)}\). Then \(a^{-1}\in S_{\rho}^{0}\big{(}\mathbb{R}^{n},{\cal L}(F,E)\big{)}\) and

\[\|a^{-1}\|_{S_{\rho}^{0}}\leq c\big{(}\|a\|_{S_{\rho}^{0}},\|a^{-1}\|_{\infty} \big{)}\ ,\]

where \(c\in C\big{(}(\mathbb{R}^{+})^{2}\big{)}\) is independent of \(a\).
**Proof** This follows from Lemma 2.4.1.
**(d)**: Let \(U\) be open in \(E\) and suppose that \(f\in C^{n+1}(U,F)\) satisfies

\[\|f\|_{n+1,\infty}:=\max_{0\leq k\leq n+1}\|\partial^{k}f\|_{\infty}<\infty\ .\]

Then \(f(a)\in S_{\rho}^{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},F)\) for \(a\in S_{\rho}^{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},U)\), and

\[\|f(a)\|_{S_{\rho}^{0}}\leq c\big{(}\|a\|_{S_{\rho}^{0}}\big{)}\,\|f\|_{n+1, \infty}\ ,\]

where \(c\in C(\mathbb{R}^{+})\) and is independent of \(a\) and \(f\).
**Proof** The assertion is easily deduced from Lemma 2.4.2.
**(e)**: If \(m_{1}\geq m_{0}\) then \(S_{\rho}^{m_{0}}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\hookrightarrow S_{\rho}^{m_{1}}(\mathbb{R} ^{n},E)\) for \(\rho>0\), and the norm of this injection is bounded by \(\rho^{m_{0}-m_{1}}\).

---

**Homogeneous Functions**

The problem of verifying that a given \(a\in C^{n+1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) belongs to \(S_{\rho}^{m}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) and of estimating the corresponding norm can often easily be solved if \(a\) is suitably positively homogeneous. For this reason we now collect some facts about maps of this type.

Let \(V\) and \(W\) be vector spaces and let \(P\) be a cone in \(V\). Then \(a:\,\hat{P}\to W\) is **positively homogeneous of degree \(z\in{\mathbb{C}}\)** if

\[a(tv)=t^{z}a(v)\ ,\qquad t>0\ ,\quad v\in\hat{P}\ .\]

If this is the case and \(\mathop{\rm Re}z>0\), then we always extend \(a\) to a map from \(P\) to \(W\), again denoted by \(a\), by putting \(a(0):=0\).

**2.4.4 Remarks**:
**(a)**: The set of all \(a:\,\hat{P}\to W\) that are positively homogeneous of degree \(z\) for some fixed \(z\in{\mathbb{C}}\) is a vector subspace of \(W^{\hat{P}}\).
**(b)**: If \(a_{j}:\,\hat{P}\to E_{j}\) is positively homogeneous of degree \(z_{j}\) for \(j=1,2\), then the map \(a_{1}\bullet a_{2}:\,\hat{P}\to E_{0}\) is positively homogeneous of degree \(z_{1}+z_{2}\).
**(c)**: Suppose that \(W_{1}\) and \(W_{2}\) are vector spaces and \(a:\,\hat{P}\to\mathop{\rm Hom}(W_{1},W_{2})\) is positively homogeneous of degree \(z\). If \(a(v)\) is bijective for each \(v\in\hat{P}\), then

\[a^{-1}:\,P\to\mathop{\rm Hom}(W_{2},W_{1})\]

is positively homogeneous of degree \(-z\).

**Proof** From

\[1_{W_{1}}=a^{-1}(tv)a(tv)=t^{z}a^{-1}(tv)a(v)\]

and

\[1_{W_{2}}=a(tv)a^{-1}(tv)=a(v)t^{z}a^{-1}(tv)\]

for \(v\in\hat{P}\) and \(t>0\) we see that \(t^{z}a^{-1}(tv)=a^{-1}(v)\), thanks to the uniqueness of the inverse.
**(d)**: Suppose that \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\). Then \(u\) is **positively homogeneous of degree \(z\in{\mathbb{C}}\)** if \(\sigma_{t^{-1}}u=t^{z}u\), \(t>0\). This agrees apparently with the above definition if \(u\) is a regular distribution. If \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) is positively homogeneous of degree \(z\) for some \(z\in{\mathbb{C}}\), then \(u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) and \(\widehat{u}\) and \({\cal F}^{-1}u\) are positively homogeneous of degree \(-z-n\). If, in addition, \(u\in C^{k}\bigl{(}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})^{\star},E\bigr{)}\) for some \(k>\mathop{\rm Re}z+n\), then \(\partial^{\alpha}\widehat{u}\in C\bigl{(}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})^{\star},E\bigr{)}\) for \(|\alpha|<k-\mathop{\rm Re}z-n\).

**Proof** The proof of [Hor83, Theorem 7.1.18] carries over to the \(E\)-valued case to show that \(u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) and that the last assertion is true. Hence we can apply (2.3.9) to obtain

\[t^{z}{\cal F}u={\cal F}(t^{z}u)={\cal F}(\sigma_{t^{-1}}u)=t^{n}\sigma_{t}{ \cal F}u\ .\]

---

Consequently, \(\sigma_{t^{-1}}\widehat{u}=t^{-z-n}\,\widehat{u}\). Thus, by replacing \(\mathcal{F}\) by \(\mathcal{F}^{-1}\) in this argument, we obtain the second assertion.

Now we suppose that \(\mathsf{H}\) is a cone in some Banach space \(\mathsf{H}:=(\mathsf{H},|\cdot|)\). Then

\[\mathbb{R}^{n}\,\times\,\mathsf{H}\to\mathbb{R}\ ,\quad(\xi,\eta)\mapsto \Lambda(\xi,|\eta|)\]

is the 'Euclidean' norm on \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,\times\,\mathsf{H}\) and

\[(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,\times\,\mathsf{H})^{\ast}\to\left[\Lambda(\xi,|\eta|)=1 \right]\,,\quad(\xi,\eta)\mapsto(\xi^{\ast},\eta^{\ast}):=(\xi,\eta)\Lambda^{-1 }(\xi,|\eta|)\]

is the '**Euclidean' radial retraction** onto the unit-sphere of \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,\times\,\mathsf{H}\).

For \(a:\,(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\ast}\times\mathsf{H}\to E\) we often set

\[a_{\eta}:=a(\cdot,\eta):\,(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\ast}\to E\ .\]

Of course, if \(\mathsf{H}=\{0\}\), then we identify \((\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\ast}\times\mathsf{H}\) and \((\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\ast}\) and omit any reference to \(\eta=0\).

**2.4.5 Lemma**: _Suppose that \(a:\,(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\ast}\times\mathsf{H}\to E\) is positively homogeneous of degree \(z\) for some \(z\in\mathbb{C}\) and that \(a_{\eta}\in C^{k}\big{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\ast},E\big{)}\) for some \(k\in\overleftarrow{\mathbb{N}}\) and each \(\eta\in\mathsf{H}\). Then \(\partial_{\xi}^{\alpha}a:\,(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\ast}\times\mathsf{H}\to E\) is positively homogeneous of degree \(z-|\alpha|\) and_

\[\partial^{\alpha}a_{\eta}(\xi)=\Lambda^{z-|\alpha|}(\xi,|\eta|)\partial_{\xi}^ {\alpha}a(\xi^{\ast},\eta^{\ast})\ ,\qquad(\xi,\eta)\in(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\ast} \times\mathsf{H}\ ,\]

_for \(\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\) with \(|\alpha|\leq k\)._

ProofBy differentiating the identity \(a(t\xi,t\eta)=t^{z}a(\xi,\eta)\) with respect to \(\xi\) it follows that

\[(\partial_{\xi}^{\alpha}a)(t\xi,t\eta)=t^{z-|\alpha|}\partial_{\xi}^{\alpha}a( \xi,\eta)\ ,\qquad t>0\ ,\quad(\xi,\eta)\in(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\ast}\times \mathsf{H}\ ,\]

proving the first assertion. The second one follows now if we replace \((\xi,\eta)\) by \((\xi^{\ast},\eta^{\ast})\) and \(t\) by \(\Lambda(\xi,|\eta|)\), respectively.

#### Spaces of Homogeneous Functions and Multipliers

For \(k\in\mathbb{N}\) and \(z\in\mathbb{C}\) we denote by

\[\mathcal{H}^{k}_{z}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,\times\,\mathsf{H},E):=\big{(}\mathcal{H} ^{k}_{z}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,\times\,\mathsf{H},E),\|\cdot\|_{\mathcal{H}^{k}_{z} }\big{)}\]

the normed vector space of all \(a:\,(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\ast}\times\mathsf{H}\to E\) that are positively homogeneous of degree \(z\) and satisfy \(a_{\eta}\in C^{k}\big{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\ast},E\big{)}\) for \(\eta\in\mathsf{H}\) as well as

\[\|a\|_{\mathcal{H}^{k}_{z}}:=\max_{|\alpha|\leq k}\sup_{(\xi,\eta)\in(\mathbb{ R}^{n})^{\ast}\times\mathsf{H}}|\partial_{\xi}^{\alpha}a(\xi^{\ast},\eta^{ \ast})|<\infty\ .\]

---

Moreover,

\[{\cal H}^{\infty}_{z}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}},E):=\bigcap_{k=0}^{ \infty}{\cal H}^{k}_{z}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}},E)\ ,\]

equipped with the obvious projective limit topology. As usual,

\[{\cal H}^{k}_{z}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}}):={\cal H}^{k}_{z}({ \mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}},{\mathbb{K}})\ ,\qquad k\in\bar{\mathbb{N}}\ ,\]

and

\[{\cal H}_{z}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}},E):={\cal H}^{n+1}_{z}({ \mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}},E)\ ,\qquad z\in{\mathbb{C}}\ .\]

**2.4.6 Remarks**: **(a)**: \((a\mapsto a_{\eta})\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\cal H}_{z}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{ \mathsf{H}},E),S^{{\rm Re}\,z}_{|\eta|}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\bigr{)}\) with the norm bounded by \(1\).

**Proof** This is a consequence of Lemma 2.4.5.

**(b)**: Point-wise multiplication induced by (2.4.8)

\[{\cal H}^{k}_{z_{1}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}},E_{1})\times{\cal H}^ {k}_{z_{2}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}},E_{2})\to{\cal H}^{k}_{z_{1}+z_ {2}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}},E_{0})\ ,\quad(a_{1},a_{2})\mapsto a_{1} \bullet a_{2}\]

is well-defined, bilinear, and continuous for \(k\in\bar{\mathbb{N}}\) and \(z_{1},z_{2}\in{\mathbb{C}}\).

**Proof** Leibniz' rule and Remark 2.4.4(b).

**(c)**: Suppose that \(a\in{\cal H}^{k}_{z}\bigl{(}{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}},{\cal L}(E,F) \bigr{)}\) satisfies \(a(\xi^{*},\eta^{*})\in{\cal L}{\rm is}(E,F)\) and

\[\|a^{-1}\|_{\infty,[\Lambda=1]}=\sup_{(\xi,\eta)\in({\mathbb{R}}^{n})^{*} \times{\mathsf{H}}}|a^{-1}(\xi^{*},\eta^{*})|_{{\cal L}(F,E)}<\infty\ .\]

Then \(a^{-1}\in{\cal H}^{k}_{-z}\bigl{(}{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}},{\cal L}( F,E)\bigr{)}\) and

\[\|a^{-1}\|_{{\cal H}^{k}_{-z}}\leq c\bigl{(}\|a\|_{{\cal H}^{k}_{z}},\|a^{-1}\|_ {\infty,[\Lambda=1]}\bigr{)}\ ,\]

where \(c\in C\bigl{(}({\mathbb{R}}^{+})^{2}\bigr{)}\).

**Proof** It follows from Remark 2.4.4(c) that \(a^{-1}\) is well-defined as a map from \(({\mathbb{R}}^{n})^{*}\times{\mathsf{H}}\) into \({\cal L}(F,E)\) and is positively homogeneous. Now the assertion is a consequence of Lemma 2.4.1.

To conclude, we consider three simple but most important examples. They will be the basis for many computations in connection with Fourier multipliers.

**2.4.7 Examples**: **(a)**: \(\bigl{[}(\xi,\eta)\mapsto\Lambda^{z}(\xi,|\eta|)\bigr{]}\in{\cal H}^{\infty}_ {z}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathsf{H}})\) and

\[\|\Lambda^{z}\|_{{\cal H}^{k}_{z}}\leq c(k)(1+|z|^{k})\ ,\qquad z\in{\mathbb{C}}\ , \quad k\in{\mathbb{N}}\ .\]

**Proof** First we note that \(\Lambda_{|\eta|}\in C^{\infty}\bigl{(}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})^{*}\bigr{)}\) for \(\eta\in{\mathsf{H}}\) and that \(\Lambda\) is positively homogeneous of degree \(1\). Moreover,

\[\partial_{j}\Lambda_{|\eta|}(\xi)=\xi_{j}\Lambda_{|\eta|}^{-1}(\xi)\ ,\qquad\xi=(\xi_{1},\ldots,\xi_{n})\in({\mathbb{R}}^{n})^{*}\ ,\quad\eta\in{\mathsf{H}}\ ,\quad 1\leq j\leq n\ .\]

---

Now we infer from (2.4.1) and Lemma 2.4.1 that

\[|\partial_{\xi}^{\alpha}\Lambda(\xi^{*},|\eta^{*}|)|\leq c(\alpha)\ ,\qquad\alpha \in\mathbb{N}^{n}\ .\]

Consequently, \(\Lambda\in\mathcal{H}_{1}^{k}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\times\mathsf{H})\) and \(\|\Lambda\|_{\mathcal{H}_{1}^{k}}\leq c(k)\). Next we put \(f(t):=t^{z}\) for \(z\in\mathbb{C}\). Then \(f\in C^{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{+})\) and

\[\partial^{j}f(t)=z(z-1)\cdots(z-j+1)t^{z-j}\ ,\qquad t>0\ ,\quad j\in\mathbb{N}\ .\]

Hence \(|\partial^{j}f(1)|\leq c(j)(1+|z|^{j})\), and the assertion follows from \(\Lambda^{z}=f(\Lambda)\) and Lemma 2.4.2.

**(b)**: If \(\eta\in\mathsf{H}\) then \(\Lambda^{z}_{|\eta|}\in\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\).

**Proof** We obtain the assertion from (a) and Lemma 2.4.5 thanks to the fact that \(\Lambda^{z}_{\rho}\in C^{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) and \(\Lambda_{\rho}\geq\rho\).

**(c)**: Let \(a_{\alpha}:\mathsf{H}\to E\) be positively homogeneous of degree \(m-|\alpha|\) for \(|\alpha|\leq m\) and put

\[a(\xi,\eta):=\sum_{|\alpha|\leq m}a_{\alpha}(\eta)\xi^{\alpha}\ ,\qquad(\xi, \eta)\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\times\mathsf{H}\ .\]

Then \(a\in\mathcal{H}_{m}^{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\times\mathsf{H},E)\) and

\[\|a\|_{\mathcal{H}_{m}^{k}}\leq c\max_{|\alpha|\leq m}\sup_{|\eta|\leq 1}|a_{ \alpha}(\eta)|\ ,\qquad k\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\]

since \(\partial^{\alpha}a_{\eta}=0\) for \(|\alpha|>m\).

Below we often identify \(\Lambda^{z}\) with \(\Lambda^{z}1_{E}\). In other words, we consider \(\Lambda^{z}\) as an element of \(\mathcal{H}_{z}^{\infty}\big{(}\mathbb{R}^{n}\times\mathsf{H},\mathcal{L}(E) \big{)}\). Then the estimates of Example 2.4.7(a) are valid in this case as well.

### Fourier Multipliers

In this subsection we derive some most important multiplier theorems for operator-valued symbols, of which Lemma 2.5.3 has to be especially mentioned. It entails, in particular, Lemma 2.5.6 that develops its full power in the framework of Besov spaces.

First we recall the multinomial theorem:

\[(x_{1}+\cdots+x_{n})^{m}=\sum_{|\alpha|=m}\frac{m!}{\alpha!}x^{\alpha}\ , \qquad m\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\quad n\in\lx@overaccentset{{\star}}{ \mathbb{N}}\ ,\quad x_{j}\in\mathbb{K}\ .\] (2.5.1)

---

Moreover, putting

\[{m\choose\alpha}:=\frac{m!}{\alpha!\,(m-|\alpha|)!}\ ,\qquad m\in\mathbb{N}\ , \quad\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\ ,\]

it follows from (2.5.1) that

\[(1+x_{1}+\cdots+x_{n})^{m}=\sum_{|\alpha|\leq m}{m\choose\alpha}x^{\alpha}\ , \qquad m\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\quad n\in\lx@overaccentset{{\bullet}}{ \mathbb{N}}\ ,\quad x_{j}\in\mathbb{K}\ .\] (2.5.2)

Observe that these formulas remain valid if \(\mathbb{K}\) is replaced by an algebra with unit, and \(x_{1},\ldots,x_{m}\) by commutative elements thereof.

#### The Multiplier Space \(\mathcal{F}L_{1}\)

Now let \(E:=(E,|\cdot|)\) be a Banach space. We put

\[\mathcal{F}L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E):=\Big{(}\big{\{}\,u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime} (\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ;\ \mathcal{F}^{-1}u\in L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\,\big{\}},\ \|\!\cdot\!\|_{\mathcal{F}L_{1}}\Big{)}\ ,\]

where

\[\|u\|_{\mathcal{F}L_{1}}:=\|\mathcal{F}^{-1}u\|_{1}\ .\] (2.5.3)

As usual,

\[\mathcal{F}L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n}):=\mathcal{F}L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},\mathbb{C})\ .\]

#### Proposition

* \(\mathcal{F}L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,,E)\) _is a Banach space and_ \[\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}} \mathcal{F}L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}C_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ .\] (2.5.4)
* _If_ \(E\) _is a Hilbert space and_ \(k>n/2\)_, then_ \[W^{k}_{2}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}} \mathcal{F}L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ .\] _Moreover,_ \[\|u\|_{\mathcal{F}L_{1}}\leq c\,\|u\|_{2}^{1-\theta}\,\Big{(}\max_{|\alpha|=k} \|\partial^{\alpha}u\|_{2}\Big{)}^{\theta}\leq c\,\|u\|_{2,k}\ ,\qquad u\in W^{k}_{2}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\] _where_ \(\theta:=n/(2k)\)_._

Proof.: (i) It is obvious that \(\mathcal{F}L_{1}\) is a Banach space. Thanks to the Riemann-Lebesgue lemma,

\[\|u\|_{C_{0}}=\big{\|}\widehat{\mathcal{F}^{-1}u}\big{\|}_{C_{0}}\leq c\,\| \mathcal{F}^{-1}u\|_{1}=c\,\|u\|_{\mathcal{F}L_{1}}\ ,\qquad u\in\mathcal{F}L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\]

hence \(\mathcal{F}L_{1}\hookrightarrow C_{0}\). Since \(\mathcal{S}\) is dense in \(L_{1}\) and \(\mathcal{F}\in\mathcal{L}\mathrm{aut}(\mathcal{S})\cap\mathcal{L}\mathrm{is}(L_ {1},\mathcal{F}L_{1})\), we infer that \(\mathcal{S}\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}\mathcal{F}L_{1}\). Consequently, (2.5.4) follows from \(\mathcal{S}\stackrel{{ d}}{{\hookrightarrow}}C_{0}\).

---

(ii) Let \(t>0\). By the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality and since \(\mathcal{F}^{-1}u=(2\pi)^{-n}\,\tilde{\vec{u}}\),

\[\int_{|x|>t}|\mathcal{F}^{-1}u|\,dx =(2\pi)^{-n}\int_{|\xi|>t}|\xi|^{-k}\,|\xi|^{k}\,|\widehat{u}(\xi)| \,d\xi\] \[\leq(2\pi)^{-n}\Bigl{(}\int_{|\xi|>t}|\xi|^{-2k}\,d\xi\Bigr{)}^{1/ 2}\Bigl{(}\int_{|\xi|>t}|\xi|^{2k}\,|\widehat{u}(\xi)|^{2}\,d\xi\Bigr{)}^{1/2}\] \[\leq ct^{n/2-k}\max_{|\alpha|=k}\|D^{\alpha}u\|_{2}\ ,\]

where we used (2.5.1) and Plancherel's theorem in the last step. Similarly, we find

\[\int_{|x|\leq t}|\mathcal{F}^{-1}u|\,dx\leq(2\pi)^{-n}\Bigl{(}\int_{|\xi|\leq t }1\,d\xi\Bigr{)}^{1/2}\Bigl{(}\int_{|\xi|\leq t}|\widehat{u}(\xi)|^{2}\,d\xi \Bigr{)}^{1/2}\leq ct^{n/2}\,\|u\|_{2}\ .\]

Thus

\[\|\mathcal{F}^{-1}u\|_{1}\leq ct^{n/2}\Bigl{(}\|u\|_{2}+t^{-k}\max_{|\alpha|=k} \|\partial^{\alpha}u\|_{2}\Bigr{)}\ ,\qquad t>0\ ,\]

and the assertion follows by estimating the minimum of the right-hand side with respect to \(t>0\).

#### Multiplier Theorems

Now suppose that \(E_{j},\ \ j=0,1,2\), are Banach spaces and

\[E_{1}\times E_{2}\to E_{0}\ ,\quad(e_{1},e_{2})\mapsto e_{1}\bullet e_{2}\] (2.5.5)

is a multiplication. The following simple multiplier result is the reason for introducing the space \(\mathcal{F}L_{1}\).

**2.5.2 Theorem**: _Suppose that \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\,BUC,C_{0},L_{p}\ ;\ 1\leq p<\infty\,\}\). Then_

\[\bigl{[}a\mapsto a(D)\bigr{]}\in\mathcal{L}\Bigl{(}\mathcal{F}L_{1}(\mathbb{R} ^{n}\,,E_{1}),\mathcal{L}\bigl{(}\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2}),\mathfrak{ F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{0})\bigr{)}\Bigr{)}\ .\]

_Moreover,_

\[\bigl{[}a\mapsto a(D)\bigr{]}\in\mathcal{L}\Bigl{(}\mathcal{F}L_{1}(\mathbb{R} ^{n}\,,E_{1}),\mathcal{L}\bigl{(}L_{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{2}),BUC(\mathbb{ R}^{n},E_{0})\bigr{)}\Bigr{)}\ .\]

_The norms of these linear maps are bounded by \(1\)._

**Proof** This follows immediately from Theorem 1.9.9 and Remark 1.9.11(b).

Now we prove some criteria for a distribution to belong to \(\mathcal{F}L_{1}\). They will play an important role in the sequel. For this we fix \(\psi\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) satisfying (2.3.13) and denote by \((\psi_{k})\) and \((\eta_{j})\) the dyadic resolutions on \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) and on \((\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star}\), respectively, induced by \(\psi\). We also recall that \(\Omega_{0}:=2\mathbb{B}^{n}\) and \(\Sigma_{j}:=2^{j+1}\bar{\mathbb{B}}^{n}\setminus(2^{j-1}\mathbb{B}^{n})\) for \(j\in\mathbb{Z}\).

---

**2.5.3 Lemma**: _Suppose that \(a\in{\cal D}^{\prime}\big{(}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})^{\raise 1.0pt\hbox{\tiny{$ \bullet$}}},E\big{)}\) and all its derivatives of order less than or equal to \(n+1\) are regular distributions._

* _Given_ \(j\in{\mathbb{Z}}\)_, suppose that_ \[\mu_{j}:=\max_{|\alpha|\leq n+1}\|\Lambda_{0}^{|\alpha|}\partial^{\alpha}a\|_{ \infty,\Sigma_{j}}<\infty\ .\] _Then_ \(\eta_{j}a\in{\cal F}L_{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\) _and_ \[\|\eta_{j}a\|_{{\cal F}L_{1}}\leq c\mu_{j}\ ,\] _where_ \(c=c(n,\psi)\) _is independent of_ \(a\) _and_ \(j\)_._
* _If_ \(a\in W_{\infty}^{n+1}(\Omega_{0},E)\)_, then_ \(\psi a\in{\cal F}L_{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,,E)\) _and_ \[\|\psi a\|_{{\cal F}L_{1}}\leq c\,\|a\|_{n+1,\infty,\Omega_{0}}\ ,\] _where_ \(c\) _is independent of_ \(a\)_._

**Proof** (i) Since \(\eta_{j}a=(\sigma_{2^{j}}\widetilde{\psi})a=\sigma_{2^{j}}(\widetilde{\psi} \sigma_{2^{-j}}a)\), it follows from (2.3.10) that

\[{\cal F}^{-1}(\eta_{j}a)=2^{jn}\sigma_{2^{-j}}{\cal F}^{-1}(\widetilde{\psi} \sigma_{2^{-j}}a)\ .\]

Now we obtain from (2.3.12) and (2.5.3) that

\[\|\eta_{j}a\|_{{\cal F}L_{1}}=\|\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2^{-j}}a\|_{{\cal F}L_ {1}}\ .\] (2.5.6)

Leibniz' rule and (2.3.11) imply

\[\partial^{\alpha}(\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2^{-j}}a)=\sum_{\beta\leq\alpha}{ \alpha\choose\beta}2^{j|\beta|}(\sigma_{2^{-j}}\partial^{\beta}a)\partial^{ \alpha-\beta}\widetilde{\psi}\ .\]

Thus, since \(\hbox{supp}(\partial^{\alpha-\beta}\widetilde{\psi})\subset\Sigma_{0}\), it follows that

\[|\partial^{\alpha}(\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2^{-j}}a)(\xi)|\leq c\sum_{\beta \leq\alpha}2^{j|\beta|}\,|\partial^{\beta}a(2^{j}\xi)|\,\chi_{\Sigma_{0}}(\xi )\leq c\mu_{j}\chi_{\Sigma_{0}}(\xi)\]

for \(|\alpha|\leq n+1\) and a.a. \(\xi\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\). Hence \(\partial^{\alpha}(\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2^{-j}}a)\in L_{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n} \,,E)\) and

\[\|\partial^{\alpha}(\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2^{-j}}a)\|_{1}\leq c\mu_{j}\ , \qquad|\alpha|\leq n+1\ .\] (2.5.7)

Consequently, by (2.3.2), (2.3.3), and the Riemann-Lebesgue lemma,

\[x^{\alpha}{\cal F}^{-1}(\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2^{-j}}a)={\cal F}^{-1}\big{(} D^{\alpha}(\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2^{-j}}a)\big{)}\in C_{0}({\mathbb{R}}^{n} \,,E)\]

and

\[|x^{\alpha}{\cal F}^{-1}(\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2^{-j}}a)(x)|\leq c\mu_{j}\ , \qquad x\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\ ,\quad|\alpha|\leq n+1\ .\] (2.5.8)

---

Since, by (2.5.1),

\[|x|^{k}=\big{(}(x_{1}^{2}+\cdots+x_{n}^{2})^{k}\big{)}^{1/2}\leq c(k,n)\sum_{| \alpha|=k}|x^{\alpha}|\ ,\qquad k\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\] (2.5.9)

it follows from (2.5.8) that

\[|\mathcal{F}^{-1}\big{(}\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2-j}\,a)(x)|\leq c\mu_{j}\,|x| ^{-n-1}\ \ ,\qquad x\in(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star}\ .\] (2.5.10)

Hence, thanks to (2.5.8) and (2.5.10),

\[\|\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2-j}\,a\|_{\mathcal{F}L_{1}}=\|\mathcal{F}^{-1} \big{(}\widetilde{\psi}\sigma_{2-j}\,a\big{)}\|_{1}\leq c\mu_{j}\Big{[}\int_{| x|\leq 1}1\,dx+\int_{|x|\geq 1}|x|^{-n-1}\,dx\Big{]}=c\mu_{j}\ .\]

Now the assertion is a consequence of (2.5.6).

(ii) From Leibniz' rule we infer that

\[|\partial^{\alpha}(\psi a)|\leq c\sum_{\beta\leq\alpha}|\partial^{\beta}a|\, \chi_{\Omega_{0}}\,\leq c\,\|a\|_{n+1,\infty,\Omega_{0}}\,\chi_{\Omega_{0}}\]

for \(|\alpha|\leq n+1\). Now the arguments of (i) apply if we replace \(\widetilde{\psi}\) by \(\psi\) and set \(j:=0\) and \(\mu_{0}:=\|a\|_{n+1,\infty,\Omega_{0}}\).

**2.5.4 Theorem**: _Suppose that \(a\in L_{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and all derivatives of order \(\leq n+1\) are regular distributions on \((\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star}\). Put_

\[\mu_{j}:=\max_{|\alpha|\leq n+1}\|\Lambda_{0}^{|\alpha|}\partial^{\alpha}a\|_{ \infty,\Sigma_{j}}\ ,\qquad j\in\mathbb{Z}\ ,\]

_and_

\[\lambda_{0}:=\max_{|\alpha|\leq n+1}\|\partial^{\alpha}a\|_{\infty,\Omega_{0}} \ .\]

_Also suppose that_

\[\kappa:=\Big{(}\sum_{j=-\infty}^{\infty}\mu_{j}\Big{)}\wedge\Big{(}\lambda_{0} +\sum_{j=1}^{\infty}\mu_{j}\Big{)}<\infty\ .\]

_Then \(a\in\mathcal{F}L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(\|a\|_{\mathcal{F}L_{1}}\leq c\kappa\), where \(c\) is independent of \(\kappa\) and \(a\)._

**Proof** Given \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), it follows from (2.3.20) and Lebesgue's theorem that

\[\Big{(}\sum_{j=-k^{\prime}}^{k}\eta_{j}a\Big{)}(\varphi)=\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}} a\big{(}\psi(2^{-k}\cdot)-\psi(2^{k^{\prime}+1}\cdot)\big{)}\varphi\,dx\to a (\varphi)\]

as \(k,k^{\prime}\to\infty\). Thus the Banach-Steinhaus theorem and the fact that \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) is a Montel space imply

\[\sum_{j=-\infty}^{\infty}\eta_{j}a=a\qquad\text{in }\mathcal{S}^{\prime}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ .\] (2.5.11)

---

Similarly,

\[\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}\psi_{k}a=a\qquad\text{in }{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ .\] (2.5.12)

Lemma 2.5.3 gives

\[\sum_{j=-k^{\prime}}^{k}\|\eta_{j}a\|_{{\cal F}L_{1}}\leq c\sum_{j=-\infty}^{ \infty}\mu_{j}\]

and, thanks to \(\psi_{j}=\eta_{j}\) for \(j\in{\mathbb{N}}\),

\[\sum_{j=0}^{k}\|\psi_{k}a\|_{{\cal F}L_{1}}\leq\lambda_{0}+c\sum_{j=0}^{\infty }\mu_{j}\]

for \(k,k^{\prime}\in{\mathbb{N}}\). Hence the series on the left-hand sides of (2.5.11) and (2.5.12) converge in the Banach space \({\cal F}L_{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) if the right-hand sides in the corresponding estimates are finite. Now the assertion follows from the continuous injection of \({\cal F}L_{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) in \({\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) and from (2.5.11) and (2.5.12), respectively.

**2.5.5 Corollary**: _Suppose that \(a\in W_{\infty}^{n+1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) and there exists \(\varepsilon>0\) such that_

\[\|a\|_{n+1,\infty}+\max_{|\alpha|\leq n+1}\|\Lambda_{0}^{|\alpha|+\varepsilon} \partial^{\alpha}a\|_{\infty}\leq\mu<\infty\ .\]

_Then \(a\in{\cal F}L_{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) and the estimate \(\|a\|_{{\cal F}L_{1}}\leq c\mu\) holds, where \(c:=c(\varepsilon,n)\) is independent of \(\mu\) and \(a\)._

**Proof** Note that \(\lambda_{0}\leq\|a\|_{n+1,\infty}\leq\mu\) and

\[\max_{|\alpha|\leq n+1}\left|\xi\right|^{\alpha}\left|\partial^{\alpha}a(\xi) \right|\leq\mu\left|\xi\right|^{-\varepsilon}\ ,\qquad\text{a.a.}\ \xi\in({ \mathbb{R}}^{n})^{c}\ .\]

Hence \(\mu_{j}\leq\mu 2^{-(j-1)\varepsilon}\) for \(j\in\overset{\centerdot}{\mathbb{N}}\) so that \(\kappa\leq\mu\bigl{[}1+(1-2^{-\varepsilon})^{-1}\bigr{]}\).

It should be noted that there is no restriction on the Banach space \(E\). This is in contrast to Proposition 2.5.1(ii) where \(E\) has to be a Hilbert space since its proof is based on Plancherel's theorem.

**2.5.6 Theorem**: _Suppose that \(\psi\in{\cal D}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) with \(\psi|\,{\mathbb{B}}^{n}=1\) and \(\operatorname{supp}(\psi)\subset 2{\mathbb{B}}^{n}\), and let \((\psi_{k})\) be the dyadic resolution of the identity on \({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\) induced by \(\psi\). Also suppose that_

\[{\mathfrak{F}}\in\{\,\mbox{BUC},C_{0},L_{p}\ ;\ 1\leq p<\infty\,\}\ .\]

_Then_

\[(\psi_{k}a)(D)\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}{\mathfrak{F}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{2}),{ \mathfrak{F}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{0})\bigr{)}\]

_and_

\[(\psi_{k}a)(D)\in{\cal L}\bigl{(}L_{\infty}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{2}),\mbox{BUC} ({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E_{0})\bigr{)}\]

---

_for \(a\in S^{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\), and_

\[\sup_{k\in\mathbb{N}}\|(\psi_{k}a)(D)\|\leq c\,\|a\|_{S^{0}}\ \ ,\qquad a\in S^{0}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E_{1})\ ,\]

_where \(c\) is independent of \(a\)._

**Proof** If \(a\in S^{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) then \(\|a\|_{n+1,\infty}\leq\|a\|_{S^{0}}\) and \(\mu_{j}\leq\|a\|_{S^{0}}\) for \(j\in\mathbb{N}\). Hence the assertion follows from Lemma 2.5.3 and Theorem 2.5.2.

**2.5.7 Corollary** _Given the hypotheses of Theorem 2.5.6,_

\[\psi_{k}(D)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\cap \mathcal{L}\big{(}L_{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E),BUC(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\ , \qquad k\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\]

_and \(\sup_{k\in\mathbb{N}}\|\psi_{k}(D)\|<\infty\)._

**Proof** Choose \(a:=1_{E}\).

Multiplier theorems of the type of Theorem 2.5.4 and its corollary are well-known in the scalar case (e.g., [1, Exercise 6.8.3] or [15, Lemma 3.3]). In this case the proof is usually based on Proposition 2.5.1(ii) so that the order of differentiation can be reduced from \(n+1\) to \([n/2]+1\). This requires, however, the use of Plancherel's theorem that is valid only if \(E\) is a Hilbert space. (Thus Lemma 6.1.5 in [1] does not hold for \(E=\mathcal{L}(H_{0},H_{1})\), in general, since \(\mathcal{L}(H_{0},H_{1})\) is not a Hilbert space if \(H_{0}\) and \(H_{1}\) are infinite-dimensional Hilbert spaces.) For our purpose it will be important that in Corollary 2.5.5 and Theorem 2.5.6 we do not have to impose restrictions on the Banach space \(E\). These results are due to the author [1].

#### A Limiting Case

We close this subsection by proving that Corollary 2.5.5 is close to being optimal in the sense that we cannot expect to obtain a similar result with \(\varepsilon=0\).

**2.5.8 Proposition** _Suppose \(a\in L_{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\cap C^{n+1}\big{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star} \big{)}\) is a Fourier multiplier for \(\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), where \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{BUC,C_{0},L_{1},L_{\infty}\}\). If \(a\) is positively homogeneous of degree \(0\) then \(a\) is constant._

**Proof** By assumption \(a(D)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\big{)}\) and

\[\|\mathcal{F}^{-1}a\ast u\|_{\mathfrak{F}}\leq c\,\|u\|_{\mathfrak{F}}\ ,\qquad u\in\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\ .\] (2.5.13)

First we suppose that \(\mathfrak{F}\neq L_{1}\). If \(\varphi\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) then we know that \(\mathcal{F}^{-1}a\ast\varphi\in\mathcal{E}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) and that \(\mathcal{F}^{-1}a\ast\varphi(0)=\langle\mathcal{F}^{-1}a,\varphi\rangle\). Hence it follows from (2.5.13) that

\[|\langle\mathcal{F}^{-1}a,\varphi\rangle|\leq c\,\|\varphi\|_{\infty}\ ,\qquad\varphi\in\mathcal{D}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\ .\] (2.5.14)
