By restricting ourselves to \(\varphi\in\mathcal{D}\big{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star}\big{)}\) and by using the density of \(\mathcal{D}\big{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star}\big{)}\) in \(C_{0}\big{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star}\big{)}\) we deduce from (2.5.14) that \(f:=\mathcal{F}^{-1}a\,|\,(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star}\) belongs to \(C_{0}^{\prime}\big{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\star}\big{)}\).

---

Thus \(f\) is a bounded measure on \((\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\bullet}\) by the Riesz representation theorem. From Remark 2.4.4(d) we know that \(f\in C\bigl{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\bullet}\bigr{)}\) and that \(\mathcal{F}^{-1}a\) is positively homogeneous of degree \(-n\). This implies that \(f\) belongs to \(L_{1}\bigl{(}(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\bullet}\bigr{)}\) and that it is positively homogeneous of degree \(-n\). Hence, if \(f\neq 0\),

\[\infty>\int_{(\mathbb{R}^{n})^{\bullet}}|f|\,dx =\int_{0}^{\infty}r^{n-1}\int_{S^{n-1}}|f(r\omega)|\,d\omega\,dr\] \[=\int_{0}^{\infty}r^{-1}\,dr\int_{S^{n-1}}|f(\omega)|\,d\omega= \infty\ ,\]

which is impossible. Thus \(f=0\) so that \(\operatorname{supp}(\mathcal{F}^{-1}a)\subset\{0\}\). Now we deduce from Example 1.8.7(d) that

\[\mathcal{F}^{-1}a=\sum_{|\alpha|\leq k}a_{\alpha}\partial^{\alpha}\delta\]

for suitable \(k\in\mathbb{N}\) and \(a_{\alpha}\in\mathbb{C}\). Hence we infer from (2.3.3) that \(a\) is a polynomial. Since it has to be homogeneous of degree \(0\), we see that \(a=\operatorname{const}\). Lastly, it is well-known that \(a\) is a Fourier multiplier for \(L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) iff it is one for \(L_{\infty}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) (e.g., [10, IV, SS 3.1]). This covers the case \(\mathfrak{F}=L_{1}\).

The above proof follows the arguments in [10, Lemma 1.10]. Note that a similar result fails to be true for \(L_{p}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\)-multipliers if \(1<p<\infty\), as Mikhlin's theorem shows.

---

## 3 The Heat Equation and Related Problems

The theory of linear and nonlinear parabolic evolution equations is built on the concept of strongly continuous analytic semigroups. So far we have not seen any concrete semigroup of this type but remained exclusively within an abstract framework. Now we turn to a first concrete situation: we study in detail the heat semigroup.

But first, in Subsection 3.1, we give a simple application of the theory of convolutions of Banach-space-valued distributions to linear evolution equations. This subsection is included mostly for curiosity since it throws new light on the possible concepts of generalized solutions to abstract evolution equations.

In Subsection 3.2 we investigate the fundamental Gauss-Weierstrass or heat semigroup and identify its generator on the 'standard' function spaces. Since this semigroup is generated by the Laplace operator, it is the prototype of an 'elliptic convolution semigroup'. This class of semigroups is at the basis of the general theory of elliptic and parabolic systems of differential and pseudodifferential operators. The information that we obtain from the study of the Gauss-Weierstrass semigroup is deepened in Subsection 3.3. There we derive additional properties of its generator.

### Distributional Solutions of Evolution Equations

As a first application of the general theory of vector-valued distributions we give a short discussion of linear evolution equations in the framework of distribution theory. For this we assume that

\[A\in\mathcal{C}(E)\ .\]

Then, given \(f\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\), we consider the **linear evolution equation**

\[(\partial+A)u=f\qquad\text{in }\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\ .\] (3.1.1)

By a **distributional solution** of (3.1.1) we mean an element \(u\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}\big{(}D(A)\big{)}\) satisfying \(\partial u+Au=f\) in \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\). To be more precise, recall from Example 1.3.1(d) that

\[\mathcal{L}\big{(}D(A),E\big{)}\times D(A)\to E\ ,\quad(B,a)\mapsto Ba\] (3.1.2)

is a multiplication. Moreover, letting \(i:\ D(A)\hookrightarrow E\), it follows that \(i\) and \(A\) belong to \(\mathcal{L}\big{(}D(A),E\big{)}\). Hence we obtain from Remark 1.6.5(c) and (d) that, recalling Remark 1.6.5(g),

\[i,A\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(D(A)),\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+ }(E)\big{)}\] (3.1.3)

and that \(\partial\circ i=i\circ\partial\). Thus, employing the natural identification \(\partial:=i\circ\partial\), we see that

\[\partial+A\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(D(A)),\mathcal{D}^{ \prime}_{+}(E)\big{)}\ .\]

This shows that the above definition of a distributional solution is meaningful.

---

#### Fundamental Solutions

Denoting the convolution with respect to multiplication (3.1.2) simply by \(\ast\) , it follows from Remark 1.9.6(i) that

\[(\partial+A)u=(\partial\delta\otimes i+\delta\otimes A)\ast u\ ,\qquad u\in \mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}\big{(}D(A)\big{)}\ .\] (3.1.4)

From this representation we easily deduce the following solvability criterion for equation (3.1.1).

#### 3.1.1 Theorem

_Suppose that there exists_

\[G\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}\big{(}\mathcal{L}(E)\big{)}\cap\mathcal{D}^{ \prime}_{+}\big{(}\mathcal{L}(E,D(A))\big{)}\ ,\] (3.1.5)

\(a\) **fundamental solution** _for \(\partial+A\), satisfying_

\[(\partial\delta\otimes i+\delta\otimes A)\ast G=\delta\otimes 1_{E}\] (3.1.6)

_and_

\[G\ast(\partial\delta\otimes i+\delta\otimes A)=\delta\otimes i\ .\] (3.1.7)

_Then equation (3.1.1) possesses for each \(f\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\) a unique distributional solution \(u\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}\big{(}D(A)\big{)}\). It is given by \(u:=G\ast f\)._

ProofPut \(E_{0}:=\mathcal{L}(E)\), \(E_{1}:=\mathcal{L}\big{(}D(A),E\big{)}\), \(E_{2}:=\mathcal{L}\big{(}E,D(A)\big{)}\), \(E_{3}:=E_{5}:=E\), and \(E_{4}:=D(A)\). Then the associativity assumption of Remark 1.6.5(e) is satisfied with respect to the obvious multiplications. Hence the associativity of the associated convolution products, guaranteed by Remark 1.9.6(c), implies

\[(\partial+A)(G\ast f)=(\partial\delta\otimes i+\delta\otimes A)\ast G\ast f=( \delta\otimes 1_{E})\ast f=f\]

in \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\), thanks to (3.1.5), (3.1.6), and Remark 1.9.6(i). Thus \(G\ast f\) is a distributional solution of (3.1.1).

Conversely, let \(E_{0}:=E_{2}:=\mathcal{L}\big{(}D(A),E\big{)}\), \(E_{1}:=\mathcal{L}(E)\), \(E_{3}:=D(A)\), and \(E_{4}:=E_{5}:=E\). Then the hypotheses of Remark 1.6.5(e) are again satisfied with respect to the obvious multiplications. Hence we deduce once more from (3.1.5) and Remark 1.9.6(c) that, given a distributional solution \(u\) of (3.1.1),

\[G\ast f=G\ast(\partial+A)u=G\ast(\partial\delta\otimes i+\delta\otimes A)\ast u =(\delta\otimes i)\ast u=u\ ,\]

thanks to (3.1.7) and Remark 1.9.6(i). This gives uniqueness.

#### 3.1.2 Remark

Given the hypotheses of Theorem 3.1.1, it follows from Theorem 1.9.7 that

\[(f\mapsto u:=G\ast f)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E), \mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(D(A))\big{)}\ ,\]

that is, the unique solution \(u\) of (3.1.1) depends continuously on \(f\) (in the topology of \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}\)). This is often expressed by saying that problem (3.1.1) is **well-posed** (in the sense of Hadamard).

---

#### Fundamental Solutions and Laplace Transforms

Theorem 3.1.1 guarantees that we can solve the evolution equation \((\partial+A)u=f\) in \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\) provided we can guarantee the existence of a fundamental solution for \(\partial+A\). This can be achieved by means of Laplace transforms, provided we impose the following assumption:

\[\begin{array}{l}\mbox{there exists $\omega\geq 0$ such that $[\operatorname{Re}z>\omega]\subset\rho(-A)$}\\ \mbox{and the map $[\operatorname{Re}z>\omega^{\prime}]\to\mathcal{L}(E),\ \lambda\mapsto(\lambda+A)^{-1}$}\\ \mbox{is polynomially bounded for each $\omega^{\prime}>\omega$.}\end{array}\] (3.1.8)

Since the resolvent \(\lambda\mapsto(\lambda+A)^{-1}\) is a holomorphic \(\mathcal{L}(E)\)-valued function it follows from Theorem 1.10.1 that there exists \(G\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}\big{(}\mathcal{L}(E)\big{)}\) such that \((\lambda+A)^{-1}\) is the Laplace transform of \(G\) at \(\lambda\in[\operatorname{Re}z>\omega]\). The following theorem shows that \(G\) is a fundamental solution of \(\partial+A\), and that it is the only one.

#### 3.1.3 Theorem

_Let assumption (3.1.8) be satisfied. Then there exists a unique fundamental solution \(G\) for \(\partial+A\)._

Proof.: Suppose that there exists a fundamental solution \(G\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}\big{(}\mathcal{L}(E,D(A))\big{)}\) which is Laplace transformable. Then we obtain from (3.1.6) and (3.1.7), thanks to Example 1.10.10, Theorem 1.10.11, and Remark 1.10.16(b),

\[(\lambda i+A)\widetilde{G}(\lambda)=1_{E}\ ,\quad\widetilde{G}(\lambda)( \lambda i+A)=i\ ,\quad\operatorname{Re}\lambda>\omega_{0}(G)\ .\] (3.1.9)

Hence \(\widetilde{G}(\lambda)=(\lambda+A)^{-1}\) for \(\operatorname{Re}\lambda>\omega\vee\omega_{0}(G)\). Thus the identity theorem for holomorphic functions implies that \(\omega_{0}(G)\leq\omega\), and the uniqueness of \(G\) is a consequence of Remark 1.10.16.

By the observations preceding this theorem we know that there exists a unique \(G\in\mathcal{L}\mathrm{ap}_{+}\big{(}\mathcal{L}(E)\big{)}\) with \(\widetilde{G}(\lambda)=(\lambda+A)^{-1}\) for \(\operatorname{Re}\lambda>\omega\). Therefore \(\widetilde{G}(\lambda)\) belongs to \(\mathcal{L}\big{(}E,D(A)\big{)}\) so that \(G\in\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}\big{(}\mathcal{L}(E,D(A))\big{)}\). Hence \(\widetilde{G}\) satisfies (3.1.9) for \(\operatorname{Re}\lambda>\omega\) and the convolution theorem implies that (3.1.6) and (3.1.7) are valid. 

#### 3.1.4 Remark

Given \(A\in\mathcal{C}(E)\), one can ask for necessary and sufficient conditions for \(\partial+A\) to be an isomorphism from \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}\big{(}D(A)\big{)}\) onto \(\mathcal{D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\). This problem has been solved by J.L. Lions by means of the theory of _distribution semigroups_ in [19]. In that paper it is also shown that condition (3.1.8) is necessary and sufficient for a distribution semigroup to be Laplace transformable. 

#### Fundamental Solutions and Semigroups

Lastly, we consider the particularly important case that \(-A\) is the infinitesimal generator of a strongly continuous semigroup. In this case it can be expected that the fundamental solution is related to the semigroup. The next theorem shows that this, indeed, is the case.

---

**3.1.5 Theorem**: _Suppose that \(A\in{\cal G}(E)\). Then there exists a fundamental solution for \(\partial+A\). It is given by the regular distribution \(e_{A}\), where_

\[e_{A}(t):=\left\{\begin{array}{cc}e^{-tA}\ ,&\quad t\geq 0\ ,\\ 0\ ,&\quad t<0\ .\end{array}\right.\]

_Thus problem (3.1.1) is well-posed._

ProofThe Hille-Yosida theorem implies, in particular, that \([\mathop{\rm Re}\nolimits z>\omega]\subset\rho(-A)\) for some \(\omega\in{\mathbb{R}}\) and that

\[\|(\lambda+A)^{-1}\|_{{\cal L}(E)}\leq M(\mathop{\rm Re}\nolimits\lambda-\omega )^{-1}\ ,\qquad\mathop{\rm Re}\nolimits\lambda>\omega\ ,\]

for some \(M\geq 1\). Hence condition (3.1.8) is trivially satisfied. Therefore Theorem 3.1.3 guarantees the existence of a unique fundamental solution \(G\) for \(\partial+A\), and the proof of that theorem shows that \((\lambda+A)^{-1}=\widetilde{G}(\lambda)\) for \(\mathop{\rm Re}\nolimits\lambda>\omega\). Now we infer from Example 1.10.14(a) and the uniqueness assertion of Remark 1.10.16(a) that \(G=e_{A}\).

Given \((x,g)\in E\times L_{1,{\rm loc}}({\mathbb{R}}^{+},E)\), denote by \(g^{\star}\in L_{1,{\rm loc}}({\mathbb{R}},E)\) the trivial extension of \(g\) over \({\mathbb{R}}\) and put \(f:=\delta\otimes x+g^{\star}\). Then \(g^{\star}\in{\cal D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\), and it follows from Remarks 1.9.6(i) and 1.9.8(c) that \(e_{A}\ast f\) is the regular distribution given by

\[e_{A}\ast f(t)=e^{-tA}x+\int_{0}^{t}e^{-(t-\tau)A}g(\tau)\,d\tau\ ,\qquad t \geq 0\ .\]

Thus the following result is an immediate consequence of Theorem 3.1.5.

**3.1.6 Corollary**: _Suppose that \(A\in{\cal G}(E)\) and \((x,g)\in E\times L_{1,{\rm loc}}({\mathbb{R}}^{+},E)\). Then the differential equation_

\[(\partial+A)u=\delta\otimes x+g^{\star}\qquad\hbox{in }{\cal D}^{\prime}_{+}(E)\]

_is well-posed and its solution \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}_{+}\bigl{(}D(A)\bigr{)}\) is the mild solution of the linear Cauchy problem_

\[\dot{u}+Au=g(t)\ ,\quad t>0\ ,\qquad u(0)=x\ .\]

It should be remarked that Laplace transform techniques can also be applied to evolution equations of the form \(\partial^{k}u+Au=f\) for \(k\geq 2\), that is, to higher order equations (e.g., [DL90, Chapter XVI]).

### The Gauss-Weierstrass Semigroup

In this subsection we give a detailed investigation of the Gauss-Weierstrass semigroup on various spaces of functions and distributions. This semigroup is perhaps

---

the best-known semigroup (of convolutions) and has numerous applications in mathematics. It has the advantage of possessing an explicit analytic representation (through the heat kernel). Moreover, its generator is the Laplace operator, perhaps the most-studied elliptic differential operator. The results on the heat semigroup and its generator that we derive in this subsection are fundamental for the study of Bessel potential and Besov spaces.

#### The Heat Kernel

We define the **Gauss-Weierstrass** (or **heat**) **kernel**\(\{\,w_{t}\ ;\ t>0\,\}\) on \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) by

\[w_{t}(x):=(4\pi t)^{-n/2}e^{-\frac{|x|^{2}}{4t}}\ ,\qquad x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ , \quad t>0\ .\] (3.2.1)

Observe that \(w_{t}\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) and \(w_{t}(x)>0\) for \(x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\) and \(t>0\).

#### 3.2.1 Lemma

\(w_{t}\in L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) _and \(\int w_{t}\,dx=1\). Moreover, \(w_{t}=\mathcal{F}^{-1}e^{-t\,|\cdot|^{2}}\) for \(t>0\)._

ProofThe first part of the assertion has already been obtained in the proof of Lemma 1.3.7.

Thanks to \(e^{-t|\cdot|^{2}}=\sigma_{\sqrt{t}}^{-1}e^{-|\cdot|^{2}}\) we obtain from (2.3.2) and (2.3.10) that

\[\mathcal{F}^{-1}e^{-t|\cdot|^{2}}=t^{-n/2}\sigma_{\sqrt{t}}\mathcal{F}^{-1}e^{ -|\cdot|^{2}}=(2\pi)^{-n}t^{-n/2}\sigma_{\sqrt{t}}\mathcal{F}e^{-|\cdot|^{2}}\ ,\] (3.2.2)

since \(e^{-|\cdot|^{2}}\) is even.

Put \(f_{n}:=e^{-|\cdot|^{2}}\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) and note that \(f_{n}=f_{1}\otimes\cdots\otimes f_{1}\) with \(n\) factors. Hence, by Fubini's theorem, \(\widehat{f}_{n}=\widehat{f}_{1}\otimes\cdots\otimes\widehat{f}_{1}\), and also \(\partial f_{1}=-2x_{1}f_{1}\). Thus (2.3.3) and (2.3.5) imply \(i\xi_{1}\widehat{f}_{1}=\overline{\partial f_{1}}=-2x_{1}\widehat{f}_{1}=-2i \,\partial\widehat{f}_{1}\). Hence \(\widehat{f}_{1}\) solves the initial value problem

\[\dot{\psi}=-(t/2)\psi\ ,\quad\psi(0)=\widehat{f}_{1}(0)=\int f_{1}\,dx=\sqrt{ \pi}\ .\]

Consequently, \(\widehat{f}_{1}(t)=\sqrt{\pi}e^{-t^{2}/4}\) for \(t\in\mathbb{R}\), so that \(\widehat{f}_{n}=\pi^{n/2}e^{-|\cdot|^{2}/4}\). Now the last assertion follows from (3.2.2).

#### The Heat Semigroup

Let \(E:=(E,|\cdot|)\) be a Banach space. We put \(w_{0}:=\delta\), where \(\delta\) is the Dirac distribution, and

\[W(t)u:=w_{t}*u\ ,\qquad u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\qquad t \geq 0\ .\]

Note that \(W(0)u=u\). Then \(\big{\{}\,W(t)\ ;\ t\geq 0\,\big{\}}\) is said to be the **Gauss-Weierstrass** (or **heat**) **semigroup**. The following theorem justifies this name. As usual, we

---

denote by

\[\Delta:=\Delta_{n}:=\partial_{1}^{2}+\cdots+\partial_{n}^{2}=-\Lambda_{0}^{2}(D)\in \mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{D}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\]

the (\(n\)-dimensional) **Laplace operator**.

A map \(U:\,\mathbb{R}^{+}\to\mathcal{L}(F)\), where \(F:=(F,\mathcal{P})\) is a LCS, is **locally uniformly bounded** if for each \(p\in\mathcal{P}\) and \(T>0\) there exist \(q\in\mathcal{P}\) and \(c>0\) such that

\[p\big{(}U(t)f\big{)}\leq cq(f)\ ,\qquad f\in F\ ,\quad t\in[0,T]\ .\]

**3.2.2 Theorem**: (i) _Suppose that \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\mathcal{S},\mathcal{S}^{\prime}\}\). Then the Gauss-Weierstrass semigroup is a locally uniformly bounded semigroup on \(\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) with infinitesimal generator \(\Delta\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\), and_

\[W(\cdot)u\in C^{\infty}\big{(}\mathbb{R}^{+},\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E) \big{)}\ ,\qquad u\in\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ .\]

(ii) _Suppose that \(\mathfrak{F}\in\{\,\mbox{BUC},C_{0},L_{p}\ ;\ 1\leq p<\infty\,\}\). Then the Gauss-Weierstrass semigroup restricts from \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) to a strongly continuous analytic contraction semigroup on the Banach space \(\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\), whose infinitesimal generator is the \(\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\)-realization \(\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}}\) of \(\Delta\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\)._

**Proof** (i) Since \(e^{-t\,|\cdot|^{2}}\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\subset\mathcal{O}_{M}( \mathbb{R}^{n})\) for \(t>0\) and since \(1\in\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), it follows from (1.9.31) and Lemma 3.2.1 that

\[W(t)=w_{t}\ast\ =\mathcal{F}^{-1}e^{-t\,|\cdot|^{2}}\mathcal{F}\in\mathcal{L} \big{(}\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\ ,\qquad t\geq 0\ .\] (3.2.3)

Note that \(\{\,e^{-t|\xi|^{2}}\ ;\ t\geq 0\,\}\) is for each \(\xi\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\) a subsemigroup of the multiplicative group \((\hat{\mathbb{R}}^{+},\cdot)\). Hence \(\{\,W(t)\ ;\ t\geq 0\,\}\) is a semigroup on \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\).

Let

\[a_{j}(t):=\partial_{t}^{j}e^{-t|\cdot|^{2}}=(-1)^{j}\,|\cdot|^{2j}\,e^{-t| \cdot|^{2}}\ ,\qquad j\in\mathbb{N}\ ,\quad t\geq 0\ .\]

Then, by Taylor's theorem,

\[a_{j}(t+h)-a_{j}(t)-a_{j+1}(t)h=h^{2}b_{j}(t,h)\] (3.2.4)

point-wise with respect to \(\xi\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\), where

\[b_{j}(t,h):=\int_{0}^{1}(1-s)a_{j+2}(t+sh)\,ds\]

for \(t\in\mathbb{R}^{+}\) and \(h\in\hat{\mathbb{R}}\) with \(t+h\geq 0\). Observe that

\[|\partial_{\xi}^{\alpha}a_{j}(t)(\xi)|\leq c(m,T)(1+|\xi|^{2})^{j+m}\] (3.2.5)

---

for \(\xi\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\), \(0\leq t\leq T<\infty\), and \(|\alpha|\leq m\). Hence we infer from (3.2.4) that, given \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(j,k,m\in\mathbb{N}\),

\[q_{k,m}\Big{(}h^{-1}\big{[}a_{j}(t+h)-a_{j}(t)-a_{j+1}(t)h\big{]}\varphi\Big{)} \leq|h|\,c(m,T)q_{\ell,m}(\varphi)\]

for \(t\in\mathbb{R}^{+}\) and \(h\in\overset{\ast}{\mathbb{R}}\) with \(t+h\geq 0\), where \(\ell:=j+2+k+m\). This shows that for \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\), \(t\in\mathbb{R}^{+}\), and \(j\in\mathbb{N}\),

\[h^{-1}\big{[}a_{j}(t+h)-a_{j}(t)\big{]}\varphi\to a_{j+1}(t)\varphi\qquad\text {in }\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\] (3.2.6)

as \(h\to 0\). Thanks to \(a_{j}(t)\in\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\),

\[a_{j}(t)(D)=\mathcal{F}^{-1}a_{j}(t)\mathcal{F}\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S }(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\ ,\qquad t\geq 0\ ,\quad j\in\mathbb{N}\ .\]

Consequently, (3.2.6) and (2.3.1) imply that

\[\begin{split}\frac{a_{j}(t+h)(D)\varphi-a_{j}(t)(D)\varphi}{h}& =\mathcal{F}^{-1}\Big{(}\frac{a_{j}(t+h)-a_{j}(t)}{h}\widehat{ \varphi}\Big{)}\\ &\to a_{j+1}(t)(D)\varphi\end{split}\] (3.2.7)

in \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) as \(h\to 0\) for each \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\), \(t\geq 0\), and \(j\in\mathbb{N}\), and it follows that

\[a_{j}(t)(D)=(-1)^{j}(-\Delta)^{j}W(t)=\Delta^{j}W(t)\ ,\qquad t\geq 0\ ,\quad j \in\mathbb{N}\ .\] (3.2.8)

From (3.2.7) and (3.2.8) we deduce that

\[W(\cdot)\varphi\in C^{\infty}\big{(}\mathbb{R}^{+},\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n} ,E)\big{)}\ ,\qquad\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\]

and that

\[\partial_{t}^{j}W(t)\varphi=\Delta^{j}W(t)\varphi\ ,\qquad t>0\ ,\quad j\in \mathbb{N}\ ,\quad\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ .\] (3.2.9)

Given \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(k,m\in\mathbb{N}\), (3.2.5) entails

\[q_{k,m}\big{(}a_{0}(t)\varphi\big{)}\leq c(m,T)q_{k+m,m}(\varphi)\ ,\qquad 0\leq t \leq T<\infty\ .\]

Thanks to \(W(t)\varphi=\mathcal{F}^{-1}\big{(}a_{0}(t)\widehat{\varphi}\big{)}\), this implies, together with (2.3.1), that the Gauss-Weierstrass semigroup is locally uniformly bounded on \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\).

Since \(a_{j}(t)\in\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), it follows from (1.9.31) that \(a_{j}(t)(D)\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\). Moreover,

\[a_{j}(t)(D)u(\varphi)=\mathcal{F}^{-1}a_{j}(t)\mathcal{F}u(\varphi)=u\big{(} \mathcal{F}a_{j}(t)\mathcal{F}^{-1}\varphi\big{)}\] (3.2.10)

for \(u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\). It is obvious that the estimates leading to (3.2.7) also imply that, given \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\),

\[\mathcal{F}\Big{(}\frac{a_{j}(t+h)-a_{j}(t)}{h}\mathcal{F}^{-1}\varphi\Big{)} \to\mathcal{F}a_{j+1}(t)\mathcal{F}^{-1}\varphi\]

---

as \(h\to 0\) in \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\). Hence it follows from (3.2.10) that

\[\frac{a_{j}(t+h)(D)u-a_{j}(t)(D)u}{h}(\varphi)\to a_{j+1}(D)u(\varphi)\qquad\text {as }h\to 0\] (3.2.11)

for \(u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\). Since \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) is a Montel space, the Banach-Steinhaus theorem guarantees that the convergence in (3.2.11) holds uniformly for \(\varphi\) in bounded subsets of \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\), that is,

\[h^{-1}\big{[}a_{j}(t+h)(D)u-a_{j}(t)(D)u\big{]}\to a_{j+1}(t)(D)u\qquad\text{ in }\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n}),E\big{)}=\mathcal{S}^{\prime}( \mathbb{R}^{n},E)\]

as \(h\to 0\). This shows that

\[W(\cdot)u\in C^{\infty}\big{(}\mathbb{R}^{+},\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^ {n},E)\big{)}\ ,\qquad u\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\ ,\]

and that (3.2.9) is true for \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\). A similar argument guarantees that the map \(t\mapsto W(t)\) from \(\mathbb{R}^{+}\) to \(\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\) is locally uniformly bounded. Since \(\big{\{}\,W(t)\ ;\ t\geq 0\,\big{\}}\) is a semigroup on \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) as well, assertion (i) has been completely proven.

(ii) It follows from Lemma 3.2.1 and Theorem 1.9.9 that \(W(t)\in\mathcal{L}(\mathfrak{F})\) with \(\|W(t)\|\leq 1\), where \(\mathfrak{F}:=\mathfrak{F}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\). Hence we deduce from (i) that \(\big{\{}\,W(t)\ ;\ t\geq 0\,\big{\}}\) restricts to a contraction semigroup on \(\mathfrak{F}\). Next observe that

\[w_{\varepsilon}(x)=\big{(}\sqrt{\varepsilon}\big{)}^{-n}w_{1}\big{(}x\big{/} \sqrt{\varepsilon}\big{)}\ ,\qquad\varepsilon>0\ ,\quad x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ .\]

Since \(w_{1}\in L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) and \(\int w_{1}\,dx=1\) by Lemma 3.2.1, we see that \(\{\,w_{\varepsilon^{2}}\ ;\ \varepsilon>0\,\}\) is an approximate identity. Hence [1, (III.4.2.23)-(III.4.2.25)] and the closedness of \(C_{0}\) in \(BUC\) imply that \(W(t)u\to u\) in \(\mathfrak{F}\) as \(t\to 0\) for each \(u\in\mathfrak{F}\). Thus \(\big{\{}\,W(t)\ ;\ t\geq 0\,\big{\}}\) is a strongly continuous semigroup on \(\mathfrak{F}\).

Let \(A\) be the infinitesimal generator of \(\big{\{}\,W(t)\ ;\ t\geq 0\,\big{\}}\) on \(\mathfrak{F}\). Then, given \(u\in\operatorname{dom}(A)\), it follows that

\[t^{-1}\big{(}W(t)u-u\big{)}\to Au\] (3.2.12)

in \(\mathfrak{F}\) as \(t\to 0\). Since \(\mathfrak{F}\hookrightarrow\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and the left-hand side of (3.2.12) converges in \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) towards \(\Delta u\) by (i), we see that \(A\subset\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}}\).

On the other hand, given \(u\in\operatorname{dom}(\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})\), the strong continuity of \(W\) implies \(W(\cdot)\Delta u\in C(\mathbb{R}^{+},\mathfrak{F})\) and, consequently,

\[\int_{0}^{t}W(\tau)\Delta u\,d\tau\in\mathfrak{F}\ ,\qquad t>0\ .\]

---

Thus we infer from Fubini's theorem that, for \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\),

\[\int_{0}^{t}W(\tau)\Delta u\,d\tau(\varphi) =\int_{\mathbb{R}^{n}}\!\int_{0}^{t}\!\bigl{(}W(\tau)\Delta u\bigr{)} (x)\varphi(x)\,d\tau\,dx\] \[=\int_{0}^{t}W(\tau)\Delta u(\varphi)\,d\tau=\int_{0}^{t}u\bigl{(} \Delta W(\tau)\varphi\bigr{)}\,d\tau\] \[=\int_{0}^{t}u\bigl{(}\partial_{\tau}W(\tau)\varphi\bigr{)}\,d\tau= \int_{0}^{t}\partial_{\tau}\bigl{[}u\bigl{(}W(\tau)\varphi\bigr{)}\bigr{]}\,d\tau\] \[=u\bigl{(}(W(t)-1)\varphi\bigr{)}=\bigl{(}W(t)-1\bigr{)}u(\varphi )\ ,\]

where we used the fact that

\[\mathcal{F}^{-1}e^{-t|\cdot|^{2}}\mathcal{F}=\mathcal{F}e^{-t|\cdot|^{2}} \mathcal{F}^{-1}\]

by the evenness of \(\xi\mapsto e^{-t|\xi|^{2}}\), and (3.2.9). Consequently,

\[\int_{0}^{t}W(\tau)\Delta u\,d\tau=W(t)u-u\]

so that

\[t^{-1}\bigl{(}W(t)u-u\bigr{)}=\frac{1}{t}\int_{0}^{t}W(\tau)\Delta u\,d\tau \to\Delta u\]

in \(\mathfrak{F}\) as \(t\to 0\). This shows that \(u\in\operatorname{dom}(A)\) so that \(A=\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}}\).

Clearly,

\[\partial_{t}w_{t}(x)=\frac{1}{t}\Bigl{(}-\frac{n}{2}+\frac{|x|^{2}}{4t}\Bigr{)} w_{t}(x)=:\dot{w}_{t}(x)\ ,\qquad t>0\ ,\quad x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\ .\]

Hence \(\|\dot{w}_{t}\|_{1}\leq c(\varepsilon)\) for \(t\geq\varepsilon>0\) and, thanks to Lebesgue's theorem,

\[(t\mapsto\dot{w}_{t})\in C\bigl{(}(0,\infty),L_{1}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\bigr{)}\ .\]

Thus we infer from Theorem 1.9.9 and Remark 1.9.6(g) that

\[(t\mapsto w_{t}*u)\in C^{1}\bigl{(}(0,\infty),\mathfrak{F}\bigr{)}\]

with

\[\partial_{t}\bigl{(}W(t)u\bigr{)}=\partial_{t}(w_{t}*u)=\dot{w}_{t}*u\ ,\qquad u \in\mathfrak{F}\ ,\quad t>0\ .\] (3.2.13)

Consequently,

\[\operatorname{im}\bigl{(}W(t)\bigr{)}\subset\operatorname{dom}(A)\ ,\qquad t>0\ .\] (3.2.14)

Note that

\[t\dot{w}_{t}=-\frac{n}{2}w_{t}+v_{t}\qquad\text{with}\quad v_{t}(x):=\frac{|x| ^{2}}{4t}w_{t}(x)\ .\] (3.2.15)

Since

\[v_{t}=\bigl{(}\sqrt{4t}\bigr{)}^{-n}\sigma_{\sqrt{4t}}h\qquad\text{with}\quad h (x):=\pi^{-n/2}\,|x|^{2}\,e^{-|x|^{2}}\ ,\]

---

we see from (2.3.12) that

\[\eqalign{\|v_{t}\|_{1}=\|h\|_{1}&=\pi^{-n/2}\,{\rm vol}(S^{n-1})\int_{0}^{\infty}r^ {n+2}e^{-r^{2}}\,dr/r\cr&=2^{-1}\pi^{-n/2}\,{\rm vol}(S^{n-1})\Gamma\Bigl{(}{n \over 2}+1\Bigr{)}=n/2\ ,\cr}\]

since \({\rm vol}(S^{n-1})=n\omega_{n}\), where

\[\omega_{n}:={\rm vol}({{R}}^{n})=\pi^{n/2}\Big{/}\Gamma\Bigl{(}{n\over 2}+1 \Bigr{)}\ .\]

Thus we obtain from Lemma 3.2.1 and (3.2.13)-(3.2.16) that

\[\bigl{\|}t\partial_{t}\bigl{(}W(t)u\bigr{)}\bigr{\|}=\|tAW(t)u\|\leq n\,\|u\| \ ,\qquad t>0\ .\]

Now Yosida's characterization of analytic semigroups (e.g., [Dav80, Theorem 2.3.9]) implies that \(\bigl{\{}\,W(t)\ ;\ t\geq 0\,\bigr{\}}\) is an analytic semigroup on \({{F}}\).

3.2.3 Remarks **(a)**: Since \(e^{-t|\cdot|^{2}}\) is an even function,

\[W(t)u(\varphi)=u\bigl{(}W(t)\varphi\bigr{)}\ ,\qquad u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({{R}} ^{n},E)\ ,\quad\varphi\in{\cal S}({{R}}^{n})\ ,\]

for \(t>0\), as noticed in the above proof.
**(b)**: In the following we often put

\[e^{t\Delta}:=W(t)\ ,\qquad t\geq 0\ .\]

Thanks to (3.2.3) this somewhat imprecise notation is consistent with the general notation \(m(D):={\cal F}^{-1}m{\cal F}\). Due to Theorem 3.2.2(ii) it is also consistent with our general notation \(\{\,e^{-tA}\ ;\ t\geq 0\,\}\) for the semigroup generated by \(-A\).
**(c)**: Suppose that \(a\in{\cal O}_{M}({{R}}^{n})\) with \(\inf{\rm Re}\,a\geq\omega>-\infty\). Then \(e^{-ta}\in{\cal O}_{M}({{R}}^{n})\) for \(t\geq 0\) and

\[e^{-ta(D)}:={\cal F}^{-1}e^{-ta}{\cal F}\ ,\qquad t\geq 0\ ,\]

is well-defined. An obvious modification of the proof of Theorem 3.2.2(i) shows that \(\{\,e^{-ta(D)}\ ;\ t\geq 0\,\}\) is a locally uniformly bounded semigroup on \({\cal S}({{R}}^{n},E)\) and on \({\cal S}^{\prime}({{R}}^{n},E)\) with infinitesimal generator \(-a(D)\). Moreover, given \(u\in{\cal S}({{R}}^{n},E)\) or \(u\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({{R}}^{n},E)\), the map \(t\mapsto e^{-ta(D)}u\) is smooth from \({{R}}^{+}\) into \({\cal S}({{R}}^{n},E)\) or \({\cal S}^{\prime}({{R}}^{n},E)\), respectively.

The above assumptions are satisfied, for example, if \(a(\xi):=i\,\langle h,\xi\rangle\) for \(\xi\in{{R}}^{n}\) and a fixed vector \(h:=(h^{1},\ldots,h^{n})\in{{R}}^{n}\), or if \(a(\xi)=z\,|\xi|^{2}\) for \(\xi\in{{R}}^{n}\) and some fixed \(z\in[{\rm Re}\,z\geq 0]\). In the first case \(a(D)=\partial_{h}\), the **directional derivative** in the direction \(h\), that is,

\[\partial_{h}:=\sum_{j=1}^{n}h^{j}\partial_{j}\ ,\]

---

and \(e^{-ta(D)}=e^{-t\partial_{h}}=\tau_{th}\), where \(\tau_{th}\) denotes right translation by the vector \(th\) (cf. [Ama95, (III.4.2.6))]. In the second case, letting in particular \(z:=i\), we obtain the **Schrodinger semigroup**.

**(d)** Let \(F:=(F,{\cal P})\) be an LCS and suppose that \(\big{\{}\,U(t)\ ;\ t\geq 0\,\big{\}}\) is a locally uniformly bounded semigroup on \(F\) such that \(U(\cdot)f\in C^{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{+},F)\) for each \(f\in F\). Then, defining \(A\) by \(Af=\partial_{t}\big{(}U(t)f\big{)}\big{|}_{t=0}\) for \(f\in F\), it follows that \(A\in{\cal L}(F)\) and that the Cauchy problem

\[\dot{u}=Au\ ,\quad t>0\ ,\qquad u(0)=f\]

has for each \(f\in F\) a unique solution \(u\in C^{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{+},F)\), namely \(u(\cdot)=U(\cdot)f\).

This follows by mimicking the corresponding proof for the case that \(F\) is a Banach space, \(-A\in{\cal G}(F)\), and \(f\in{\rm dom}(A)\).

**(e)** From Theorem 3.2.2 and (d) we infer that, given \(u^{0}\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\), the Cauchy problem

\[\partial_{t}u-\Delta u=0\quad\hbox{in }\mathbb{R}^{n}\times\dot{\overline{ \mathbb{R}}}^{+}\ ,\qquad u(\cdot,0)=u^{0}\] (3.2.18)

has a unique solution \(u(\cdot,t,u^{0})\) with

\[\big{[}t\mapsto u(\cdot,t,u^{0})\big{]}\in C^{\infty}\big{(}{\mathbb{R}}^{+},{ \cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\big{)}\ ,\]

and that it is given by \(u(\cdot,t,u^{0})=w_{t}*u^{0}\) for \(t>0\). Since \(\partial_{t}^{j}w_{t}\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,)\) for \(t>0\) and \(j\in\mathbb{N}\), Proposition 1.2.7 and Remark 1.9.6(g) show that

\[\partial_{t}^{j}u(\cdot,t,u^{0})\in{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ .\]

From this one easily derives

\[\big{[}(x,t)\mapsto u(x,t,u^{0})\big{]}\in C^{\infty}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times \dot{\overline{\mathbb{R}}}^{+},E)\] (3.2.19)

for \(u^{0}\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\). This shows that the Gauss-Weierstrass semigroup possesses a rather strong 'smoothing property'.

Recall that \({\cal E}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\hookrightarrow{\cal S}^{\prime}({ \mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\). Thus the Cauchy problem (3.2.18) has for each initial value \(u^{0}\) that is a distribution with compact support the unique solution \(u(\cdot,t,u^{0})\in{\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\), and it satisfies (3.2.19). In particular, we can choose \(u^{0}=\delta\) and find that the _heat kernel is the unique temperate solution of the heat equation_

\[\partial_{t}u=\Delta u\quad\hbox{on }\mathbb{R}^{n}\ ,\qquad t>0\ ,\]

_satisfying \(u(0)=\delta\)._ In other words, \(w_{t}\) is for \(t>0\) the temperature distribution evolving according to the heat equation from a unit-strength point-source at \((x,t)=(0,0)\). The fact that \(w_{t}\) is smooth (indeed: analytic) for \(t>0\) reflects the property of **infinite speed of propagation** of the heat equation.

**(f)** The elements of \({\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) are slowly increasing distributions. More precisely, \(u\in{\cal D}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,)\) belongs to \({\cal S}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,)\) iff there exist \(k\in\mathbb{N}\), \(\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n}\,\), and \(f\in BC({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,)\)

---

such that \(u=\partial^{\alpha}(\Lambda_{1}^{k}f)\) (cf. [Sch66, Chap. VII, Theoreme VI]). Thus, given any continuous function \(u^{0}\) on \(\mathbb{R}^{n}\) growing at most polynomially, the initial value problem (3.2.18) has a unique solution \(u(\cdot,u^{0})\) that is smooth and such that \(u(t,u^{0})\) grows at most polynomially in \(x\) for each \(t>0\). If we drop the requirement that \(u(t,u^{0})\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) for \(t\geq 0\), uniqueness can no longer be guaranteed. In fact, Tychonov constructed a classical solution of the heat equation vanishing for \(t=0\) and growing faster than exponentially in \(x\) (cf. [Hel60, I.4.3] or [DiB95, V.4.1]). The same construction also shows that the backward Cauchy problem

\[\partial_{t}u=\Delta u\ ,\quad t<0\ ,\qquad u(\cdot,0)=u^{0}\]

is not well-posed.

### Bessel Potentials

Since \(-\Delta\) generates the Gauss-Weierstrass semigroup on the classical function spaces we can define the fractional powers \((\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z}\) of \(\rho^{2}-\Delta\) by means of the general theory developed in [Ama95, Subsection III.6]. On the other hand, we can also define \((\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z}\) naturally by means of Fourier transforms. It is reasonable to expect that these two definitions give the same result. That this is indeed the case, at least if \(\operatorname{Re}z\neq 0\), is proven in Proposition 3.3.2. If we assume that \(E\) is a UMD space then we can show that this equality prevails for every \(z\in\mathbb{C}\). More importantly, we show in Theorem 3.3.3 that \(\rho^{2}-\Delta\) has bounded imaginary powers. Lastly, we complement these results by some classical representation theorems.

#### Bessel Kernels

Let \(E:=(E,|\cdot|)\) be a Banach space. In the following we put

\[J_{\rho}^{z}:=\Lambda_{\rho}^{z}(D)\ ,\quad J^{z}:=J_{1}^{z}\ ,\qquad z\in \mathbb{C}\ ,\quad\rho>0\ .\] (3.3.1)

Since \(\Lambda_{\rho}^{z}\in\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) by Example 2.4.7(b), it follows that

\[J_{\rho}^{z}\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\cap \mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\ .\] (3.3.2)

It is also obvious that

\[J_{\rho}^{z_{1}+z_{2}}=J_{\rho}^{z_{1}}J_{\rho}^{z_{2}}\ ,\quad J_{\rho}^{0}= \operatorname{id}\ ,\qquad z_{1},z_{2}\in\mathbb{C}\ ,\quad\rho>0\ ,\] (3.3.3)

so that \(\{\,J_{\rho}^{z}\ ;\ z\in\mathbb{C}\,\}\) is a group of continuous automorphisms of \(\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\) and of \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\), respectively.

Noting that \(\Lambda_{\rho}^{z}=\rho^{z}\sigma_{\rho}\Lambda_{1}^{z}\), it follows from (2.3.9) and (2.3.10) that

\[J_{\rho}^{z}=\rho^{z}\sigma_{\rho}^{-1}J^{z}\sigma_{\rho}\ ,\qquad z\in\mathbb{ C}\ ,\quad\rho>0\ .\] (3.3.4)

---

Observe that \(J_{\rho}^{2}=\rho^{2}-\Delta\). Hence it is natural to put

\[(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z/2}:=J_{\rho}^{z}\ ,\qquad z\in{\mathbb{C}}\ ,\quad\rho>0\ .\] (3.3.5)

Then (3.3.3) implies that \((\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{k}\) are the ordinary powers of

\[\rho^{2}-\Delta\in{\mathcal{L}}\big{(}{\mathcal{S}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\big{)} \cap{\mathcal{L}}\big{(}{\mathcal{S}}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\big{)}\]

for \(k\in{\mathbb{Z}}\). Thus (3.3.5) is a natural way of defining the powers of \(\rho^{2}-\Delta\) for every \(z\in{\mathbb{C}}\).

Now let

\[{\mathfrak{F}}\in\{\,BUC,C_{0},L_{p}\ ;\ 1\leq p<\infty\,\}\] (3.3.6)

and let \(\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}}\) be the \({\mathfrak{F}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\)-realization of \(\Delta\in{\mathcal{L}}\big{(}{\mathcal{S}}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\big{)}\). Then, \(\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}}\) being the infinitesimal generator of the Gauss-Weierstrass semigroup on \({\mathfrak{F}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\), it follows that \(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}}\) is of positive type for \(\rho>0\). Hence the fractional powers \((\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{z}\) are well-defined by [1, Theorem III.4.6.5]. In the following we show that \((\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{z}\) equals the \({\mathfrak{F}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\)-realization \(\big{[}(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z}\big{]}_{\mathfrak{F}}\) of

\[(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z}=J_{\rho}^{2\,z}\in{\mathcal{L}}\big{(}{\mathcal{S}}^{ \prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\big{)}\]

for \(\rho>0\) and \(\operatorname{Re}z\neq 0\). This shows that (3.3.5) is also consistent with the theory of fractional powers developed in [1, Subsection III.4.6], provided \(\operatorname{Re}z\neq 0\). To prove this we need some preparation.

We define the **Bessel potentials** or **Bessel kernels**\(\{\,G_{\rho}^{z}\ ;\ \operatorname{Re}z>0\,\}\) for \(\rho>0\) by

\[G_{\rho}^{z}(x):=\frac{1}{\Gamma(z/2)}\int_{0}^{\infty}t^{z/2}e^{-t\rho^{2}}w_ {t}(x)\,dt/t\ ,\qquad x\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\ .\] (3.3.7)

Note that \(G_{\rho}^{z}(x)>0\) for \(z>0\).

**3.3.1 Lemma**: \(G_{\rho}^{z}={\mathcal{F}}^{-1}\Lambda_{\rho}^{-z}\in L_{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) _with \(\|G_{\rho}^{z}\|_{1}\leq\rho^{-\operatorname{Re}z}\,\big{|}\Gamma(\operatorname {Re}z/2)/\Gamma(z/2)\big{|}\). If \(z>0\) then \(\|G_{\rho}^{z}\|_{1}=\int G_{\rho}^{z}\,dx=\rho^{-z}\)._

**Proof** Observe that, trivially, \(\Lambda_{\rho}^{2}(\xi)\in{\mathcal{G}}({\mathbb{C}};1,-\rho^{2})\) for \((\xi,\rho)\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{\mathbb{R}}\). Thus Theorem III.4.6.6 of [1] implies

\[\Lambda_{\rho}^{-2\,z}(\xi)=\frac{1}{\Gamma(z)}\int_{0}^{\infty}t^{z}e^{-t\rho ^{2}}e^{-t|\xi|^{\,2}}\,dt/t\ ,\qquad(\xi,\rho)\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\times{ \dot{\mathbb{R}}}\ ,\]

for \(\operatorname{Re}z>0\). Since \(\,\Lambda_{\rho}^{-2\,z}\in L_{\infty}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\hookrightarrow{ \mathcal{S}}^{\prime}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\,\) for \(\,\rho>0\,\) and \(\operatorname{Re}z>0\), Fubini's theorem guarantees that, given \(\varphi\in{\mathcal{S}}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\),

\[\langle\Lambda_{\rho}^{-2\,z},\varphi\rangle=\frac{1}{\Gamma(z)}\int_{0}^{ \infty}t^{z}e^{-t\rho^{2}}\big{\langle}e^{-t\,|\cdot|^{2}},\varphi\big{\rangle} \,dt/t\ .\]

---

By replacing \(\varphi\) by \({\cal F}^{-1}\varphi\) and observing Lemma 3.2.1 we infer that

\[\bigl{\langle}{\cal F}^{-1}\Lambda_{\rho}^{-2z},\varphi\bigr{\rangle} =\bigl{\langle}\Lambda_{\rho}^{-2z},{\cal F}^{-1}\varphi\bigr{\rangle}= \frac{1}{\Gamma(z)}\int_{0}^{\infty}t^{z}e^{-t\rho^{2}}\bigl{\langle}e^{-t\,| \cdot|^{2}},{\cal F}^{-1}\varphi\bigr{\rangle}\,dt/t\] \[=\frac{1}{\Gamma(z)}\int_{0}^{\infty}t^{z}e^{-t\rho^{2}}\langle w_ {t},\varphi\rangle\,dt/t=\langle G_{\rho}^{2z},\varphi\rangle\]

for \(\varphi\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\), where in the last step we used Fubini's theorem once more. This proves that \({\cal F}^{-1}\Lambda_{\rho}^{-2z}=G_{\rho}^{2z}\) for \(\mathop{\rm Re}z>0\) and \(\rho>0\).

Since \(w_{t}\in{\cal S}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) for \(t>0\) and \(\int w_{t}\,dx=1\) by Lemma 3.2.1, Fubini's theorem implies

\[\|G_{\rho}^{2z}\|_{1}\leq\frac{1}{\Gamma(z)}\int_{0}^{\infty}t^{\mathop{\rm Re} z}e^{-t\rho^{2}}\,dt/t=\rho^{-2\mathop{\rm Re}z}\]

for \(\mathop{\rm Re}z>0\) and \(\rho>0\), and \(\|G_{\rho}^{z}\|_{1}=\rho^{-z}\) if \(z>0\).

**Powers of \(1-\Delta\)**

**3.3.2 Proposition** _Suppose that \(\rho>0\). Then \(\bigl{[}(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z}\bigr{]}_{\mathfrak{F}}\supset(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{ \mathfrak{F}})^{z}\) and \(\bigl{[}(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z}\bigr{]}_{\mathfrak{F}}=(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{ \mathfrak{F}})^{z}\) for \(\mathop{\rm Re}z\neq 0\)._

**Proof** If \(\mathop{\rm Re}z>0\) then the fact that \(\Lambda_{\rho}^{-2z}\in{\cal O}_{M}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\), Lemma 3.3.1, and Remark 1.9.11(b) imply

\[J_{\rho}^{-2z}u=G_{\rho}^{2z}*u\ ,\qquad u\in\mathfrak{F}:=\mathfrak{F}({ \mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\ .\] (3.3.8)

Thus we infer from \(G_{\rho}^{2z}\in L_{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n})\) and Theorem 1.9.9 that

\[(J_{\rho}^{-2z})_{\mathfrak{F}}=\bigl{[}(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{-z}\bigr{]}_{ \mathfrak{F}}=J_{\rho}^{-2z}\,\bigl{|}\mathfrak{F}\in{\cal L}(\mathfrak{F})\ , \qquad\mathop{\rm Re}z>0\ .\] (3.3.9)

By means of Fubini's theorem we obtain from (3.3.7) and (3.3.8) that

\[J_{\rho}^{-2z}u=\frac{1}{\Gamma(z)}\int_{0}^{\infty}t^{z}e^{-t\rho^{2}}w_{t}*u \,dt/t\ ,\qquad\mathop{\rm Re}z>0\ .\]

Thus, thanks to Theorems 3.2.2 and [Ama95, III.4.6.6],

\[J_{\rho}^{-2z}u=\frac{1}{\Gamma(z)}\int_{0}^{\infty}t^{z-1}e^{-t(\rho^{2}- \Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})}u\,dt=(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{-z}u\ ,\qquad \mathop{\rm Re}z>0\ ,\] (3.3.10)

for \(u\in\mathfrak{F}\). Now it follows from (3.3.9) and (3.3.10) that the assertion is true for \(\mathop{\rm Re}z<0\).

Suppose that \(\mathop{\rm Re}z>0\) and \(u\in\mathop{\rm dom}\bigl{(}(J_{\rho}^{2z})_{\mathfrak{F}}\bigr{)}\). Then \(u\in\mathfrak{F}\) and \(v:=J_{\rho}^{2z}u\in\mathfrak{F}\). Hence

\[(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{-z}v=(J_{\rho}^{-2z})_{\mathfrak{F}}v=J_{ \rho}^{-2z}J_{\rho}^{2z}u=u\ ,\]

---

that is, \(u\in\operatorname{dom}\bigl{(}(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{z}\bigr{)}\) and \((\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{z}u=v=\bigl{[}(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z}\bigr{]}_{ \mathfrak{F}}u.\) Thus

\[(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{z}\supset\bigl{[}(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z}\bigr{]} _{\mathfrak{F}}\ ,\qquad\operatorname{Re}z>0\ .\]

Since \((\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{z}\) is injective and \((J_{\rho}^{2z})_{\mathfrak{F}}\) is surjective, [1, Lemma V.1.4.2] implies that the assertion is true for \(\operatorname{Re}z>0\) as well.

Lastly, suppose that \(\operatorname{Re}z=0.\) Then [1, Theorem III.4.6.5(ii)] and what has already been proven show that

\[\begin{split}(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{z}u& =\frac{\sin\pi z}{\pi z}\int_{0}^{\infty}s^{z}\bigl{(}s+(\rho^{2}- \Delta)_{\mathfrak{F}}\bigr{)}^{-2}(\rho^{2}-\Delta)_{\mathfrak{F}}u\,ds\\ &=\frac{\sin\pi z}{\pi z}\int_{0}^{\infty}s^{z}(s+J_{\rho}^{2})^{ -2}J_{\rho}^{2}u\,ds\end{split}\] (3.3.11)

for \(u\in\operatorname{dom}(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}}).\) Since \(\Lambda_{\rho}^{2}\) and \((s+\Lambda_{\rho}^{2})^{-2}\) belong to \(\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n}),\) we see that

\[(s+J_{\rho}^{2})^{-2}J_{\rho}^{2}=\mathcal{F}^{-1}(s+\Lambda_{\rho}^{2})^{-2} \Lambda_{\rho}^{2}\mathcal{F}\ .\]

Given any \(\varphi\in\mathcal{S}(\mathbb{R}^{n})\) and \(\alpha\in\mathbb{N}^{n},\) it is easily verified that

\[\bigl{\|}\varphi\partial^{\alpha}\bigl{[}(s+\Lambda_{\rho}^{2})^{-2})\Lambda_ {\rho}^{2}\bigr{]}\bigr{\|}_{\infty}\leq c(\alpha,\varphi,\rho)(s+\rho^{2})^{- 2}\ ,\qquad s>0\ .\]

This implies that the integral

\[\int_{0}^{\infty}s^{z}(s+\Lambda_{\rho}^{2})^{-2}\Lambda_{\rho}^{2}\,ds\] (3.3.12)

converges absolutely in \(\mathcal{O}_{M}(\mathbb{R}^{n}).\) Using these facts and the continuity of \(\mathcal{F}^{-1}\) in \(\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E),\) it follows that

\[\begin{split}\int_{0}^{\infty}s^{z}(s+J_{\rho}^{2})^{-2}J_{\rho} ^{2}u\,ds&=\int_{0}^{\infty}\mathcal{F}^{-1}\bigl{(}s^{z}(s+ \Lambda_{\rho}^{2})^{-2}\Lambda_{\rho}^{2}\widehat{u}\bigr{)}\,ds\\ &=\mathcal{F}^{-1}\int_{0}^{\infty}s^{z}(s+\Lambda_{\rho}^{2})^{-2 }\Lambda_{\rho}^{2}\,ds\,\mathcal{F}u=\frac{\pi z}{\sin\pi z}\mathcal{F}^{-1} \Lambda_{\rho}^{2z}\mathcal{F}u\end{split}\]

for \(u\in\mathfrak{F}\hookrightarrow\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E),\) where the last equality follows from Theorem III.4.6.5(ii) of [1] as well. Hence we obtain from (3.3.11) that

\[(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{z}u=\bigl{[}(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z}\bigr{]}_{ \mathfrak{F}}u\ ,\qquad u\in\operatorname{dom}(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})= \operatorname{dom}(\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})\ .\] (3.3.13)

From the definition of \((\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{z}\) in [1, (III.4.6.21)] we know that \(\operatorname{dom}(\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})\) is a core for \((\rho^{2}-\Delta_{\mathfrak{F}})^{z}.\) Thus, since \(\bigl{[}(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z}\bigr{]}_{\mathfrak{F}}\) is closed in \(\mathfrak{F},\) it follows from (3.3.13) that \(\bigl{[}(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{z}\bigr{]}_{\mathfrak{F}}\supset(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{ \mathfrak{F}})^{z}\) for \(\operatorname{Re}z=0.\)

After these preparations we can prove the main result of this subsection.

---

**3.3.3 Theorem** _Let \(E\) be a UMD space and let \(\Delta_{p}\) be the \(L_{p}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\)-realization of \(\Delta\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}\mathcal{S}^{\prime}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\). Then \(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{p}\in\mathcal{BIP}\big{(}L_{p}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\) for \(1<p<\infty\) and \(\rho>0\), and_

\[\|(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{p})^{\,it}\|\leq c(n)(1+|t|^{n+1})\ ,\qquad t\in\mathbb{R}\ , \quad\rho>0\ .\]

ProofWe infer from Example 2.4.7(a) and Remark 2.4.6(a) that

\[\Lambda_{\rho}^{\,it}\in S_{\rho}^{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n}\,)\qquad\text{with}\quad \|\Lambda_{\rho}^{\,it}\|_{S_{\rho}^{0}}\leq c(n)(1+|t|^{n+1})\ ,\qquad t\in \mathbb{R}\ .\]

Hence (2.4.7) and [1, Theorem III.4.4.3] imply

\[J_{\rho}^{\,it}=\big{[}(\rho^{2}-\Delta)^{\,it}\big{]}_{L_{p}(\mathbb{R}^{n}, E)}\in\mathcal{L}\big{(}L_{p}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\big{)}\ ,\qquad t\in\mathbb{R}\ ,\quad\rho>0\ ,\]

with the norm bounded by \(c(n)(1+|t|^{n+1})\). Consequently, by Proposition 3.3.2,

\[\|(\rho^{2}-\Delta_{p})^{\,i}\,t\|_{p}=\|J_{\rho}^{\,it}u\|_{p}\leq c(n)(1+|t|^ {n+1})\,\|u\|_{p}\ ,\qquad\rho>0\ ,\]

for \(u\in\operatorname{dom}(\Delta_{p})\) and \(t\in\mathbb{R}\). Since \(\operatorname{dom}(\Delta_{p})\) is dense in \(L_{p}(\mathbb{R}^{n},E)\), the assertion follows.

**Fourier Transforms of Radial Functions**

We close this subsection by giving another representation of the Bessel potentials, at least for \(\operatorname{Re}z>n\), explaining the name. For this we first prove a general theorem on Fourier transforms.

Recall that the Bessel function \(J_{\nu}\) (of the first kind) of index \(\nu\in\mathbb{C}\backslash(-\lx@overaccentset{{\bullet}}{\mathbb{ N}})\) is defined by

\[J_{\nu}(z)=\Big{(}\frac{z}{2}\Big{)}^{\nu}\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}\frac{(-1)^{n}}{n !\,\Gamma(n+\nu+1)}\Big{(}\frac{z}{2}\Big{)}^{2n}\ ,\qquad z\in\mathbb{C}\ ,\] (3.3.14)

where we use the principal value of the power \(z^{\nu}\). If \(\operatorname{Re}\nu>-1/2\), the integral representation

\[J_{\nu}(z)=\frac{(\frac{z}{2})^{\nu}}{\sqrt{\pi}\Gamma(\nu+\frac{1}{2})}\int_ {0}^{\pi}(\sin\vartheta)^{2\nu}e^{-i\,z\cos\vartheta}\,d\vartheta\ ,\qquad z \in\mathbb{C}\ ,\] (3.3.15)

is valid, and from (3.3.14) it follows easily that

\[J_{-1/2}(z)=\frac{\sqrt{2}\cos z}{\sqrt{\pi z}}\ ,\qquad z\in\mathbb{C}\ ,\] (3.3.16)

(cf. [20, Section 3.3] or [19, Chap. IX]).

---

Now we compute the Fourier transform of a radial function. That is, we assume that

\[f(x)=F(|x|)\ ,\qquad x\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\ ,\]

for some \(F:\ {\mathbb{R}}^{+}\to E\). Fubini's theorem implies that

\[f\in L_{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad F\in L_{1}({ \mathbb{R}}^{+},r^{n-1}\,dr;E)\ .\] (3.3.17)

First suppose that \(n=1\). Then, by Euler's formula for the exponential function and since \(\sin\) is odd,

\[\widehat{f}(\xi)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}e^{-i\,\xi x}F(|x|)\,dx=2\int_{0}^{ \infty}\cos(|\xi|\,r)F(r)\,dr\ ,\qquad\xi\in{\mathbb{R}}\ .\] (3.3.18)

If \(n\geq 2\),

\[\widehat{f}(\xi)=\int_{0}^{\infty}\int_{|x|=r}F(r)e^{-i\,\langle\xi,x\rangle} \,d\sigma(x)\,dr\ ,\qquad\xi\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\ ,\] (3.3.19)

where \(d\sigma\) is the volume measure of \(rS^{n-1}\). If \(\xi\neq 0\), we introduce polar coordinates in the inner integral such that \(\xi/|\xi|\) is the north pole and \(\vartheta\) is the angle between \(x\) and \(\xi\). Then

\[\int_{|x|=r}e^{-i\,\langle\xi,x\rangle}\,d\sigma(x)=r\int_{0}^{\pi}e^{-i\,|\xi| r\cos\vartheta}\,\mathrm{vol}(r\sin\vartheta S^{n-2})\,d\vartheta\ .\]

Since

\[\mathrm{vol}(r\sin\vartheta S^{n-2}) =r^{n-2}(\sin\vartheta)^{n-2}(n-1)\omega_{n-1}\] \[=2r^{n-2}(\sin\vartheta)^{n-2}\pi^{(n-1)/2}\Big{/}\Gamma\Big{(} \frac{n-1}{2}\Big{)}\ ,\]

thanks to (3.2.17), and since

\[\Gamma\Big{(}\frac{n+1}{2}\Big{)}=\frac{n-1}{2}\Gamma\Big{(}\frac{n-1}{2} \Big{)}\ ,\]

it follows that

\[\int_{|x|=r}e^{-i\,\langle\xi,x\rangle}\,d\sigma(x)=\frac{2r^{n-1}\pi^{(n-1)/2 }}{\Gamma(\frac{n-1}{2})}\int_{0}^{\pi}(\sin\vartheta)^{n-2}e^{-i\,|\xi|\,r \cos\vartheta}\,d\vartheta\ .\] (3.3.20)

Now the next theorem is almost obvious.

3.3.4 Theorem: _Suppose that \(f\in L_{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n},E)\) with \(f(x)=F(|x|)\) for \(x\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\). Then_

\[\widehat{f}(\xi)=\frac{(2\pi)^{n/2}}{|\xi|^{(n-2)/2}}\int_{0}^{\infty}F(r)r^{n /2}\,J_{(n-2)/2}(|\xi|\,r)\,dr\ ,\qquad\xi\in{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\ .\]

---

**Proof** This follows from (3.3.15)-(3.3.20).

**3.3.5 Corollary** _Suppose that \(\rho>0\) and \({\rm Re}\,z>n\). Then_

\[G_{\rho}^{z}(x)=(2\pi)^{-n/2}\,|x|^{(2-n)/2}\int_{0}^{\infty}(\rho^{2}+r^{2})^{- z/2}r^{n/2}J_{(n-2)/2}(|x|\,r)\,dr\enspace.\]

**Proof** Since \(\Lambda_{\rho}^{-z}\in L_{1}({\mathbb{R}}^{n}\,)\) for \({\rm Re}\,z>n\), the assertion follows from Lemma 3.3.1, Theorem 3.3.4, and (2.3.2).

---

**Abstract**

We study the \(\beta\)-function of the \(\beta
